This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited
Kenya in May-June, 1976. The mission was composed of G. Ablasser, M. Fireman,
K. Niemann, and P. Santos (Bank) and M. Alipao, R. Cruz and J. Thijsse
(Consultants). A follow-up mission in October-November, 1976 consisted of
G. Ablasser, P. Santos and J. Mullan.

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        In efforts to save foreign exchange by better utilizing domestic
resources, to provide employment opportunities in rural areas and to strive
for a regional balance in the country's development, Government places high
priority on increasing the production of sugar in Kenya. Despite the estab-
lishment of three new sugar factories over the past decade, import require-
ments of sugar have not decreased significantly, averaging 55,000 t annually
for the period 1968-75 and placing an increasing burden on the country's
balance of payments. Without any additional investments in the sugar industry,
Kenya's domestic shortage of sugar would be in the range of 140,000 - 170,000
t by 1985. The Project, which is one of several sugar development projects
either under implementation or in preparation, would assist Kenya in increasing
sugar production to reduce imports and promote small-scale farm development in
a hitherto neglected area of the country.
ii.       The Project would strengthen the Kenya Sugar Authority in an effort
to create an effective body with responsibility for the orderly development
of the sugar industry. It would expand sugarcane production through the
establishment in South Nyanza District of a 2,650 ha nucleus estate and
9,000 ha outgrowers area; the latter would be phased over a 7-year period.
A sugar factory with a crushing capacity of 2,000 t of cane per day (TCD),
expandable to 3,000 TCD with ancilliary facilities, and staff housing would be
erected near Awendo, the geographic center of the proposed Project area.
Expansion to 3,000 TCD would require the development of a total of 15,900 ha
of outgrowers area over a period of 11 years. The Project also would provide
for the improvement of existing and the construction of new roads and tracks,
and for vehicles and equipment to transport the sugarcane to the factory.
Staff training and sugarcane research would be organized to benefit the sugar
industry in general and the new sugar company in particular.
iii.      The Kenya Sugar Authority, to be expanded under the supervision
of the Ministry of Agriculture, would assume an increasing role in the plan-
ning of sugar industry development and in providing advice to Government,
the sugar companies and to sugarcane growers. The agricultural and factory
development in South Nyanza would be the responsibility of the board of
directors of the South Nyanza Sugar Company; management would be provided by a
Managing Agent under contract with the Company. The Company also would be
responsible for the construction of staff houses, and of access roads and
tracks. The construction of major roads would be the responsibility of the
Ministry of Works. The research and training program to meet Company needs
would be executed by Company staff; the national research effort is the
responsibility of the National Sugar Research Institute, and industry manpower
training would be coordinated by the Kenya Sugar Authority.
iv.       The organizational structure proposed for the Company is designed to
maximize productive efficiency and to foster a harmonious relationship between
the Company and outgrowers. The Company's Agricultural Department would be
responsible for the outgrowers' development program, in addition to the devel-
opment of its own nucleus estate, through planning, selection of participating



ii -
farmers and farmers' land with adequate soils, and provision of all necessary
inputs, services and extension advice. Farmers, who would be under contract
with the Company, would adhere to Company regulations throughout the growing
cycle of the cane and pay for the inputs and services provided by the Company
at the time of the harvest and delivery of the cane to the factory. There
would be no subsidy granted farmers by the Company or the Government.
v.        The Project would cost US$88.5 million.  With interest during
construction it would cost US$105.3 million, or US$95.3 million excluding
taxes and duties related to implementation or operation of Project facilities;
the foreign exchange component would be US$70.7 million. Of total Project
cost net of taxes and duties, plus interest during construction, about 25%
would be financed through the Government of Kenya, one percent would be
contributed by the Managing Agent of the sugar company, 65% would be financed
through a total of seven external loans, and nine percent would be covered by
the Company's self-generated funds. Overall total external financing, amounting
to US$61.7 million, would cover 72% of total foreign exchange costs and 32% of
local costs. Co-financing would be on a parallel basis, except roads would be
financed jointly with the African Development Bank. The supply and erection
of the sugar factory would be under a turnkey contract. A possible expansion
from 60,000 t to 90,000 t annual capacity, provided for in the Project's
design, would cost an additional US$11.2 million and require short-term loan
financing of US$2.7 million.
vi.       The World Bank would finance the sugar company's agricultural
development, research activities, management, administrative overhead, and
interest during construction on the World Bank loan; Government's road construc-
tion program and additional support for the National Sugar Research Institute;
and the strengthening of the Kenya Sugar Authority, and the Authority's
overseas training program and specific studies. The procurement under the
World Bank loan of vehicles, equipment, office machines and furniture (US$3.5
million), and civil works contracts for housing and social amenities (US$7.4
million) and roads under the responsibility of the Ministry of Works (US$5.5
million) would be by international conmpetitive bidding, except for items
costing less than US$100,000 and civil works contracts costing less than
US$200,000 which would be procured in accordance with local procedures. Local
manufacturers would be allowed a preference margin of 15% or the existing rate
of duty, whichever is lower, and prequalified local construction firms would
be given a 7-1/2% preference margin in the evaluation of their bids. Incre-
mental fertilizer requirements and fuels and lubricants (US$2.2 million) would
be procured in accordance with local procedures, and the construction of minor
access roads and tracks (US$3.6 million) would be by the Company's Road
Construction and Maintenance Unit and local contractors selected under local
procurement procedures. An overseas training component (US$0.3 million) and
special studies (US$0.3 million) would be the responsibility of the Kenya
Sugar Authority. Architects for the housing component, and the road engineer-
ing consultants would be appointed under terms and conditions satisfactory to
the Bank. For all contracts expected to cost in excess of US$300,000 draft
tender documents would be submitted to the Bank for approval, and bid analysis
and recommendation for award would be submitted for comment.



- iii -
vii.      Project incremental sugar production would be about 25,000 t in
Year 4 and 60,000 t annually at full development in Year 9 without expansion;
with factory expansion from 2,000 TCD to 3,000 TCD, production would reach
90,000 t in Year 12. Valued at projected import parity and expressed in
constant (March) 1977 prices, 90,000 t of sugar would be worth KShs. 367
million (US$44 million) and, thus, represent significant foreign exchange
savings. With the foreign exchange portion of continuing expenditures,
excluding debt service, estimated to amount to KShs. 80 million (US$10 mil-
lion), net annual foreign exchange savings at full development would be
about KShs. 287 million (US$34 million); including debt service, net foreign
exchange savings would amount to KShs. 210 million (US$25 million) in Year
12 and continue to increase thereafter. The Project would provide permanent
employment for about 1,800 persons, and casual employment as cane harvest
labor for 2,200 persons. The incomes of about 7,900 participating outgrower
families would be increased by an average of KShs. 3,055 (US$368) annually
or about 180% above current levels.
viii.     The economic rate of return of the Project is estimated to be
25%. The major risks facing the Project include more frequent or\severe
drought damage to sugarcane than has been assumed in the evaluation, disrup-
tions in cane supply to the factory related to unforeseen difficulties with
outgrowers' production of sugarcane, and marketing problems in conjunction
with other sugar projects Government intends to implement if world market
prices of sugar remained below the cost of production in Kenya and the com-
bined production exceeded self-sufficiency levels. However, the sensitivity
analysis indicates that even with the adverse assumption that output were
20% below expectation the economic rate of return would still be 20%; with
the projected export parity prices, and if export of total Project output
were assumed, the rate of return would be 19%.
ix.       Seventeen World Bank Loans and 11 IDA Credits, totaling nearly
US$131 million have been approved so far to assist in the financing of 13
projects in Kenya's agriculture sector. These include projects concerned
with livestock development, afforestation, tea development, settlement,
smallholder credit, rehabilitation of group farms, and integrated agricul-
tural development. Implementation of these projects has generally been
satisfactory, except of livestock projects where it has been slow.
x.        The Project is suitable for a World Bank Loan of US$25 million for
20 years, including a grace period of 4.5 years.






KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      Kenya has been a net importer of sugar for decades even though
the feasibility of producing sugar domestically was recognized since the
establishment of the first sugar factory in the 1920s. Since independence in
1963, Government has actively supported the expansion of the domestic sugar
industry and established three new factories in addition to the two private
factories existing prior to independence. Despite these efforts, however,
sugar imports remained high at an annual average of 55,000 t since 1968 and
the cost of such imports continued to increase. To counteract this trend,
rapid further development of the industry was foreseen in the 1974-78 Agri-
cultural Development Program. A UNDP-financed study was carried out, with the
Bank as Executing Agency, to provide a better basis for the planning of this
development 1/ and a number of projects have been identified.
1.02      This Project is one of Government's four high-priority sugar projects.
It is in line with Government's objectives to stimulate development in economic-
ally backward areas and to involve smallholder farmers in the development
process.  The other three projects concern the rehabilitation of the three
Nyanza Sugar Belt factories (Miwani, Chemelil and Muhoroni) and Ramisi at the
Coast, the establishment of a new factory at Nzoia with French Government as-
sistance, and the expansion to about double its present capacity of the Mumias
plant with assistance from the United Kingdom. Depending on Government's
price policy and the phasing of these projects, the resulting increase in
production either would result in some surpluses of sugar or more likely,
would make Kenya self-sufficient.
1.03      Since 1960, the Bank Group has extended 17 loans and 11 IDA credits
for 13 projects totaling nearly US$131 million to assist Kenya's agriculture.
Two projects each concerned livestock development and afforestation for pulp
and saw wood production, four supported the development of smallholder tea,
tea roads and tea factories, three were for settlement and smallholder credit,
one assisted the rehabilitation of group farms, and the most recent (1976) was
the Integrated Agricultural Development Project. In general, projects have
been well executed although there have been substantial delays affecting the
livestock projects. The nature of these projects differed substantially from
that of the present project; risks and deficiencies previously encountered in
smallholder production (farm planning, credit and service delivery systems,
etc.) would apply only to a very limited extent to the present project as
the proposed organization has been designed specifically to avoid these (para.
5.08). The Bank has also supported agricultural development in Kenya through
continuous agricultural policy discussions. A Bank agricultural sector survey
was completed in 1973.
1.04      The Project was prepared for Government by the Mehta Group, Ltd., a
private firm which has been subsequently appointed managing agent for the
1/   Tate and Lyle Technical Services, Ltd., Kenya Sugar Industry Expansion
Study, Vol. I - IV, September, 1975, Vol. V - VI, May, 1976.

Project. This report is based on the findings of a Bank mission which
visited Kenya in May-June 1976 consisting of Messrs. G. Ablasser, M. Fireman,
K. Niemann, and P. Santos (Bank) and M. Alipao, R. Cruz and J. Thijsse (Con-
sultants). Messrs. K.G.V. Krishna and B. Mutharika (Bank) and R. Adams and R.
Vermeersch (European Investment Bank) joined the mission part-time in the
field. A follow-up mission in October-November, 1976 consisted of Messrs. G.
Ablasser, P. Santos and J. Mullan, and Mr. K.G.V. Krishna (part-time).
II . BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      Lying astride the eq ator on the eastern coast of Africa, Kenya
has a total area of 583,000 km  with wide variations in climatic and ecolo-
gical conditions. While more than one-half of the total area is semi-desert,
the remaining land, varying in conditions from tropical to temperate, in-
cludes some of the most fertile soils on the African continent. The climate,
modified by altitude ranging from sea level to over 5,000 meters, is prin-
cipally differentiated in terms of rainfall regimes which vary from under
250 mm per year in the northern deserts to 1,900 mm per year in the western
highlands near Lake Victoria. The annual mean maximum temperature exceeds
26 C over 75% of the country, while the corresponding mean minimum tempera-
ture is about 14 C.
2.02      Kenya's population is estimated at 13.7 million (mid 1976) and is
growing at a rate of about 3.5% annually. It is estimated to double in
about 20 years. Approximately 20% of the population is under four years
of age, and 57% is under age 20. About 90% of the population is classified
as rural and is primarily dependent on agriculture for its livelihood. There
are extreme geographic variations in population density.
2.03      Kenya's GDP at factor cost in 1975 was about KShs. 20.8 billion,
or KShs. 1,573 (US$210) per capita. GDP growth averaged about 6.2% annually
in real terms between 1964 and 1974, but slowed down to 0.7% in 1975 following
accelerated world inflation, including oil price increases. GDP outside the
monetary economy in agriculture, forestry and fishing (subsistence) is estima-
ted to have increased 3.6% annually on average during the 1964-74 period,
while commercial agriculture grew by 5.3% annually. In 1975, subsistence
agriculture expanded by 0.4%, while commercial agriculture declined by 1%.
Kenya's imports of KShs. 6.9 billion in 1975 exceeded exports by KShs. 2.7
billion. Consumer prices rose about 19% in 1975, compared with 16% during
the preceding year.



B. The Agricultural Sector (Annex 1)
2.04      In 1975, the agricultural sector contributed KShs. 6.2 billion,
or about _0% to the country's GDP; KShs. 2.6 billion represented the GDP
of commercial agriculture. In comparison, the total agricultural sector
share in GDP was 39% in 1964. Currently, agriculture accounts for about 70%
of total exports; the latter increased from KShs. 2.0 billion to KShs. 4.2
billion in the period 1965-75. The main agricultural exports are coffee,
tea, sisal, pyrethrum, meat, dairy products, hides and skins, cotton, wool,
nuts and animal feed. The main subsistence foodcrops are maize, sorghum,
millet and pulses. The main cash crops are coffee, tea, maize, sugarcane,
wheat and sisal.
2.05      About 20% of Kenya's total land area of 58 million ha is of
high or medium potential for crops or livestock, including the highlands
and the coastal belt. A further 10% is suitable for subsistence dryland
farming and cattle ranching, while the rest is suitable only for extensive
production of livestock for both commercial and subsistence needs. Irriga-
tion development in the past had been confined to minor schemes but, more
recently, Government decided to accelerate the development of the country's
irrigation potential; about 150,000 ha have been identified as suitable for new
large-scale irrigation.
C. Rural Development Strategy
2.06      Government's development objectives for agriculture are to
(a) increase both the output and productivity of export commodities so
as to increase foreign exchange earnings and maintain their competitive
position in export markets; (b) diversify output to spread the risks of
price fluctuations; (c) increase production of food crops to meet growing
demand resulting from population growth; and (d) develop physical and
social infrastructure in integrated programs for both cash and food crops,
raise incomes and living standards in the poorer parts of the country
and reduce rural-urban disparities as well as income disparities and the
population-land imbalance among regions.
2.07      To attain these objectives, Government strategy consists of
(a) implementing programs centering on the smallholder sector; (b) providing
support to and integrating parastatals, cooperatives and private entrepreneurs
to contribute more to smallholder development; (c) taking measures to reduce
the population-land imbalance and the associated under-use of land and labor;
(d) promoting food crop production; (e) executing large-scale diversification
projects; and (f) developing the country's irrigation potential.



- 4 -
D. The Sugar Subsector
2.08      History (Annex 2).  The commercial production of sugar in Kenya
began with the establishment in the 1920s of the privately-owned factories
at Ramisi near the Coast and Miwani in the Nyanza Sugar Belt. After
World War II, the combined production of Ramisi and Miwani fell increasingly
short of meeting domestic demand, and the cost of importing sugar escalated
with rising demand resulting from population and income growth. After in-
dependence, Government decided to expand sugar production to attain eventual
self-sufficiency and, at the same time, increase incomes of small farmers.
New factories, majority-owned by Government, were established in Muhoroni
(1966) and Chemelil (1968) in the Sugar Belt of Nyanza Province and in
Mumias (1973) in Western Province. All factories produce "mill-white" sugar
which is yellowish-brown in color. In terms of value of its output and
contribution to wage employment, the sugar industry is of great importance to
the economy.
2.09      Institutions (Annex 3).  The Ministry of Agriculture has overall
responsibility for sugar industry development.  Through its representation
on the Boards of Muhoroni, Chemelil and Mumias, the Ministry directs the
operations of a major part of the industry. Its extension service is respon-
sible for providing advice to farmers, although very little advice is given on
the growing of sugarcane. The Ministry's National Sugar Research Station was
set up in 1971 at Kibos in the Sugar Belt; the results to date are poor,
however, because of inadequate guidance and lack of funds.
2.10      In 1972, the Kenya Sugar Authority (KSA) was created under the
Ministry of Agriculture to promote the development of the sugar industry
in general and give advice to Government. Its members represent Government,
the sugar companies and farmers; a Chief Executive Officer is in charge of
conducting current business. While the basic objectives of KSA are general-
ly sound and its legal basis is fairly broad, KSA has not been able to assume
its intended role due to reluctance on the part of some major Government
ministries to relinquish some of their responsibilities and control in the
sugar industry. Among the Authority's accomplishments are the zoning of
outgrowers areas and a study through an ad hoc committee of transport problems
in the Sugar Belt, and advising Government on pricing policy. In the future,
the development of the sugar industry would benefit from an increased role of
KSA (para 5.01).
2.11      The Ministry of Finance and Planning plays an important role in the
sugar subsector through its national planning function, budgeting powers and
influence on pricing policy; its foreign exchange and taxing policies to a
large extent determine the conditions for investment in the sector. The
IMinistry of Commerce and Industry determines import requirements of sugar for
household consumption and makes purchases abroad. It allocates sugar locally
on a monthly basis and has responsibility for domestic distribution through
the Kenya National Trading Corporation (KNTC) (para. 2.23). The Ministry also
participates in the formulation of price policy.



2.12      The Ministry of Lands and Settlement has been closely connected
with the development of sugarcane production through its Sugar Settlement
Scheme at Muhoroni. The Ministry's Lands Officer is responsible for ad-
judication surveys, registration of land rights and the acquisition of land
for Government use. The Settlement Office is primarily engaged in settling
landless farmers on new small-scale holdings established through reallocation
of former large-scale entities.
2.13      The Ministry of Cooperatives and Social Services is responsible
for administering a range of programs concerning community development,
vocational rehabilitation, adult education and training, and cooperative
development. It has occasionally served as a channel for development funds
as, for example, in the Sugar Belt for the purpose of rehabilitating and
expanding the area under sugarcane. The Cooperative movement has played a
prominent role in Kenya's sugar industry through the Sugar Belt Cooperative
Union (SBCU) and its 35 member societies. Over the past several years, SBCU
has served as the main vehicle for improving smallholder production of sugar-
cane in the Sugar Belt. A German organization, the Friedrich-Ebert-Stiftung,
has been providing technical assistance to SBCU for several years and current-
ly is funding approximately one-half of SBCU's annual budget. Despite an
enthusiastic staff, SBCU's success to date in raising farm production has been
limited, largely because of its broad integrated approach covering a much
wider sphere than production thus spreading management and staff rescurces too
thinly; its statutory reliance on voluntary cooperation by farmers and,
consequently, lack of effective control; and its susceptibility to political
intervention. The problem of stagnant farm incomes, which has reduced SBCU's
credibility with farmers, though partly due to SBCU's management shortcomings,
is mainly the result of past planning and operating mistakes by other organi-
zations, and the relatively low cane prices. With some staff assistance from
the Ministry of Agriculture (para. 2.19) SBCU has been implementing a Govern-
ment program to alleviate the Sugar Belt factories' cane shortage. Coopera-
tives are not involved at Mumias and are not expected by Government to play a
major role in any new projects because it considers a direct relationship
between the sugar companies and farmers to be more conducive to the efficient
production of sugarcane.
2.14      Kenya's banking institutions have been a major source of financing
for the sugar factories but have been less important in assisting sugarcane
growers. The Development Finance Company of Kenya, Ltd., (DFCK) and the
Agricultural Development Corporation (ADC) have either loan or equity capital
invested in Muhoroni, Chemelil and Mumias and are expected to participate in
the financing of new projects. The Agriculture Finance Corporation (AFC) is
providing production loans to sugarcane farmers but has been inclined to give
preference to medium or larger farmers over smallholders because of the lower
cost of loan administration. The Cooperative Bank of Kenya, Ltd., through
its Cooperative Production Credit Scheme, has been providing credit and
saving facilities to members of cooperative societies; Sugar Belt farmers
have benefitted from this scheme.
2.15      Performance.  The technical and financial performance of Kenya's
sugar industry, with the exception of Mumias, has been poor. The private



-6-
Ramisi and Miwani companies have shied away from long-term reinvestment in
plant and equipment while aiming at profitable current account operations.
This has resulted in the old factories being in relatively poor repair and in
neglect by those companies of their outgrower farmers. Both Muhoroni and
Chemelil had major start-up problems, leading to huge losses at Chemelil and a
slightly better than break-even situation at Muhoroni. In contrast, the
Mumias company has been able to realize its planned development schedule and
to generate profits.
2.16      The major immediate cause of the poor company performance has
been insufficient cane supply and, consequently, underutilization of plant
capacity. In 1975, actual utilization of rated capacity ranged from 28%
at Ramisi to 62% at Chemelil; Mumias, approaching its first expansion phase,
had already exceeded its rated capacity. Overall, the industry performed
about 40% below capacity at a time when the country was faced with mounting
requirements of expensive sugar imports. Smallholder outgrowers' cane yields
in the Sugar Belt are low, averaging around 38 t/ha/year in the Miwani zone,
and somewhat higher at the Muhoroni end of the Belt. Nucleus estate yields
are 10-20% above smallholder yields. The yield problem has been compounded by
the organizational difficulties in harvesting and transporting (para. 2.20)
the cane. Considerably better yields are being achieved on some of the
private large farms. Average outgrower yields at Mumias reportedly exceeded
50 t/ha/year in 1975 and are expected to rise. The planned rehabilitation
project, for which Government has requested financial assistance from the
World Bank, is envisaged to remove the major constraints to better performance
of the existing sugar companies, except Mumias. At loan negotiations for the
South Nyanza project Government outlined its plans for the organization of
estate and outgrowers development in the Nyanza Sugar Belt, which will enable
the Bank to proceed with the appraisal of the rehabilitation project; an
interministerial committee has been established to assist the appraisal
mission.
2.17      Structure and Organization (Annex 3).  All factories are supplied
with cane from estates and outgrowers; the outgrowers' share ranges from
25% at Ramisi to 80% in Chemelil and Muhoroni. All factories own a nucleus
estate, except Muhoroni which is, however, in the process of acquiring the
privately-owned Nyando estate. The two private factories are managed by the
owners while Government has entrusted management of its majority-owned fac-
tories to agents; Bookers Agricultural Services of the United Kingdom are in
charge of Chemelil and Mumias, and The Mehta Group, Ltd. are managing Muhoroni.
Government's equity share in the companies ranges from 84% in Muhoroni to
almost 100% in Mumias and Chemelil.
2.18      There exists great diversity among companies in the organization of
agricultural production and the interrelationship between factory management
and cane producers. The effects of this diversity are closely correlated
with company performance, with the poor performance of all companies but
Mumias being primarily the result of insufficient cane supply (para. 2.16).
While low yields at the nucleus estates are caused largely by technical
and financial factors, the difficulties related to smallholder outgrowers



-7-
are mostly organizational and, in part, related to sugarcane prices. At
Muhoroni, most outgrowers are organized into a settlement scheme; services
are provided through the Sugar Settlement Organization and involve private
contractors for land preparation and cane transport. At Chemelil, the com-
pany's agricultural department serves mainly its own nucleus estate, but its
transport fleet is increasingly moving cane from private growers in addition
to cane from its estate. The supply and organization of services to small-
holders is mainly the responsibility of SBCU. The Union owns some equip-
ment but provides most technical services on a contract basis through private
operators. SBCU also serves some farmers at Muhoroni and the majority of out-
growers at Miwani. The Miwani company's agricultural department is providing
limited assistance to its outgrowers. At Mumias, the organization and pro-
vision of services to outgrowers has been the responsibility of the company's
agricultural department. However, an Outgrowers Company is now being formed,
leading to a gradual separation of the outgrowers responsibility from the
company. Virtually no formal organization is in charge of assisting outgrowers
at Ramisi.
2.19      Outgrowers' performance problems have been caused mainly by
inadequate extension advice, poor organization of cane harvesting, and
transport difficulties. These factors, coupled with low cane prices
(para. 2.25) resulted in low incomes to farmers and, consequently, little
interest in improving output. The extension service of the Ministry of
Agriculture is multi-purpose, serving all crops, but has made only minimal
input to the production of sugarcane. After SBCU had assumed the major
service functions to outgrowers, including extension, some of the Ministry
extension staff was made available to SBCU. This arrangement is not very
satisfactory because of low staff morale and dissatisfaction within SBCU with
the quality of the staff. SBCU's own extension staff also is inadequate. At
Muhoroni, the Settlement Organization provides virtually no extension service.
Recognizing these deficiencies, the extension service at Mumias was organized
within the company's agricultural department which enforces strict criteria
for the selection of growers; assists in land preparation; provides inputs,
advice and current financing; and exercises tight control over all outgrowers'
operations related to cane production. Mumias' results to date are encouraging.
2.20      Because of lack of coordination between the factories and out-
growers in the Sugar Belt, the harvesting of cane has at times been very
unsatisfactory. Without proper control, farmers tend to bunch harvesting
in months with low labor requirements for other crops, sometimes forcing
acceptance of their cane by the factory through the burning of cane, claim-
ing emergency delivery privileges. The resulting artificial seasonality
in supply has contributed to low factory performance. Efforts are now being
made to establish appropriate harvesting schedules and coordinate these
between the companies and outgrowers organizations and among the companies
themselves. Harvesting in the Sugar Belt has also been hampered by a lack
of transport facilities for cane. In the absence of a carefully planned
master harvesting schedule, it has been difficult to effectively communicate
with transporters and to direct vehicles to destinations where needed as
the latter tended to be widely scattered. Private transporters also either
are reluctant to service outlying areas, or provide service to such areas
only at very high cost. With SBCU having acquired some transport equipment



- 8 -
and Chemelil expanding its fleet, the transport constraint has been eased
recently. All of the transport at Mumias is done by company-owned vehicles
and closely coordinated with the harvesting activities.
2.21      The organization of other services and activities, such as the
disposal of by-products, procurement of agricultural or factory inputs,
and manpower development is fragmented and often unsatisfactory. There is
a continuous problem with the transport of molasses for export, and the
provision of suitable sugar bags is requiring excessive management attention.
2.22      Supply and Demand Situation (Annex 4).  Kenya's sugar imports
averaged 59,000 t/year for the period 1955-67, and 55,000 t/year for 1968-75.
Because of the high world market prices in 1975, domestic prices were raised
sharply in Kenya, reducing import requirements for the year to about 33,000 t.
A further sharp drop was expected for 1976. Total consumption fell from
225,000 t in 1974 to 194,000 t in 1975, while domestic production declined
from 164,000 t to 161,000 t, respectively. On a per capita basis, consump-
tion decreased from a record 17.6 kg per year in 1974 to 14.6 kg in 1975;
preliminary indications are that the 1976 consumption was below 13.0 kg.
Without further investments, the domestic deficit in sugar would increase
sharply over the next few years (para 6.04).
2.23      Marketing and Pricing of Sugar (Annex 4).  The Kenya National
Trading Corporation (KNTC) under the Ministry of Commerce and Industry is the
sole national distributor of sugar. Upon an allocation order by the Ministry,
the sugar companies dispatch sugar to the 18 KNTC depots across the country.
Sugar imported by the Ministry is channeled through Kenya Farmers Association
(KFA) warehouses in Mombasa and dispatched by KFA. Payments to the factories,
including excise tax, and for imports, commissions and transportation, are
made out of the national sugar account by the Chief Accountant in the Office
of the President. KNTC, which performs primarily a warehousing wholesalers
function, sells to about 350 authorized private wholesalers throughout the
country for cash. The sales proceeds are deposited in local subaccounts of
the national sugar account. For this service, KNTC is paid a fixed commission.
Wholesalers sell to retailers on a private customer basis.
2.24      While this distribution system has been operational for a number
of years and has demonstrated that it can perform satisfactorily, difficul-
ties are occasionally encountered. These are due to a variety of causes
of which poor allocation of sugar by the Ministry of Commerce and Industry
and transport difficulties are the most prominent. Among the more serious
consequences of marketing deficiencies have been (a) local shortages and
consequent consumer complaints when less sugar arrived locally than needed,
and (b) congestions at factory warehouses when less sugar was moved than
anticipated. Government would prepare a plan, satisfactory to the Bank, for
the improvement of the sugar distribution system and of sugar marketing and
submit it together with an indicative timetable for implementation to the Bank
before December 31, 1978. This plan would, as a minimum, cover the improvement
of sugar market research and analysis, the domestic sugar allocation system,
the organization of sugar transport, storage requirements, and the accounting
procedures relating to the national sugar account. An assurance to this
effect was obtained during negotiations. Funds from KSA's budget could be
used for preparing the plan (para. 4.16).



- 9 -
2.25      All prices and margins for sugar are fixed by Government upon
recommendation by KSA. The price of sugarcane, delivered to the factory
was increased from KShs. 52/t in 1966 to KShs. 92/t in 1975 and KShs. 105/t
in 1976. Because of transportation and other charges being deducted, the
net price to farmers is generally much lower than the factory gate price,
however. In order to encourage farmers to increase the use of modern pro-
duction inputs and adopt better husbandry practices, particularly timely
replanting, cane prices need to be increased. The ex-mill price of sugar
increased from KShs. 919/t in 1966 to KShs. 1,860/t in 1975 and KShs. 2,300/t
in 1976; the respective retail price was KShs. 1,550, KShs. 3,500 and KShs.
4,500 per ton. The increased margin between the ex-mill price and the retail
price is accounted for largely by increases in the import equalization levy
which was KShs. 1,362/t in 1976 but only KShs. 100/t in 1966; this levy is
intended to balance the national sugar account in the event the cost of
imported sugar exceeds the cost of domestically produced sugar. With gradual
import substitution through domestic sugar development a change in the
price structure will be required (paras. 6.06-6.08).
III. THE PROJECT AREA
General (Annex 5)
3.01      The Project area is situated just south of the equator within
the administrative area of South Nyanza District in Nyanza Province; a
small area located in neighboring Kisii District to the east may be included
if necessary. South Nyanza District borders on Tanzania in the south and on
Lake Victoria in the west. The only major township in the Project area is
Awendo, located approximately 35 km from Homa Bay, the District capital. A
newly improved 200-bed hospital is located just outside the Project area at
Migori, a primary school is situated near Awendo at Maringa, and the District's
Farmers Training Center is located in Homa Bay.
3.02      Access is by means of the tarmac A-1 national highway which runs
from Kisumu, the Provincial capital some 160 km to the north, to Tanzania
in the south. Minor lake traffic links Kisumu with Homa Bay, which is
connected to the main highway by an all-weather gravel road. All other
roads in the Project area are access roads and tracks which become im-
passable to most motor vehicles even during short rains. The nearest
railhead is at Kisumu. The distance from Kisumu to Nairobi is 310 km, and
Kisumu to Mombasa 800 km.
3.03      The total population of the Project area is approximately 95,000
people, with an average f~mily size of about seven persons. The population
density2is 123 persons/km , slightly above the Dist5ict average of 116 per-
sons/km , but considerably below the 306 persons/km in the neighboring
Kisii District. Of the total of about 13,600 families in the area, 500-600
would be displaced by the Project's nucleus estate (para. 4.05). The
inhabitants are almost exclusively Luos whose predominant occupation used
to be fishing in the Nile Valley and who migrated to the area several genera-
tions ago; they have adapted well to agricultural and pastoral life.



- 10 -
Land Tenure and Type of Farming (Annex 5)
3.04      Because of the area's relatively recent settlement, the average
farm size is relatively large at about six hectares. Practically all land
is owned in freehold. Adjudication and registration of land by the Ministry
of Lands and Settlement is virtually completed in the area designated for
the nucleus estate, but only about one-half finished in the outgrowers'
area; completion is expected in 1977. The natural vegetation consists of
grass and scattered trees and strips of riverine forest. Because of the
relatively large holdings, the high fertility of the soil and virtual absence
of commercial agricultural development, only about 20% of the land is under
cultivation at any given time and an additional 20% is under fallow. Most
crops grown are for subsistence purposes and include maize, millet, cassava,
sweet potatoes, beans and groundnuts. Sugarcane grown for local jaggeries
is the main cash crop. The poorly drained areas are mostly used for cattle
grazing.
Climate (Annex 5)
3.05      Rainfall increases from the southwestern to the northeastern part
of the Project area, averaging about 1,150 mm and 1,350 mm per year, respec-
tively. There is considerable inter-year and intra-year variation, however.
At Uriri, at the center of the Project area, annual rainfall over the past
18 years for which complete records are available averaged 1,350 mm and
ranged from 1,012 mm to 1,728 mm. It is estimated that there is a severe
dry spell of three months or more at least one year in five. The estimated
depressing effect of the drought on cane yields amounts to maximally 35% per
crop, or about 10% per year over a 5-year cycle with three crops (para. 6.01).
Temperature, though relatively stable throughout the year, is on the low side
for cane and is the main reason for the relatively long growing period of
20-22 months for the plant crop. However, cool temperatures also result in a
high sugar content in the cane juice and slow the decrease in sugar content in
case of delayed harvesting and processing after maturity.
Topography and Soils (Annex 6)
3.06      Approximately 15-20% of the Project area of some 77,000 ha has
a slope of less than one percent, with the remainder being moderately to
highly undulating. The area with an elevation of 1,400 - 1,500 m above
sea level slopes toward the northwest and is traversed by a number of
small to modera te-sized streams, including the Sare River with a minimum
flow of 0.11 m /sec. The geology is moderately complex, consisting of
mixtures of both acidic and basic pre-Cambrian primary and secondary rocks.
Differences in parent material and local topography have produced a dis-
tinguishable sequence of soils. Reddish or brownish, well drained soils of
variable depth dominate on the ridge crests and upper slopes; browner, finer,
deeper and less well drained soils are primarily found on the slopes; and in
the valley bottoms the soils are deep, dark brown to black, very fine textured
and poorly drained. The soils are unusually high in clay content and in
organic matter for tropical soils.



- 11 -
3.07      The poorly drained soils in the valley bottoms and some of the
higher elevated plains are unsuitable for cane production without drainage.
In addition, some shallow soils are marginally suitable and some are a
priori unsuitable on account of steep slopes or for other reasons. A
gross area of 3,410 ha, located within a 16 km radius from the sugar
factory site, has been designated as nucleus estate area, of which about
2,820 ha have been judged suitable for cane production (Map IBRD 12431).
Of the suitable nucleus estate area, 29% is suitable without reservation,
30% is slightly shallow and 41% needs drainage. Of the remaining part of
the Project area, or about 74,000 ha, which would be the potential outgrowers
area, 35% is suitable without reservation and 16% is relatively shallow but
considered suitable; 49% is unsuitable. Relatively shallow land, when culti-
vated with sugarcane by outgrower farmers, would produce economic yields in
years with rainfalls normal for the area, but could pose critical risks in
drought years. Only 25% of the Project area would eventually be under sugar-
cane; development of the arable portion of the remaining 75% would be part
of Government's overall development effort in the District.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
4.01      The Project would over five years (1977-1981) establish a new sugar
complex in South Nyanza District, including a factory, a nucleus estate,
outgrowers' cane production and housing and transportation infrastructure. It
would also assist in the strengthening of the Kenya Sugar Authority. The
Project would increase the annual production of mill-white sugar by about
60,000 tons, which could later be expanded to about 90,000 tons. A Project
implementation schedule is attached as Chart World Bank-16499.
4.02      Specifically the Project would comprise:
(a)  Agricultural development, including (i) vehicles, machinery
and equipment, and staffing and operating costs for the
Agricultural Department of the new South Nyanza Sugar
Company; (ii) development of a 3,410 ha nucleus estate
(2,650 ha net area of cane), including the acquisition of
the land; (iii) development of 7,050 ha (9,000 ha after
6 years) of outgrowers' land for the production of
sugarcane; and (iv) drainage for nucleus estate land.
(b)  A sugar factory, including the supply, erection and commis-
sioning of a 2,000 TCD sugar factory, expandable to 3,000
TCD, complete with ancillaries under a turnkey contract;
vehicles and equipment; and operating costs.
(c) General Management and Administration, consisting of
(i) vehicles and equipment, and general Company overhead;



- 12 -
and administrative support staff and other operating
costs; and (ii) provision of management services for the
South Nyanza Sugar Company under a separate agreement
with an international firm.
(d) Infrastructure, including (i) construction and maintenance
of roads and tracks in the nucleus estate and outgrowers'
areas; and (ii) construction of staff houses and ancillary
facilities, and provision of social amenities including
health, nursing, canteen, shopping and recreational facilities.
(e) Research, consisting of provisions to carry out soils and
agronomic research in the Project area, and to strengthen
the National Sugar Research Institute.
(f) Training, including local on-the-job and formal training
for agricultural, factory and administrative personnel,
and overseas training to meet current and future industry
manpower needs.
(g) The Kenya Sugar Authority, providing for an expanded
role of KSA and its strengthening through additional
staff, vehicles and equipment, and for studies to be
undertaken by KSA.
4.03      Although the Company would have operating revenues in Years 4-5, the
whole of the first 5-year period has to be considered as development period.
Accordingly, agricultural and management staff salaries, and incremental
current inputs such as seedcane, fertilizers, chemicals and fuel and lubricants
would be treated as capital under the Project for Years 1-5. The Project is
to be executed by the South Nyanza Sugar Company, Ltd. (hereafter referred to
as the Company) which was established in July 1976.  Management is to be
provided by Mehta Group International, Ltd. (hereafter referred to as Mehta)
under a Management Agreement concluded in July 1976 (para 4.10).  The Project
would be in itself complete and would not require further non-Company capital
to reach the 60,000 t/year capacity.  To bring the capacity to 90,000 t/year
would cost an additional KShs. 93 million (US$11.2 million), of which KShs. 22
million (US$2.7 million) would be financed through a short-term loan and the
balance through self-generated funds.
B. Detailed Features
Agriculture
4.04      Agricultural Department.  The Department would be created within
the Company for the purpose of planning and executing all agricultural
operations under the Project (para. 5.06). 1/ It would be staffed with a
1/   For costing purposes, only the Department management and the Agricultural
Services Division are included in this overhead component; the Department's
Nucleus Estate Division is covered in the nucleus estate component (para.
4.05), the Outgrowers Division in the outgrowers component (para. 4.06)
and the Research Staff Unit in the research component (para. 4.13).



- 13 -
Department Manager, division managers, section supervisors and various
administrative, specialist and general personnel and provided with the
necessary furnishings; office machines, equipment and materials; vehicles
for staff transport; land surveying equipment; and with a complete work-
shop to service and repair all of the Company's agricultural (para. 4.05-
4.06) and road construction (para. 4.11) machinery and equipment.
4.05      Nucleus Estate (Annex 7).  A nucleus estate of about 2,650 ha
net cane area would be established over a four-year period around the town
of Awendo. A drainage system would be provided for part of the estate (para.
4.07). The share of total cane produced by the estate would decrease from
about 46% in the initial year of operation to 29% at full development 1/. A
total of approximately 3,410 ha of land would have to be acquired from the
existing smallholder farmers in the area, and 500-600 families would be
displaced. After full compensation for their property, the displaced farmers
and their families would (a) be given priority in recruitment by the Company
as factory or agricultural workers; (b) to the extent possible and desired,
acquire land in the outgrowers area and participate in the Project as cane
producers; or (c) move elsewhere in the country. The Government would provide
for the orderly resettlement of all displaced persons and agreed to designate
the District Commissioner of South Nyanza as the official responsible for
carrying through (a) - (c) above in an orderly fashion. The good prospects
for employment with the Company, the availability for sale of some land in the
Project area, and the opportunity to participate in Government's settlement
schemes outside the Project area provide reasonable assurance that farmers
would not be forced into alternatives which are less desirable than their
present situation. All land required has already been gazetted which enables
the Company to claim it for its use; compensation proceedings are underway.
The time of departure of farmers from the acquired land would depend on the
Company's need to have access to the land. The Nucleus Estate Division of the
Agricultural Department which would be responsible for the development of the
estate, would be fully staffed and equipped for this purpose. The major
equipment requirements include agricultural tractors and implements, lorries,
cane transport vehicles and cane loaders.
4.06      Outgrowers (Annex 7).  The development of outgrowers land for
sugarcane production would extend over a period of 6 years and reach 9,000 ha
and involve some 4,500 smallholders. 2/ Development would progress from the
nucleus estate radially outward to approximately less than 13 km to the east
and west and 16 km, or slightly beyond, in a north-easterly and south-westerly
direction. Suitable land also is found in Kisii District adjacent to and
within about 2 km of the border with South Nyanza District and could be
utilized as needed. Generally, only the top identified soil category would
be used for cane production. The next lower suitable category with shallower
soils could be included only after special review by the Agricultural Manager
of the profitability of production on such soils. The Outgrowers Division of
the Agricultural Department would be staffed and equipped to implement the
1/   After expansion to 90,000 t annual capacity, the estate's share would
decline to 20%.
2/   Expansion to 90,000 t annual capacity would take an additional five
years and expand the number of smallholders to 7,900 and the out-
growers cane area to 15,900 ha.



- 14 -
outgrowers' production program; the Division's staff would include agricultural
extension personnel. An Outgrowers Committee would be established to promote
harmonious outgrower relationships (para. 5.08). The type of equipment required
would be similar to equipment used in the development of the nucleus estate
(para. 4.05), and all equipment would be pooled and managed by the Agricultural
Services Division. The Agricultural Department would furnish all production
inputs to outgrowers and recover its costs upon delivery of farmers' cane to
the factory. Seed cane would be produced in nurseries to be established in
both nucleus estate and outgrowers areas. Farmers would be responsible for
land clearing, planting, gapping, weeding, fertilizer application and trash
raking after harvest; they would also be able to participate in cane harvesting
as casual laborers employed by the Company. The Project's total land development
program is summarized below:

Cane harvesting and transport schedules, and machinery and equipment required
are shown in Annex 8.
4.07      Drainage (Annex 6).  Field drainage infrastructure would be installed
on nucleus estate land, about 1,170 ha of which would require drainage. To
achieve effective drainage, cross drains and main drains would traverse the
entire estate area of 3,410 ha. Field drains, to be constructed in 8-plot
units of 10 ha each, would connect with cross drains which would empty into
main drains; all drains would be open ditches. A total of 60 km cross drains
and 43 km of main drains would be built. Major excavations would be done
mechanically, while manual labor would be used for minor excavations, trimming,
dressing and maintenance. Rotary ditchers, mounted on 4-wheel drive tractors,
would be the principal type of equipment provided. The Nucleus Estate Division
would execute the work. No drainage works would be provided in the outgrowers
area as no land requiring draLnage would be utilized for cane growing there.
Sugar Factory
4.08      A new factory capable of producing mill-white sugar is to be con-
structed in the Project area. The factory is to be supplied under a turnkey
contract 1/ which includes cane handling facilities; a milling plant; juice
clarification, evaporation, boiling, cooling, curing, sugar drying and bag-
ging facilities; a steam and power generating plant; factory and agricultural
workshops; a laboratory; all standard auxiliary equipment; and spare parts
for the factory equipment. The contract provides for the supply on-site,
1/   Signed on July 15, 1976 and effective as of October 15, 1976.



-15 -
erection and commissioning of the factory over a 3-year period after the
date of effectiveness of the contract (Annex 9). The factory as specified
under the contract is a standard plant for the manufacture of sugar by the
double-sulphitation process, capable of milling 2,000 tons of cane in a
22-hour working day (TCD), and expandable to 3,000 TCD. It is to be sited
about one kilometer from Awendo town and 120 m from the Sare River which will
supply all of the required water; it will be easily accessible from the main
highway (Annex 10).
General Management and Administration
4.09      Administration.  The Finance and Administration Department and
Personnel and Industrial Relations Department of the Company would be
fully staffed and equipped with office machines, materials and furniture;
cars and communication equipment; and an ambulance. 1/ Together with the
General Manager's office, these departments would have major implementa-
tion responsibility and, after commissioning of the factory, would perform
the Company's regular administrative tasks.
4.10      General Management.  Management services for the Company are to
be provided by The Mehta Group International, Ltd. under a Management Agree-
ment signed July 15, 1976. Mehta is to be remunerated through an annual fee
up to the factory commissioning date, and through an annual fee plus a variable
commission thereafter, which is re-negotiable, for a total period of ten
years. In addition, Mehta is to be reimbursed for certain expenses. Mehta is
to pay the General Manager's salary. The scope of the services to be provided
includes technical, financial and managerial responsibility for the Company's
industrial, agricultural, personnel and administrative matters (Annex 11).
At negotiations, Government reviewed with the Bank the terms and conditions
of the Management Agreement. In the event the existing Management Agreement is
terminated, the terms and conditions of a new agreement would have to be
acceptable to the Bank.
Infrastructure
4.11      Roads.  To facilitate easy access to fields from the factory, the
road network in the Project area would be improved and expanded. Of the
estimated total of 735 km of roads and tracks to be built, 187 km would be
in the nucleus estate and 548 km in the outgrowers area; 649 km would be
non-classified and 86 km classified roads under the national highway system.
In terms of standards, the construction program would include access tracks,
access roads, feeder roads, collection roads, and a factory access road.
Foundation investigations would be undertaken to determine whether a bridge or
underpass would be built for crossing the A-1 highway. Additional survey work
would be required for roads in selected areas at distances beyond the 13 km
radius from the factory up to which roads have already been surveyed. Engi-
neering consultants for the survey and for supervision of construction would
be appointed under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank; an assurance
to this effect was obtained during negotiations. The construction of all
1/   Training activities, administered by the Personnel and Industrial Rela-
tions Department are costed under the training component (para. 4.15).



- 16 -
bitumen roads, gravel roads involving large quantities of earthwork, and major
structures (KShs. 45.8 million or US$5.5 million, including contingencies)
would be the responsibility of the Ministry of Works. The construction of
access roads and tracks and of minor works (KShs. 29.6 million or US$3.6
million) would be the responsibility of the Company. However, all roads,
except non-classified estate roads, would be eventually owned and maintained
by the Ministry of Works. The Company would create a Road Construction and
Maintenance Unit to enable it to best coordinate its agricultural development
program with the construction of access roads and tracks, and to establish road
maintenance expertise. Details on Project area roads are shown in Annex 12.
4.12      Housing and Social Amenities (Annex 13).  The Project would provide
830 housing units for the Company's permanent factory and agricultural workers
of which 10 would be for managerial staff; 34 for supervisors; and 786 for
other workers. The houses would be located close to the factory in the
direction of Awendo town. To provide accommodation needed by some estate
laborers, traditional-style housing (i.e. rondavels) would be built at several
locations within or adjacent to the estate for a total amount of KShs. 5.4 mil-
lion (US$650,000); construction would be under a sites-and-services approach,
with the Company preparing the sites and providing materials to Company
employee participants and recovering the cost through payroll deduction. A
residence for the General Manager, a guest house, fully equipped dispensary
and mother-and-child center, Company store, and facilities to conduct social
and sports activities for Company staff also would be established. All
necessary infrastructure such as lighting, water supply, sewage disposal,
community streets, etc., would be provided with the houses and auxiliary
facilities. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that architects
would be appointed under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank.
Research
4.13      The Research Staff Unit of the Company's Agricultural Department
would be staffed and equipped to (a) furnish agronomic advice to Department
staff, (b) oversee the Company's nursery operations, (c) conduct experiments
as needed to address agricultural problems that are currently encountered in
the Project area or could occur in the future, e.g. depletion of soil fertility,
weed and pest control, etc., (d) conduct experiments and investigations
towards improving sugarcane yields in the long run, and, (e) cooperate with
the National Sugar Research Institute in pursuing an effective, coordinated
national research effort to avoid duplication with research of other sugar
companies. Among the main activities of the Research Unit would be investiga-
tions related to soils, drainage and irrigation (Annex 6); variety and fertili-
zer trials, development of improved cultural practices for outgrower farmers,
pest control, and evaluation of meteorological observations (Annex 7).
4.14      To improve the effectiveness of the national research effort, the
Ministry of Agriculture's National Sugar Research Institute 1/ would be
strengthened through additional staff, vehicles and equipment. KSA would
assist in the identifcation of research needs and in the review of research
results.
1/   A change in title from the present "National Sugar Research Station,
Kibos" is recommended.



- 17 -
A comprehensive sugar research program acceptable to the Bank, covering the
key areas described in Annex 6, para. 18-20 and Annex 7, para. 26-27, would
be submitted to the Bank before July 1, 1978; an assurance to this effect was
obtained during negotiations.
Training (Annex 14)
4.15      A training program would be instituted for agricultural, factory
and administrative staff to facilitate a smooth start-up of the Company,
prepare for future staff replacements, and make a contribution to industry-
wide manpower development. The local program would include apprenticeships
in existing sugar companies, training at Industrial Training Centers, and
on-the-job training supplemented by work-time classes within the Company.
Overseas training in business administration and sugar technology would
be provided under a special program to meet industry needs.  The Company's
Training Officer would cooperate with the Training Officer of KSA in preparing
specific Company training programs. In order to best meet the long-term
manpower requirements of the sugar industry, KSA would prepare an industry-wide
training program showing specific needs for personnel and targets to be
achieved; a program acceptable to the Bank would be submitted to the Bank
before December 31, 1978. An assurance to this effect was obtained during
negotiations.
Kenya Sugar Authority (KSA)
4.16      The Project would strengthen the Kenya Sugar Authority through
the provision of additional staff, vehicles, and office space and equipment,
and assist its reorganization to enable it to play a wider and more effective
role in the development of the sugar industry and to make it the Government's
principal instrument for overseeing the industry (para. 5.01). The Project
would also provide funds (KShs. 3.1 million or US$375,000) for studies to
be conducted by KSA as summarized in Annex 17, para. 26. At negotiations,
Government agreed to reorganize KSA along the lines described in Annex 17. It
would be a condition of loan effectiveness that Government had taken all
actions necessary to expand the membership of KSA (Annex 17, para. 7) and to
authorize it to carry out the enlarged functions (Annex 17, para. 4). It was
agreed that in addition to the Chief Executive and Economist/Statistician
already in post, a Planning Officer, Financial Analyst, Agronomist, Agricul-
tural Extension Specialist, Sugar Technologist, Training Officer and appro-
priate support staff would be employed by January 1, 1978. KSA would prepare
and submit to the Bank a plan for future financing of its operation by July
1,1979. Until such a plan can be implemented Government would assure that
sufficient funds are made available to KSA to finance all expenditures
approved by the Members of KSA and not covered under the Project. KSA's
Financial Analyst, to be provided under the Project, would be responsible for
monitoring the progress of the Project.
Interest During Construction (Annex 20)
4.17      Total interest and other charges on Project loans from all sources
(para. 4.19) would amount to KShs. 139.4 million (US$16.8 million) over five
years. Interest and other charges payable by the Company over the five-year
period would be KShs. 105.6 million (US$12.7 million), of which KShs. 33.0

lending rate to Government rather than the on-lending rate to the Company

amount to the Company for interest and other charges payable by the Company to
Government on the on-lent portion of the World Bank loan.

turnkey contract, the Company management fee, not directly Project-related
staff training, and Kenya Sugar Authority salaries, for which no physical
contingencies have been allowed. Price contingencies for the five years
1977-81 have been included at the following annual rates: 13%, 12%, 12%,
12% and 10% respectively, for civil works; 9%, 8%, 8%, 8% and 7% for vehicles
and equipment; and 10%, 10%, 10%, 10%, and 9% for salaries (Annex 15, Appen-

portion of the factory turnkey contrat since that contract does not provide
for such contingencies. Local taxes, principally including import duties,
excise duties and income taxes on earnings of Project staff, estimated at
KShs. 82.6 million (US$10.0 million) are included in the above Project costs
estimates.

in Year 5, the year with the least favorable ratio.

E. Procurement
4.23      Procurement from the proceeds of the World Bank loan would be as
follows:
(a) The Company's procurement of vehicles, equipment, and office
machines and furniture (US$3.5 million, including contingen-
cies) would be by international competitive bidding, following
Bank guidelines. Local manufacturers would be allowed a
preference margin of 15% or the existing rate of duty, whichever
is lower. Orders for less than US$100,000, incremental fertili-
zers and all fuels and lubricants (US$2.2 million) would be
procured in accordance with local procedures which are satisfac-
tory; the national distribution and prices of fuels are Govern-
ment controlled and fertilizers are bulk-procured by Government
on a national basis, and international competitive bidding
would not be appropriate. Orders would be bulked to the
maximum extent possible. While the procurement of vehicles and
equipment for KSA and the National Sugar Research Institute
(US$0.3 million) would in principle be by international competi-
tive bidding, individual orders would be spread out over a
three-year period and, most likely, exempt on account of size.
Procurement for the National Sugar Research Institute would be
through the Ministry of Agriculture; KSA would be responsible
for its own procurement.
(b) Civil works contracts for the construction of houses and social
amenities (US$7.4 million), and of roads under the responsibility
of the Ministry of Works (US$5.5 million) would also be procured
by international competitive bidding except for contracts or
items costing less than US$200,000. Prequalified local firms
would be given a 7-1/2% preference margin in the evaluation of
their bids. The construction of access roads and tracks
(US$3.6 million) would be the responsibility of the Company and
undertaken by the Company's Road Construction and Maintenance
Unit or, to the extent that the Unit's capacity is exceeded,
by local contractors to be selected under local procurement
procedures (para. 4.11 and Annex 12).
(c) Training activities other than those under the Company's re-
sponsibility would be conducted by KSA (US$0.3 million), with



- 22 -
the candidates for training and the educational institu-
tions to be selected by KSA's Training Officer. KSA's Planning
Officer would undertake industry-related studies (US$0.4 million)
as approved by the Bank.
Draft tender documents for all contracts expected to cost in excess of
US$300,000 would be submitted to the Bank for approval before invitations
are issued, and bid analysis and recommendation for award would be sub-
mitted to the Bank for comment before contracts are awarded. Any items
retroactively financed would have had to meet the above procurement re-
quirements. Assurances that the above procedures would be followed were
obtained at negotiations.
