G. Accounts and Audits
4.26     Satisfactory separate accounts geared to producing cost control
and management information related to all operating departments would be kept
by the Finance and Administration Department of the Company. The Department
would also be in charge of maintaining the credit accounts for individual
outgrowers. Satisfactory separate accounts also would be kept by the Kenya
Sugar Authority, the National Sugar Research Institute and other Government
agencies concerned. Assurances were be obtained during negotiations that
the above accounts would be maintained, that the accounts would be audited by
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank and that the accounts and auditors'
reports would be submitted to the Bank within six months of the close of their
fiscal year, except for the Company's accounts and reports which would be
submitted within four months of the close of its fiscal year.
H. Ecological and External Effects
4.27      The proposed location of the factory was selected taking into
account the prevailing wind direction to minimize any annoyance of the
nearby community through factory odor and smoke. Fly-ash arresting equip-
ment would be installed to keep ash dust precipitation to an acceptable
minimum. The proposed factory process would require relatively few and
harmless chemicals; the industrial lime to be used would be disposed with
the filter mud as a soil conditioner in the cane fields. The major factor
in polluting the effluents would be leaks of sugar solution into factory
drains. Equipment and facilities to be provided for controlling effluents
from the factory would include a treatment plant with mixing chambers and
a series of evaporation ponds. The proposed use of fertilizers would not
have any harmful long-run effects on soil fertility or the groundwater
in the area. There would be no danger of soil erosion in view of the long
vegetative cover resulting from the extended growing period of sugarcane
and exclusion of steep slopes from cane cultivation. Soil damage through
cane transport vehicles would be minimized by limiting overall vehicle
weight for infield operations and the tire pressure on the ground through
appropriate design of cane trailers. An assurance was obtained at negotiations
that the Company would implement the Project with due regard to environmental
factors and, specifically, that the air and water purification facilities
provided for in the factory turnkey contract should be installed.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A.   The Kenya Sugar Authority (Annex 17)
5.01      The Kenya Sugar Authority would be a Government organization which
would advise it and be empowered to regulate, guide, monitor, advise and in
certain circumstances direct the development of the sugar industry. The sugar



- 25 -
companies would, however, remain self-governing. Established under the
Agriculture Act, KSA's primary responsibility would be to the Minister of
Agriculture. The management of the Authority would be entrusted to the
Members of the Authority, with the Chairman and representatives of sugarcane
growers, the sugar companies and the commercial sector at large to be appointed
by the Minister of Agriculture; other Members would represent the Ministry of
Agriculture and other Government Ministries concerned with the sugar industry.
The Chief Executive, appointed by the Members with the approval of the Minister
of Agriculture, would be in charge of operations and administration of the
Authority. Government would be responsible for ensuring financing of KSA;
financial requirements, in addition to Project funds, would be met initially
from the national budget and eventually as determined in KSA's future financing
plan (para. 4.16). The proposed organization of KSA is depicted in Chart
World Bank - 16461.
B.   Project Construction Organization
5.02      The overall responsibility for implementing the agricultural,
company research and training, factory and housing components of the Project
would rest with the Company, and specifically the Company's Managing Agents.
The Company would also be responsible for the construction of access roads
and tracks. The Ministry of Works would be responsible for the construction
of roads other than access roads and tracks, and for all road maintenance,
except the Company-owned roads in the nucleus estate which would be maintained
by the Company. The implementation of the programs to strengthen the Kenya
Sugar Authority and the National Sugar Research Institute would be the
responsibility of the Ministry of Agriculture. The component concerning
training not directly related to the South Nyanza Sugar Company would be
implemented through KSA.
5.03      The agricultural development program would be handled by the
staff of the Company's Agricultural Department using machinery and equipment
provided under the Project (para. 4.04). The factory development program \
would be handled as a turnkey package with equipment provided by, and civil
works undertaken by a German/Indian group of reputable firms with experience
in the sugar industry (Annex 9). The Bank was given an opportunity to comment
on the specifications, terms and conditions of the turnkey contract (para.
4.08) prior to signing even though it would not be involved in financing any
costs under that contract. The terms and conditions of the contract were also
reviewed at negotiations.
5.04      The supervision of construction, equipping and testing of the
factory would be by Mehta under a Management Agreement with the Company (Annex
11). Government would engage at its own expense a consultant or firm of
consultants, who would report to the Company's Board of Directors, to monitor
the performance in construction under the factory turnkey contract. Such an
arrangement would be desirable since the Company's management firm, i.e.,
Mehta, is also a party to the factory supply contract. An assurance was
obtained at negotiations that Government would have appointed a consultant or
firm of consultants satisfactory to the Bank by October 15, 1977 and retain
the consultant's service until the acceptance tests for the factory have been
successfully completed.



- 26 -
C.   Company Organization and Management (Annex 18)
General Management
5.05      The chief executive officer of the South Nyanza Sugar Company, Ltd.
would be the General Manager who would be appointed and salaried by the
Company's Managing Agent and be responsible to the Company's Board of
Directors. He would manage the affairs of the Company through four adminis-
trative departments: Agricultural, Factory, Finance and Administration, and
Personnel and Industrial Relations (Chart World Bank - 16462). The Minister of
Agriculture is to appoint the Board of Directors, on which the Mehta Group
is to be represented by one Director. The Management Agreement appointing the
Company's Managing Agent is described in Annex 11.
The Agricultural Department
5.06      Under the supervision of its manager, the Agricultural Depart-
ment of the Company would be responsible for all agricultural aspects of
the Project. The Department's Agricultural Services Division, through its
Construction, Harvesting, Transport and Workshop Sections, would provide
ali necessary services for the development of estate and outgrowers' land,
except agricultural extension which would be the responsibility of the
Company's Outgrowers Division. The Survey Unit in the Construction Section
would be responsible for surveying the area of farmers' fields, and assist the
Engineering Consultants in aligning roads and tracks to be constructed by the
Company (para. 4.11). The Land Preparation Unit would carry out the mechanized
operations of the planting program under supervision by the Nucleus Estates
Division (para. 5.07) and the Outgrowers' Division (para. 5.08). The Harvest-
ing Section and Transport Section would be in charge of sugarcane harvesting
and transport both for the nucleus estate and outgrowers. A workshop would be
fully equipped to service and repair all agricultural machinery and equipment
and the machinery and equipment used by the Road Construction and Maintenance
Unit. The latter unit would be administratively part of the Agricultural
Services Division to make possible its easy use of the workshop facilities and
an exchange of equipment with the Land Preparation Unit should such be required.
T`he Department's Research Staff Unit would carry out the Company's experimental
activities and supervise the establishment of the cane nurseries in cooperation
with the National Sugar Research Institute.
5.07      The Nucleus Estates Division of the Agricultural Department would be
responsible for the planning and implementation of the estate's development
program through its three operational sect-ions. Land clearing and preparation
would be mechanized, weed controi would be done both mechanically and manually,
and harvesting would be entirely manual- The planting and harvesting schedule
would be coordinated with outgrowers  operations to best meet the factory's
cane requirements. The cane would be burned before harvesting. The Nucleus
Estates Division would also implement the drainage component with the Depart-
ment's own staff and equipment.



- 27 -
5.08      The Outgrowers' Division of the Agricultural Department, organized
into four operational sections, would be responsible for executing the
outgrowers program. Upon indication of farmers' interest, the Division
would arrange for a soil survey through Government or private agencies 1/
and require neighboring farmers to form blocks of land of sufficient size for
efficient cane production (i.e., at least 10 ha) with assistance from the
Department's Research Staff Unit and its own Survey Unit. Individual contracts
would be established between each farmer and the Company. While farmers would
be required to clear their own land, the Outgrowers Division would arrange for
subsequent preparation of the land through the Department's Land Preparation
Unit (para. 5.06); provide seed cane, inputs and implements on credit; and
furnish full extension advice with regard to planting, weed and pest control
and trash disposal after harvesting. The Division would also arrange for cane
harvesting, transport and replanting. To minimize harvesting costs, the
Division's staff would investigate the possibility of permitting the burning
of outgrowers' cane under Company supervision on blocks of land where, in the
judgement of Company staff, cane fires would not pose any environmental risks.
The full cost of all services rendered by the Company, including interest at
commercial rates (i.e. 11% in 1977), would be deducted from farmers' proceeds
from the sale of sugarcane to the factory, based on a pre-determined cost
recovery schedule; farmers' accounts would be kept by the Company's Finance
and Administration Department (para. 5.10). The Company would establish and
maintain an Outgrowers Committee, the members of which would be representatives
of Government, the Company and outgrowers; the Committee would meet regularly
for the purpose of promoting harmonious cooperation among Government officials,
outgrowers and the Company. An assurance was obtained during negotiations
that the proposed outgrowers organization would be maintained during the
Company's initial five year period, except for changes approved by the Bank.
The Department's organization is shown in Chart World Bank - 16464.
The Factory Department
5.09      The Factory Department Manager would operate the factory through
three Divisions. The Engineering Division staff would be responsible for
mechanical processing of the cane through the cane yard and the milling and
boiler stations, and for providing civil construction, and electrical and
mechanical engineering services for the factory as a whole. The staff of
the Processing Division would supervise the extraction process from juice
clarification to the bagging of sugar, and be responsible for the Company's
water supply and effluent treatment. The staff of the Control Division,
reporting directly to the Factory Manager, would gather standard technical
data in the factory and perform analyses for quality control purposes.
The Factory Department's organization is shown in Chart World Bank - 16465.
The Finance and Administration Department
5.10      Headed by the Controller and organized into three Divisions, the
Assistant Controllers' and Budget Office and the Auditing Office, the staff of
1/   Although Government agencies would conduct soil surveys at no cost to
the Company, funds are provided under the Project for supplementary
surveys to be undertaken by private agencies, if necessary, to guarantee
timeliness of the surveys.



- 28 -
the Department would prepare operational and financial plans, keep financial
and other operational records, handle receipt and disbursement of funds, order
and manage materials and supplies, obtain all necessary insurance coverages,
arrange for financing, and continuously improve procedures to the benefit
of operational efficiency. It would also prepare, and furnish to the Kenya
Sugar Authority, information on the cost of cane production and on factory
margins for purposes of formulating pricing recommendations and periodically
determine the cost of Company services rendered to outgrowers and formulate a
plan for the full recovery of those costs upon delivery to the factory of
outgrowers' cane. Outgrowers' accounts with the Company would be administered
by the General Accounting Division. The Department's organization is depicted
in Chart World Bank - 16466.
The Personnel and Industrial Relations Department
5.11      The staff of the Department, under the Personnel and Industrial
Relations Manager, in addition to attending to standard personnel matters,
also would be responsible for security and safety matters, for supervising
the Company's health program and social activities, and for fostering good
relations with outgrowers. The Department's Training Officer would prepare
the Company's staff training program in cooperation with KSA's Training
Officer. The Department's organization is shown in Chart World Bank - 16467.
D.   Staffing
5.12      Of the Company's estimated 920 permanent employees, about 36 would
be recruited internationally and provided under the Management Agreement
(Annex 11). Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the Bank
would be consulted in the appointment or replacement of the General Manager,
the Controller, and the managers of the Agricultural Department, Factory
Department, and Personnel and Industrial Relations Department. The appoint-
ment of the General Manager, Controller and Agricultural Department Manager
would be a condition of loan effectiveness. Local administrative and skilled
staff would be obtained through a country-wide recruitment effort; semi-skilled
and unskilled workers would be available in Nyanza and Western Provinces.
Training would be provided for about 320 Company employees (para. 4.15). The
intensive training program combined with advance hiring of selected staff, and
the initial placement of some unskilled but trainable personnel into positions
requiring minor skills would enable the Company to meet its staffing needs
without depleting the trained staff of existing sugar companies. The training
programs supported by the Project also would enable the Company to comply with
Government's requirements for replacing internationally recruited staff with
qualified Kenyans consistent with the need to maintain a sound management and
technical cadre in the Company.
5.13      The Project would provide 18 additional staff to the Kenya Sugar
Authority, of whom five senior staff would be internationally recruited in the



- 29 -
event no suitable local candidates were available. Assurances were obtained
that the Bank would be consulted in the appointment or replacement of KSA's
Planning Officer, Agronomist and Sugar Technologist. Of the 22 additional
staff provided to the National Sugar Research Institute, three would be
internationally recruited.   A recruitment period of one year has been allowed
in the evaluation for the internationally recruited staff of the National
Sugar Research Institute; all senior KSA staff would be employed by January 1,
1978 (para. 4.16).
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETING AND FINANCIAL RESULTS
Cane and Sugar Yields and Production
6.01      The average yield projections for nucleus estate and outgrowers
cane have been derived from the experience of Mumias and the Sugar Belt
companies, taking into account micro-climatic conditions in the Project
area and making adjustments based on experimental and practical results.
While pests and diseases are not expected to be a serious problem, recurrent
dry spells would have a depressing effect on yields; yield projections have
been adjusted on the assumption of a dry year occurring once in five years. A
system of one plant crop and two ratoon crops is recommended. The predominant
cane variety grown initially would be C0421. Preventive pest and disease
control measures would be investigated and variety trials expanded, and the
production system and variety recommendations would be altered in the light of
new results. The cane yield assumptions are:

6.02      The cane/sugar ratio has varied greatly over the years and among
companies in Kenya; current extraction averages, for example, 9% at Chemelil
and 11% at Mumias. The Project's sugar projections are based on 10% extrac-
tion for the first five years of factory operation, and on 10.5% thereafter.
6.03      The production of cane and sugar for the Project, and with expansion
to an annual capacity of 90,000 tons, is estimated to develop as follows:

Markets
6.04      With an annual growth in per capita consumption of 3.5-4.5% after
1977 and a projected population of 18.6 million in 1985, total consumption
of sugar would reach 327,000-355,000 tons by 1985. The production of estab-
lished areas, with the minor improvements currently being implemented, would
stabilize around 189,000 tons per year after 1979, thus creating domestic
deficits amounting to 138,000-166,000 tons in 1985. Taking the South Nyanza
project alone, the effect would be to reduce these deficits. Other sugar
projects (para. 1.02) together with South Nyanza would eliminate the deficits
and, it is estimated, eventually produce a temporary surplus. It is uncertain,
lowever, to what extent actual performance under these projects would result
in the projected output; under the most favorable assumptions, Kenya's total
sugar production would increase to 460,000 tons by 1985 (Annex 4). Although
South Nyanza's cost of production would be very close to or above export
parity (not import parity) it may be that other producers would be able to
compete in international trade. In view of the uncertainties surrounding pro-
jected industry production it has been assumed in the evaluation that the
Project's output would replace imports. It was agreed at negotiations that
Government would keep the proiected productioni and consumption of sugar in
Kenya under continuous review, and that the Bank would be informed annually
of the results of that review and of Government's plans to market all sugar
produced in Kenya and to invest in substantial additional sugar milling
facilities over the three-year period fcllowing the review.



- 31 -
Prices
6.05      The world market price of sugar for 1980, expressed in March, 1977
constant dollar terms, is projected at 15.2 USJ/lb., raw f.o.b. Caribbean,
compared with 31.1 US/lb. in 1974, 22.1 USJ/lb. in 1975, and 14.0 USJ/lb. in
the first half of 1976 (Annex 19). The average realized import price of
refined sugar for 1975-76 was KShs. 3,489/t (US/lb. 19.1), landed in Mombasa;
the projected price for 1980 and thereafter is KShs. 4,076/t (USU/lb. 22.3),
expressed in March, 1977 constant terms. The evaluation of the Project is
based on these import substitution prices. Molasses could be exported at
prices of KShs. 350/t f.o.b. Mombasa; funds for the transport of molasses
have been included in the Project cost estimates.
6.06      The price paid at the factories for sugar was KShs. 2,300/t in 1976,
compared with KShs. 1,860/t in 1975 and KShs. 1,230/t in 1974. Assuming a
cane/sugar ratio of 10:1, the factories' gross margin in 1976 was KShs. 1,250/t.
KSA is proposing a factory price of KShs. 2,900/t for 1977, implying a factory
margin of KShs. 1,400/t. The evaluation is based on a factory price of
KShs. 3,050/t from 1980 onwards, which is not inconsistent with Government's
long-term pricing policy. The margin and operating costs of the factories
would be reviewed, and factory prices adjusted annually (para. 6.08).
6.07      The price of sugarcane, at factory gate, was KShs. 105/t in 1976,
KShs. 92/t in 1975 and KShs. 62/t in 1974. The 1976 farm gate price in the
Sugar Belt, i.e., net of transport and cooperative charges was around
KShs. 70-78/t. After deducting cash outlays for production, farmers' net
returns to labor and land were in many instances reduced to disincentive
levels, contributing to the cane shortage in the Sugar Belt. To provide
greater incentives to farmers, KSA is proposing an increase to KShs. 150/t in
1977. Based on the Project's financial analysis, a price of about KShs. 155/t
(i.e. effective from 1979) would be desirable (Annex 20). Government is
expected to adopt a price of only KShs. 132/t for 1977 and it was agreed at
negotiations that the 1977 price would be fully adjusted for inflation in 1978
and 1979, and that an incentive margin of at least 6% would be added after
adjustment for inflation in each of those years.
6.08      The retail price of sugar was raised sharply to KShs. 4.50/kg
in 1976 from KShs. 3.50/kg in 1975 and KShs. 2.40/kg in 1974, with the effect
of depressing per capita demand for sugar from about 17.6 kg in 1974 to below
13.0 kg in 1976. To keep import requirements for sugar in the near future at
a minimum, Government intends to set retail prices at levels which would
allow the consumption of sugar to expand only at the same rate as domestic
production. If self-sufficiency were achieved in the early 1980's with
an annual increase in per capita consumption of 3.5-4.5% (para. 6.04), which
would be reasonable, and production continued to increase more rapidly tor a
few years, a price policy would have to be designed in accordance with the
prevailing world market situation. Should exports become possible, the retail
price could be kept relatively high to facilitate the accumulation of export-
able surpluses. If exports were not profitable, domestic consumption could be
further stimulated by reducing the excise tax on sugar and, thus, lowering



- 32 -
the retail price. A reduction in the retail price could not result in a
reduction in the ex-factory price, however, if this would effect the financial
viability of the sugar companies. Another alternative would be to maintain a
relatively high retail price and generate a sufficient equalization fund to
subsidize exports if necessary. Limited export subsidies could be justified
on economic grounds as Kenya's overvalued currency reduces the competitiveness
of the country's products in export markets (para. 7.03). Assurances were
obtained at negotiations that Government would review the prices of sugarcane
and sugar annually and submit to the Bank a report on the outcome of the
review.
Company Financial Results (Annex 20)
6.09      With the initial factory capacity of 2,000 TCD, the Company would
reach full production levels in Year 9. Annual operating profits would be
achieved from Year 6 onwards, but because of heavy pre-production expendi-
tures and debt service obligations, the Company would not produce surpluses
and pay dividends before Year 11. The financial rate of return on invest-
ments would be 11%.
6.10      Expansion of the factory to 3,000 TCD and additional agricultural
development to achieve an annual sugar production of 90,000 t would cost
KShs. 93 million (US$ 11.2 million) and require additional short-term loan
financing of about KShs. 22 million (US$2.7 million) in Year 6; full produc-
tion would be achieved in Year 12. The Company's ratio of long-term debt to
equity would increase from 58:42 in Year 1 to 64:36 in Year 5, then decline
to 50:50 in Year 9 and to 25:75 in year 15. With full production, annual
net income after taxes would amount to 17% of revenue, and 24% of equity;
the first dividends would be paid in Year 10. With expansion to 3,000 TCD
and the assumed prices (para. 6.06), the internal financial rate of return
on investments would be 13%. The rate of return would be 10% if sugar prices
declined by 10%, and 12% if capital cost increased by 10%.
Government Budget and Foreign Exchange (Annex 20)
6.11       As a result of Government's equity participation in the Company
(para. 4.21), its direct investments under the Project and debt service,
there would arise a net claim on the national budget in each of the first
three Project years, amounting to KShs. 92 million (US$11.1 million) on a
cumulative basis by the end of Year 3. From Year 4 onwards, there is a
possibility of generating Government revenues through the sugar excise tax,
the magnitude of which would depend on market conditions for sugar and,
consequently, Government's price policy (para. 6.08). With the present excise
tax of KShs. 1,000/t, the Project would make a net annual contribution to the
budget from Project Year 4 onwards. Expressed in constant Year 5 (1981) prices,
Government's annual net revenue from the Project would remain modest during
Years 4 to 9, averaging about KShs. 30 million (US$3.6 million); it would
then increase sharply in Year 10 and reach KShs. 149 million (US$18.0 million)
in Year 16, excluding Company profits or KShs. 212 million (US$25.6 million)
including Company profits. Foreign exchange outflows would exceed inflows



- 33 -
in three of the first five years of the Project, producing a foreign ex-
change deficit for the whole five-year period of KShs. 103 million
(US$12.4 million); thereafter, the annual foreign exchange deficit would
be about KShs. 208 million (US$25.1 million) in Year 6, then decline
gradually and amount to KShs. 120 million (US$14.5 million) in Year 16.
However, there would be foreign exchange savings as the sugar produced as
a result of the Project would substitute for imports. Based on the assumed
prices (para. 6.05) the overall net foreign exchange savings effect of the
Project is estimated at KShs. 217 million (US$26.2 million) for the first
five-year period as a whole. The annual net savings effect would be KShs. 41
million (US$4.9 million) in Year 6 and increase to KShs. 375 million (US$45.2
million) in Year 16 (expressed in Year 5 (1981) prices). Expressed in constant
1977 prices, net foreign exchange savings at full development in Year 12 would
be KShs. 210 million (US$25 million).
Farmer Benefits (Annex 20)
6.12      The outgrowers' budget for sugarcane indicates that the price
paid to farmers for sugarcane would have to be raised significantly to provide
sufficient incentives for production, especially since farmers would get only
three harvests in five years (para. 6.07). To make cane production more
attractive to farmers, in addition to raising the price of sugarcane, the
Company would provide all services and inputs on credit. Assuming a cane
price of KShs. 155/t and a sugarcane area per farm family of 2.0 ha, the
average increase in annual farmer income at full development would be KShs.
3,055 (US$368); it would be negative every fifth year and average KShs.
3,931 (US$474) in the intervening four years. On a per capita basis, average
incomes would increase from KShs. 244 (US$29) to KShs. 681 (US$82). This
compares with an average national GDP per capita of KShs. 1,573 (US$210) in
1975. Farmers' income risks would be minimized by not requiring any cash
contributions from farmers (at least initially), and rescheduling the repayment
of credit in the event of crop failures; intercropping in the first year after
planting of sugarcane would reduce the Project's potentially adverse effect
on subsistence production.
VII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
(Annex 21)
7.01      The primary direct benefits of the Project would be increased
production of sugar and a resultant reduction of sugar imports. While,
with present levels of taxation and as the majority-owner of the Company,
Government would be the predominant beneficiary from the Project, signifi-
cant benefits also would be derived by the approximately 4,000 persons
gaining employment under the Project, and by some 7,900 outgrower farmers
whose incomes would be increased.
7.02      The Project's internal economic rate of return is estimated to be
25% over a 25-year period. All Project costs except 25% of the costs of roads
and tracks and 85% of the costs of the Kenya Sugar Authority, the National



- 34 -
Sugar Research Institute, and training not directly related to the Company
have been included in the calculation. All non-farm family labor has been
fully costed at market wage rates; farm family labor has been shadow-priced
at 55% of market wage rates. To account for some loss in agricultural pro-
duction resulting from the planting of sugarcane under the Project, the net
value of production foregone on an area equivalent to about 40% of the cane
area has been included as an economic cost. The value of the Project's
incremental sugar output is based on import parity prices as the entire output
would substitute for sugar imports; molasses has been valued on the basis of
export-parity prices. Foreign exchange costs and benefits are valued at
KShs. 11.00 = US$1.00 to reflect the economic costs of foreign exchange to
Kenya; if foreign exchange were valued at the prevailing exchange rate of
KShs. 8.30 = US$1.00, the rate of return would be 23%.
7.03      The risks most likely to effect the Project's economic rate of
return are the uncertainties surrounding the availability of sugarcane for
the factory. Shortfalls below expectations could result from the occurance
of more frequent or more severe droughts in the Project area than antici-
pated, or from difficulties in relations between the Company and its out-
grower farmers. The drought risk, which would become apparent before the
planned expansion from 60,000 t to 90,000 t annual capacity in Years 6-8,
could be alleviated by foregoing that expansion and producing sugarcane only
within a shorter distance from the factory and only on the very best soils to
cushion the drought impact on farmers' incomes through higher yields and
through higher farm gate prices resulting from transport savings; and by
providing supplementary irrigation in selected areas. The financial risk to
the Company as a result of an early or very severe drought would not be
critical as the proposed financial structure of the Company (para. 6.10) would
provide for extra borrowing capacity to meet exceptional needs. To minimize
the risk associated with outgrowers' cane supply, a suitable incentive price
policy would be pursued by Government, and an organizational structure is
proposed under the Project which incorporates experience gained in the Sugar
Belt and at Mumias. Nevertheless, even if incremental output or import
substitution prices were 20% below expectation, the Project would still show a
rate of return of 20%; without expansion beyond the initial 60,000 t annual
capacity, the rate of return would be 23%. Another risk for the Project would
be the production of sugar surpluses as a result of three other sugar projects
that are likely to be implemented concurrently, and the possibility that the
cost of production under the Project could exceed the prevailing export price
of sugar. However, this risk appears less severe for the industry as a whole
as other sugar companies might still be able to export profitably at prices
which would make exports unprofitable for the South Nyanza Sugar Company.
Furthermore, Kenya's overvalued currency adversely affects the country's
competitiveness in export markets; hence, exports could be promoted through
devaluation or a subsidy to producers. That exports would be economically
feasible (though might entail a financial subsidy to the industry) is reflected
in the economic rate of return based on export parity prices which would be
19% (16% without expansion to 90,000 t annual capacity).



- 35 -
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
8.01      During loan negotiations, agreement was reached on the following
principal points:
(a) the preparation of a plan for the improvement of the sugar
distribution system and of sugar marketing in Kenya by
December 31, 1978 (para. 2.24);
(b) the orderly resettlement of farmers displaced by the nucleus
estate (para. 4.05);
(c) the appointment of engineering consultants (para. 4.11) and
architects (para. 4.12) under terms and conditions satisfactory
to the Bank;
(d) the preparation of a comprehensive national sugar research
program by July 1, 1978 (para. 4.14);
(e) the preparation of a sugar industry training program by
December 31, 1978 (para. 4.15);
(f) the employment of all senior staff of the Kenya Sugar Authority
by January 1, 1978 (para. 4.16);
(g) the preparation of a future financing plan for the Kenya Sugar
Authority by July 1, 1979 (para. 4.16);
(h) the appointment of a consultant or firm of consultants satis-
factory to the Bank to monitor the performance in construction
under the factory turnkey contract by October 15, 1977 (para. 5.04);
(i) the appointment, in consultation with the Bank, of key staff of
the Company (para. 5.12) and the Kenya Sugar Authority (para. 5.13);
(j) the continuous review by Government of the production and consump-
tion of sugar in Kenya, and the reporting to the Bank of the
results of the review and of plans to market all sugar produced
in Kenya and to invest in additional sugar milling facilities
(para. 6.04);
(k) the adjustment of the price of sugarcane for the years 1978 and
1979 (para. 6.07), and the annual review by Government of the
prices of sugarcane and sugar and the reporting to the Bank of
the results of the review (para. 6.08).



- 36 -
8.02      The following would be conditions of loan effectiveness:
(a)  that the Government had taken all actions necessary to expand
the membership of KSA and to authorize it to carry out its
newly enlarged functions (para. 4.16);
(b)  an agreement satisfactory to the Bank covering the financing
terms between Government and the Company had been executed
(para. 4.20);
(c)  that evidence satisfactory to the Bank of the availability
of the loans of the European Investment Bank and the African
Development Bank had been provided (para. 4.22);
(d)  a share subscription agreement satisfactory to the Bank between
the Company and Mehta had been entered into (para. 4.22);
(e)  the Company's General Manager, Controller and Agricultural
Department Manager had been appointed (para. 5.12).
8.03      The Project is suitable for a Bank Loan of US$25.0 million for
20 years, including a grace period of 4.5 years.

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
1.        Agriculture, forestry and fishing comprise the largest sector of
the economy, contributing about KShs. 6.3 billion, or 30% of the Gross Domes-
tic Product (GDP) in 1975. Between 1964 and 1974, subsistence agriculture,
according to the National Accounts Statistics, expanded at an annual rate of
3.6%, while commercial agriculture grew at a rate of 5.3% per year and the
economy as a whole at 7.1%. In the wake of the world-wide recession, annual
growth rates in 1975 slowed to 0.7% for the economy as a whole and 0.4% for
subsistence agriculture; commercial agriculture declined by 1.0% (Table 1).
2.        Agriculture also accounts for about 70% of total exports.  Agricul-
tural exports increased from KShs. 2,020 million in 1965 to KShs. 4,540 mil-
lion in 1975. The major export commodities in 1975 were coffee (KShs. 704
million), tea (KShs. 458 million), sisal (KShs. 146 million), and meat and
meat products (KShs. 100 million); other major foreign exchange earners were
dairy products, pyrethrum and hides and skins (Table 2). Against these ex-
ports were agricultural imports, aggravating the already serious balance of
payments problem; imports of food amounted to KShs. 264 million and of edible
oils and fats to KShs. 152 million.
3.        Forty-two million ha, or about 73% of Kenya's total land area of 58
million ha has moderate or better agricultural or range potential. Eleven
million ha, or about 20% of the land area, is of medium or high agricultural
potential. The other areas have limited livestock potential, partly due to
tsetse fly infestation. Approximately 2.7 million ha is under large farms.
Wheat, sisal and maize are the predominant cash crops on large farms by area,
followed by sugarcane, coffee and tea. Smallholders' major subsistence food
crops are maize, sorghum, millet, pulses and bananas. Cash crops are also
grown by smallholders, especially tea, cotton, coffee, cashewnut and sugarcane.
Irrigation development has been of relatively minor importance
to date, but increased emphasis is now being given to more fully utilize
the country's limited potential. About 160,000 ha have been identified as
suitable for new large-scale irrigation.
4.        Marketed production of crops has been fluctuating considerably
in recent years. For the period 1970-75, no discernable trend was apparent
for rice and cotton production. Wheat production followed a down-trend,
while maize, coffee and tea increased. Sisal production fluctuated over
100% during the 5-year period. The production of sugarcane is reported to
have varied by 80%, increasing from the period's low in 1972 to a record high
level of 1.9 million tons in 1974 (Table 3). In terms of farmgate value of
marketed production in 1975, coffee ranked highest (KShs. 661 million),
followed by tea (KShs. 413 million), maize (KShs. 325 million), and cattle
(KShs. 373 million). Sugarcane was valued at KShs. 165 million. The esti-
mated total gross farm revenue was KShs. 3,005 million in 1975 (Table 4).



ANNEX 1
Page 2
5.        In order to counteract the adverse effects of recent external
economic changes, the Government has developed a restructuring policy which,
in the agricultural sector, aims at icreasing domestic food production,
substituting domestic products for imports, and increasing exports. The
sector is to play an even larger role than before in absorbing labor and
maintaining incomes and consumption. Agricultural investments are seen as
producing relatively high returns, contributing to a more even regional
distribution of development, and as promising with respect to generating a
significant rural impact. In support of this policy, Government intends
to offer adequate price incentives to farmers, increase the share of the
development budget allocated to agriculture, and make the necessary organi-
zational changes within Government to improve administrative efficiency. The
development of smallholder agriculture is to play a key role and efforts are
underway to overcome major obstacles such as the large number of farmers
involved, the remoteness and marginal quality of some of the land, and the
need to increase the productivity of subsistence crops in order to release
land for the production of cash crops.
December 30, 1976

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
HISTORY AND PERFORMANCE OF THE SUGAR INDUSTRY
Early Development
1.        The Kenya sugar industry has its origins in the establishment in
the 1920s of two privately-owned factories, one in Ramisi, Coast Province,
and another in Miwani, Nyanza Province. No new mills were added during the
following four decades, and the country depended primarily on sugar imports
to meet its increasing domestic requirements. Imports for the twelve years
1955-66, for example, amounted to an annual average of 59,000 tons, about
twice as much as domestic annual average production of mill-white sugar of
28,900 tons for the same period. The so-called "mill-white" sugar produced
in Kenya is a "sulfitation" sugar, the color of which is much darker than
that of lustrous white crystal sugar. Kenya's sugar does not conform with
Grade "B" sugar in the World Health Organization standards in terms of color
even though Government policy defines this as the official industry standard.
However, the quality in terms of its nutritional content is the same and is
acceptable for Kenya household consumption.
2.        Sugarcane is also produced in areas without sugar factories,
primarily for processing in "jaggery" factories. Jaggery is a form of dark-
brown sugar in solid form which is produced by a crude method of boiling
mechanically extracted cane juice and solidifying the concentrate into molds
by cooling. There were some 83 factory units producing an estimated 12,500
tons of jaggery in 1965, mainly for fermentation into "waragi," an illicit
and medically dangerous alcoholic drink. Current production is believed to
be somewhat higher. With the establishment of a sugar factory in an area,
jaggery factories are required by the Government to cease operations.
Expansion in the Sugar Belt and Other Areas
3.        Beginning in 1966, the Government of Kenya embarked on an expan-
sion program designed to attain eventual self-sufficiency in sugar produc-
tion and, at the same time, increase income opportunities for small farmers.
Two Government majority-owned mills were established in Muhoroni (1966) and
Chemelil (1968), both in the Nyanza Province Sugar Belt, which also includes
Miwani. An additional Goverment-owned factory was commissioned in 1973, in
Mumias, Western Province, thereby opening up a new area for commercial cane-
growing.
4.        Sugarcane for mill-white sugar production is presently grown on
about 50,400 hectares, with a large portion being supplied by small and some
large-holding independent farmers. Except for Muhoroni, all of the factories
have their own nucleus estates which account for 20-33% of the factories' cane
supply; only Ramisi obtains up to 75% of its cane supply from its own nucleus
estate. The origin of total cane supply is as indicated below.

ing capacity is rated at 285,000 tons of mill-white sugar. However, the
actual production in 1975 reached only a level of about 161,000 tons, 44%
below the rated capacity of the mills. Individual capacities and utilization
rates are as follows:

7.        Plant utilization has been generally poor in most factories because
of inadequate supply of cane which has been irregular and insufficient,
mostly due to low cane yields, badly organized harvesting, and inefficient
cane transport systems and facilities. Cane in various outgrowers areas
overmatured and, at times, remained unharvested. Field management standards
are generally low and there seems to be inadequate effective encouragement
and assistance to farmers for improving cane quality and productivity. These
problems are recognized and some initial steps have been taken by various
organizations to correct them. M4umias and Chemelil, and to a much lesser
extent also Miwani, have been increasingly providing active assistance to
farmers to improve agricultural practices and have helped in organizing
systems for havesting and transporting of cane to the factories; success has
been varied. Cooperatives and the Land Settlement Organization also have
been intensifying their assistance programs for their members. However,
with the exception of Hlumias, these organizations' efforts have started from
a very low base and been greatly hampered by financial and managerial con-
straints. To coordinate the activities of all organizations concerned, and
to concentrate major responsibilities for the development of the sugar indus-
try into a single body, Government created the Kenya Sugar Authority in 1972
(Annex 3).
Recent Development and Future Plans
8.        In view of the poor performance of the existing industry and Gov-
ernment's plans for rapid expansion, the World Bank, in 1974, suggested that
a thorough study be made of the sugar industry before new project proposals
are finalized. The Government agreed to this and a study was carried out
with UNDP financial assistance, which encompassed a review of industry or-
ganization and performance and a proposal for rehabilitation of the Sugar



ANNEX 2
Page 4
Belt area. 1/ The rehabilitation project, including Miwani, Chemelil,
Muhoroni and Ramisi, would increase annual sugar production by about 70,000
tons and consist of a regional drainage component, and improvements in
agricultural practices, factory equipment, and the companies' structure and
organization. In addition to the rehabilitation project, which is given high
priority by Government, two new factories either are being proposed, i.e.
South Nyanza, or constructed, i.e. Nzoia, now in its early stage of develop-
ment with a planned capacity of about 60,000 tons. The Mumias factory, after
expansion to 76,000 tons from its previous rated capacity of 45,000 tons, is
expected to be extended further to 156,000 tons.
9.        After rehabilitation of the older factories, establishment of the
Nzoia and South Nyanza factories, and the expansion of Mumias, all of which
are scheduled to be completed within the next 5-10 years, Government plans to
implement several more projects in the following decade. These tentatively
identified future projects are in the vicinity of Busia, the Kano Plain, Tana
River, Yala Swamp, Meru and Embu. The final implementation schedule will have
to be determined on the basis of available resources, financial and economic
viability, and developments in the domestic and world markets for sugar.
