KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
EXISTING STRUCTURE AND ORGANIZATION OF THE SUGAR INDUSTRY
A. Introduction
1.        The present structure of the industry is generally characterized by
a proliferation of numerous agencies involved in various industry development
activities with an apparent lack of coordination among them. With barely ten
years since the Government's active involvement in sugar industry development
(Annex 2), the organization reflects an early stage of its evolution process
under public auspices. The differences in the organizational relationships
among the factories, nucleus estates, and outgrowers in Muhoroni, Chemelil and
Mumias illustrate the industry's search for workable arrangements to achieve
increased productivity. The existing organization of the sugar industry at
the national and company levels is shown in Chart World Bank 16460.
B. Institutions Affecting the Industry
The Ministry of Agriculture
2.        The Ministry has overall responsibility for sugar industry devel-
opment; it formulates and implements national policies and programs. Its
major instrument to carry out this mandate is the Kenya Sugar Authority
(para. 20). Through its representation on the Boards of Muhoroni, Chemelil
and Mumias, where Government has substantial investments, the Ministry of
Agriculture, together with other Government representatives, exercises man-
agement control and direction over the operations of a major part of the
industry.
3.        There are two technical divisions in the Ministry of Agriculture,
the Crops Division and the Extension and Training Division. Each province has
a Provincial Director of Agriculture with District Agricultural Officers
stationed in each district to handle agricultural extension work. At the
Ministry's farmers' training centers, the training of farmers for improving
agricultural practices covers most crops, except sugarcane. Practically no
extension services are provided directly to cane growers through the Ministry
of Agriculture although, recently, some extension staff ha're been transferred
to the Sugar Belt Cooperative Union (SBCU) by the Director of Agriculture in
Nyanza Province (para. 31). No extension services are provided to farmers
growing cane for jaggery production.
4.        In 1971, the Ministry of Agriculture established the Kibos National
Sugar Research Station in the Sugar Belt. This station has since been devot-
ing about 80% of its time to cane variety experimentation, the rest to fer-
tilizer trials and soil improvement experiments. Apart from three herbicide
trials at the main station, one ratoon stunting disease trial at Mumias, and
one nitrogen rates trial at Rodi in South Nyanza, there were no new research



ANNEX 3
Page 2
programs undertaken since 1974, reportedly because of inadequate research and
technical staff. The research methods applied are poor, and after five years
of its existence, nothing so far had been developed that would be of signifi-
cance to the sugar industry. With a present staffing of 76, of which 6 are
senior research officers with 24 technical assistants, better results could
be expected from this station with proper management and guidance (Annexes 6
and 8).
The Ministry of Finance and Economic Planning (The Treasury).
5.        This Ministry is concerned with the development strategy for the
industry as it relates to the overall national development effort, particu-
larly with respect to commitments of Government funds and foreign exchange,
and is involved in the financial planning and negotiations for new projects
to assure that project financing conforms with established fiscal policies.
It also participates in the determination of sugar prices, imposes taxes on
sugar production and approves levies on sugar imports.
The Ministry of Commerce and Industry
6.        This Ministry performs major marketing functions in the sugar sub-
sector. It monitors the local supply and demand situation, and through a
system of allocation quotas, directs the physical flow of sugar within the
country. Another major function is the importation of sugar for household
consumption to supplement local supplies. The Kenya National Trading Cor-
poration responsible to the Ministry has a monopoly in the distribution of
sugar from the source of supply to a national network of wholesale depots.
All of the Ministry's financial transactions in relation to sugar are cen-
tralized in the national sugar account. This account, like all other of the
Ministry's accounts, is administered by the Chief Accountant in the Office of
the President. It also codetermines sugar prices with the Treasury and the
Ministry of Agriculture. Details are presented in Annex 4.
The Ministry of Lands and Settlement
7.        This Ministry is responsible for adjudication surveys and regis-
tration of land rights, the acquisition of land for Government use for
residential and industrial purposes, and the acquisition of large farm units
for redistribution and settlement of landless small-scale farmers. Settlers
are chosen from a list prepared by each Provincial Commissioner's Office. In
the Sugar Belt, the Ministry, through the Sugar Settlement Organization, has
become further involved in mechanized operations to assist settlers in the
growing of cane in an effort to increase their productivity, although this is
not a responsibility of the Ministry (para. 30). This involvement which has
not proven very successful, was prompted by the inability of the Ministry of
Agriculture and the Muhoroni factory organization to provide the services
required by farmers.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
The Ministry of Cooperatives and Social Services
8.        This Ministry is responsible for administering a wide range of
programs among which are cooperatives and community development, vocational
rehabilitation, adult education and training. The development of primary
cooperative societies is supported through training programs, management
advice, and financial assistance under the Cooperative Production Credit
Scheme. The Ministry operates the Cooperative College of Kenya where, to
date, more than 3,000 staff of cooperative societies have been trained in
administration, bookkeeping and management. In the sugar industry, the
Ministry has played an important role in the organization of primary coop-
erative societies and a cooperative union in the Sugar Belt. It also
granted two loans to smallholders in the Chemelil area, totaling KShs. 10
million. The most recent loan in 1974-75, KShs. 5 million at 3.5% interest
per year with a two-year moratorium on principal and interest, was for the
rehabilitation of about 3,600 ha of sugarcane; the program was reduced to
2,400 ha due to inflation.
The Sugar Belt Cooperative Union (SBCU)
9.        Cooperatives have a relatively long history in the Sugar Belt, with
the initial primary societies being primarily marketing and farm purchase
societies. Cooperative production societies were established in 1965 in
preparation for the then planned Chemelil factory as a new approach to or-
ganizing outgrowers production; this was in contrast to the Sugar Settlement
Organization at Muhoroni which was already experiencing difficulties at that
time. SBCU was established in 1971 to coordinate the activities of the
various primary societies. There are presently 35 societies affiliated with
SBCU with about 8,500 members in the Miwani, Chemelil and Muhoroni factory
zones. Of these 35 societies, 11 are production, 7 farm purchase and 17
marketing societies.
10.       When the first primary production societies were organized, the
members were not properly trained and there was a lack of understanding of,
members' responsibility in the society. The farmers received no assistance
or guidance. With the establishment of the SBCU, the Government hoped to
have a more organized approach to farmers' assistance in cane growing. The
Friedrich-Ebert-Stiftung (FES), a German organization, has been providing
technical assistance to SBCU and currently is funding about one-half of its
annual cost of operations.
11.       A re-zoning of the factories in the Sugar Belt was made in 1972 and
areas from Miwani and Muhoroni allocated to Chemelil, necessitating the devel-
opment of new areas for cane growing to supply the underutilized i4uhoroni and
Miwani factories. A new loan of KShs. 11.7 million, interest-free with no
definite repayment schedule, made available by the Ministry of Finance and
Economic Planning through the Ministry of Agriculture, was channeled through
the SBCU for the development of about 2,740 hectares in the Miwani and Muho-
roni zones. SBCU has been onlending these funds at 7% interest a year with



ANNEX 3
Page 4
a 2-year moratorium on principal and interest. In its initial loan opera-
tions, SBCU was not able to maintain adequate accounting control and subse-
quently, when permitted to use part of the loan funds to cover operational
costs, hiired additional staff for proper loan administration. A new program
was added and, through the Cooperative Bank of Kenya, small-scale farmers
obtained a total of KShs. 1.8 million under the Cooperative Production Credit
Scheme (CPCS). W.[hile these loans bear 10% interest per annum and are payable
in full at harvest time, SBCU's recovery experience has been relatively
successful, with KShs. 325,000 repaid as of December 31, 1975 of an original
loan of KShs. 890,000 disbursed in 1974.
12.       Besides acting as the vehicle for the channeling of Government
funds for development and rehabilitation of small-scale farms of members,
SBCU, with the assistance of FES, has been instrumental in obtaining workable
arrangements with the Chemelil factory on behalf of its societies and members.
A joint Chemelil-SBCU operating committee has been established to identify
land for new cane planting, plan and supervise cultivation procedures, and
for the coordination and harvesting and transport of cane. Technical staff
from the tinistry of Agriculture have also been provided to SBCU for the
purpose of supervising field operations of farmers. In support of these
activities, SBCU owns and operates farm and transport equipment to service
member societies.  In 1975, it acquired 5 Same Corsaro and 4 Saturno tractors
which completed cultivation of a monthly average of 234 hectares from August
to December 1975, and transported 25,400 tons out of a total of 73,237 tons of
cane produced by the marketing societies.
13.       Besides the involvement in the agricultural program, SBCU has ex-
panded its activities to include education, social welfare, and the initia-
tion of a banking section which operates as a savings and loan setup. After
starting with 3 society memberships in early 1975, the program has grown to
9 societies by December 1975.  Members' accounts are credited for proceeds
from the sale of sugarcarne to the factories; instead of making direct cash
payments to members.  These deposits amrounted to KShs. 1.7 million for 1,399
members in 1975, of which KShs. 262,500 or 15% remained credited as savings
deposits as at December 31, 1975, earning 5% annual interest. About 80% of
savings deposits generated can be loaned to members at 10% annual interest
~,c r  se` in the repurchase of CPCS notes.
The National Trrigation Board (NIB)
14.       Several NIB-sponsored irrigation schemes for cane-growing are pres-
ently operating oll a  experimental scale.  While there are only 10 hectares
of experimental plots, about 30 hectares of irrigated cane has been grown
commercially under a tenant farmer scheme near Miwani.  Yields of up to 120
t/ha of plant cane, harvested after 14 months, have been achieved; ratoon
cane was harvested after 12 months with yields averaging 80% of the plant
crop.  With these encouraging results, irrigation of sugarcane could become
more impportant In the future.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
Banking Institutions
15.       The segment of the banking industry which plays a significant role
in Kenya's sugar industry development includes the Agricultural Development
Corporation (ADC), the Agricultural Finance Corporation (AFC), the Cooperative
Bank of Kenya and the Development Finance Company of Kenya Limited (DFCK).
These institutions have been responsible for meeting part of the equity
and/or loan financing requirements of some of the sugar mills and of farmers.
In addition, private banking institutions are becoming increasingly important
as a source of funds.
16.       Agricultural Development Corporation (ADC).  Financed from grants
and loans received from the Ministry of Agriculture, this Government-owned
development bank has made investments in Chemelil Sugar Company, Ltd.,
amounting to KShs. 151 million as of March 31, 1976, of which KShs. 94.4
million is in equity, the rest in loans.  ADC's investments in East African
Sugar Industries (Muhoroni) amount to KShs. 19.5 million for 700,000 of its
outstanding shares.
17.       Agricultural Finance Corporation (AFC).  Another Government-owned
company, AFC has no equity investments in sugar factories but is a source of
financing for outgrowers. It lends to sugarcane farmers at 9% interest up
to KShs. 3,700 per hectare payable over a period of 2 years to cover part of
farmers' cash inputs for cane growing. This amount generally exceeds the
value of the land used as collateral which is estimated between KShs. 1,235 and
KShs. 1,483 in Nyanza. AFC's experience indicates that it is much easier
to administer loans to large-scale farmers than to smallholders, with a
consequent bias of lending operations in favor of large farms. AFC also
finances up to 50% of the value of farm and transport equipment. These loans
are at 9% interest per annum, payable semi-annually over 3 years.
18.       Development Finance Company of Kenya Limited (DFCK).  This develop-
ment finance company is owned partly by the Government through the Industrial
and Commercial Development Corporation (ICDC). The other stockholders owning
equal shares as ICDC are the Commonwealth Development Corporation, German
Development Company, Ltd., and the Netherlands Finance Company for Developing
Countries, Ltd. DFCK invests in the establishment and/or expansion of manu-
facturing and agricultural processing enterprises and has made equity invest-
ment in Chemelil Sugar Company. Largely because Chemelil has not produced a
profit after more than 7 years of operation, DFCK did not subscribe to Mumias
shares when invited.
19.       The Cooperative Bank of Kenya, Ltd.  The Cooperative Bank of Kenya,
Ltd., acts as a banker to the cooperative movement. Besides operating as a
channel through which public funds are made available for cooperative devel-
opment, the Bank also assists the movement by using funds generated from
within. The Cooperative Production Credit Scheme in which the bank is in-
volved, is a means of providing credit and saving facilities for members of
agricultural cooperatives; the average loan is about KShs. 200 per farmer,
and the annual number of loans is expected to rise from 25,000 in 1972 to
100,000 in 1978.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
C. Industry Organization at the National Level
The Kenya Sugar Authority (KSA)
20.       Recognizing the need to support the development of the sugar indus-
try, Government, in 1972, created the Kenya Sugar Authority as a statutory
board under Cap 318, Section 190 of the Agricultural Act. According to the
Act the Authority's general duties are "to promote the development of the
sugar industry in the most efficient and economical manner possible, and in
particular, to promote and foster practices which enhance the efficiency of
the production, processing and marketing of sugarcane, sugar in all its
forms, and molasses." In the same Act sugarcane, sugar and molasses were
declared as special produce, thus becoming subject to price regulation by
Government. To implement the provisions of the Act, an Interim Sugar Author-
ity was created in April 1972 with the appointment of the Provincial Commis-
sioner of Nyanza as chairman, and the Permanent Secretaries of the Ministries
of Agriculture and Finance, the Provincial Commissioner of Western Province,
and representatives of farmers and factories as members. The conduct of
KSA's business was made the responsibility of a Chief Executive Officer.
21.       The purpose of the Interim Sugar Authority was defined, specif-
ically, to advise the Government on all aspects of the industry covering,
among other aspects:
(a) sugarcane production, planting material, credit and infra-
structure for growers;
(b) cane harvesting and transport to factories;
(c) growing of cane supplied to different factories;
(d) factory development and improvements;
(e) sugarcane processing and the utilization of by-products;
(f) sugarcane quality control and improvements;
(g) cesses to be raised from all parts of the industry;
(h) the price of sugarcane to growers and the price of sugar
to millers;
(i) legislation required by the sugar industry;
(j) staff requirements of the industry in general and of the
Authority in particular, and the remuneration of such staff;
(k) specialist committees of the Authority, their need, admin-
istration and supervision; and



ANNEX 3
Page 7
(1) the development of the industry in general and other
matters related thereto.
22.       Shortly after the members of KSA were appointed, committees were
organized for the purpose of making proposals to the Minister on the fore-
going aspects of the industry. Headed by the Provincial Commissioner of
Nyanza Province, these committees consisted of farmers, representatives of
cooperatives and settlement schemes, factory managers, provincial adminis-
tration officials, and the technical staff of the Ministry of Agriculture
in Nyanza Province. The report of these committees, submitted to the Min-
ister of Agriculture in late July 1972 included detailed recommendations on
a wider role of KSA in industry development, its organization and staffing,
and proposed legislation granting certain powers to the Authority to act on
behalf of the Minister of Agriculture within the terms of the Agriculture
Act. In Legal Notice No. 32, issued on February 15, 1973, however, the Min-
ister decided to maintain the KSA as an advisory body only, and the interim
arrangement became, in effect, permanent.
23.       Staffed with the Chief Executive Officer, an Economist/Statistician
and an Executive Officer, besides the ten members who jointly act similar to
a company Board, the KSA has made studies on zoning in the Sugar Belt and
reviewed a number of other issues and problems. KSA is presently involved in
appraising schemes for the development and extension of production areas and
in preparing studies on cane pricing; it submits its recommendations to the
Minister as required. Even as an advisory body, the KSA, with its present
staffing, could not be expected to undertake the comprehensive studies that
are needed as a basis for Government decisions. In the past, it relied on ad
hoc committees organized occasionally to investigate specific problems, such
as the Ad Hoc Committee on Sugarcane Transport within the Nyanza Sugar Belt
which investigated the cane transport problems in early 1975 (para. 36).
24.       There are a host of problems that the industry is faced with and
which need national Government attention, appropriately through the KSA.
Some examples of these are underutilization of four existing factories, low
productivity and cane quality in four factory zones, inefficient procurement
of materials and equipment, inadequate market outlets for molasses, and
inefficient sugar distribution. These problems, in turn, are related to
inadequate financial and technical assistance to smallholder farmers, mostly
in the Nyanza Sugar Belt area, resulting from poor organization of institu-
tions serving the farmers, inadequate financial resources of four existing
factories to undertake rehabilitation or improvments, slow progress in the
sugar research effort, lack of qualified manpower, and ineffective planning
throughout the industry. The lack of a single agency which could effectively
implement projects and programs is a serious shortcoming as the Government's
efforts are dissipated over individual agency assistance programs which are
invariably hampered by loose coordination and inefficiency. KSA would be a
more effective agency if it were able to advise the industry as well as the
Government and empowered with proper delegation of authority and sufficient
resources, to coordinate and implement development activities in the industry
(Annex 17).



ANNEX 3
Page 8
D. Organization at the Company Level
Factories and Nucleus Estates
25.       East African Sugar Industries, Ltd., at M4uhoroni is managed by The
Mehta Group, Ltd., who own 16% of shareholdings in the company. The majority
shareholdings are held by the Government through ADC (para. 16). The factory
has a crushing capacity of 420,000 tons of cane over a period of 300 milling
days, expandable to 600,000 t, and presently employs 550 permanent workers of
which 17 are expatriates. It does not have its own nucleus estate, although
the company is presently in the process of acquiring the Nyando estate with
a total area of 2,024 hectares from which it already derives 20% of its cane
supply. The rest of the cane supply comes from 3,000 farmers in the Sugar
Settlement Scheme comprising 5,700 hectares (para. 30), farmers not affiliated
with any scheme controlling 2,000 hectares and an SBCU-sponsored area of 600
hectares. The company does not have its own agricultural department nor does
it maintain farm machinery or transport equipment-to assist farmers in land
preparation, cultivation and cane transport and, consequently has no direct
control over its outgrowers. The already serious problem of inadequate cane
supply was aggravated by the re-zoning in 1972 when part of Muhoroni's catch-
ment area for cane was allocated to Chemelil. Government sought to remedy the
situation, temporarily by diverting back to Muhoroni some cane from Chemelil,
and permanently through loans for the development of new areas (para. 11).
There is a need for the Muhoroni company to assume responsibility for ren-
dering services to its farmers.
26.       The Chemelil Sugar Company, Ltd., has been managed since 1969 by
Bookers Agricultural and Technical Services, Ltd., who do not own any equity
interest in the company. The Government owns 97% of the shares through the
Agricultural Development Corporation (para. 16) with the remaining shares
being held mainly by DFCK. The factory has a crushing capacity of about
600,000 tons of cane a year over a 300-day milling period. The company owns
a nucleus estate with an area of 3,240 hectares, which is about 22% of the
Chemelil zone's cane area of 14,170 hectares. About 14% of the total area is
farmed by large-holding, independent farmers with farm sizes ranging from 40
- 1,600 hectares. Smallholder farmers, who are members of 11 primary socie-
ties affiliated with SBCU, account for about 3,800 hectares and the remaining
area is farmed by smallholder farmers who are not members of a cooperative
society. The company presently employs about 1,300 people; its expatriate
staff of 14 was expected to be reduced to 8 by the end of 1976. An agricul-
tural department manages the nucleus estate and, to a limited extent, provides
services to outgrowers; about 800 hectares were ploughed for cane growing in
outgrower areas. The company's transport fleet has recently been expanded.
Chemelil had been incurring losses since it started operations, primarily
because it had been operating below its capacity. In 1975, plant utilization
was only 62%, with a total of 413,600 tons of cane crushed.
27.       The Mumias Sugar Company, Ltd., which is also managed by Bookers
Agricultural and Technical Services, Ltd., is the newest company majority-



ANNEX 3
Page 9
owned by Government. Other investors include East African Development Bank,
Kenya Commercial Bank, Commonwealth Development Corporation and Booker
McConnell. With an initial cane crushing capacity of 450,000 tons a year,
the factory expanded to a capacity of 700,000 tons of cane a year and has
plans to double the latter as soon as increased cane production warrants it.
The nucleus estate of about 4,450 hectares gross area and 3,240 hectares cane
area was fully planted by the end of 1975. At that time, there were also
5,100 smallholder farmers under contract with the company growing about 7,800
hectares of cane. With the recent expansion, additional areas are being
brought into cultivation, increasing the total area to 9,715 hectares and
the number of outgrowers to 6,000. An agricultural department manages the
nucleus estate, renders extension services to the outgrowers and operates a
fleet of farm machinery and transport equipment for land preparation, cultiva-
tion and cane transport. With a staff of 3,000, of which 25 are expatriates
(50 at the time of commissioning of the factory) Mumias has been able to
develop on target. In 1975, it hit a record production of 59,200 tons of
sugar, exceeding its rated pre-expansion capacity and earning pre-tax profits
of KShs. 26 million.
28.       Miwani and Ramisi are privately-owned and managed companies.  Miwani
is presently producing about 28,000 tons of sugar a year which is only 47% of
its capacity. Its nucleus estate totals 3,240 hectares and accounts for one-
third of its cane supply. The Company employs 1,400 permanent staff of which
8 are expatriates. It has an agricultural department for its own nucleus es-
tate operations but does not service its outgrowers. The Ramisi company has
an estate of about 5,000 ha gross area which supplies approximately 100,000 t
cane/year; outgrowers produce 18,000 t/year.
Outgrowers Production
29.       Of the total cane area of about 50,000 ha, 34,000 ha are farmed by
outgrowers, 20,600 ha of which by small-scale farmers; 16,000 ha are estate
land. The large-scale farmers, especially in the Chemelil zone, with farms
ranging from 40 - 1,600 ha, are generally equipped to handle their own land
preparation, cultivation, harvesting and transport. Smallholders, however,
generally do not have the know-how or the facilities to undertake cane
growing practices of acceptable standards.
30.       The Government realized outgrowers' difficulties and when it was
decided to devote part of the settlement area to grow cane for supplying the
Muhoroni factory, the Sugar Settlement Scheme was organized to service the
farmers. Established in 1964, the Scheme involved about 3,000 farmers with
an average holding of about 4 hectares each, a little over one-half of which
was planted to cane. To assist farmers in land preparation, the Sugar Set-
tlement Organization, which manages the Scheme, acquired farm machinery and
operated a tractor pool. Because of management and financial problems, the
operation of this tractor pool was discontinued a few years later. Cultiva-
tion is presently handled by private contractors through arrangements made
by the Settlement Organization. Because of basic inefficiencies in schedul-
ing and cultivation of scattered small plots and higher operational costs



ANNEX 3
Page 10
resulting from inflation, contractors' rates are high; e.g., the rates for
ploughing, harrowing and ridging more than doubled in 1976 (from KShs. 1,480/
ha to KShs. 3,460/ha). These rates are considered by the Sugar Settlement
Organization to be excessive in relation to the farmers' sugarcane output
and acquisition of agricultural machinery and equipment is again being con-
templated. Overall, the role of the Sugar Settlement Scheme in sugarcane
production has not been very successful. The start of actual operations of
the Muhoroni factory suffered delays, causing delays in the harvesting of
cane to the detriment of the farmers who were unable to service their produc-
tion credits. There were also initial problems with the transport of cane,
resulting further in unharvested and overmatured cane. These factors may
have contributed to an adverse attitude of farmers in the Settlement Scheme,
only half of whom actually occupy and farm the land originally allocated to
them. Income levels are reportedly low and repayment of credits to the Land
Settlement Organization in arrears; losses from default on credit are expected
to be written off.
31.       In response to the unfavorable experience at Muhoroni, the Chemelil
company assumed initial responsibility for outgrowers when the factory was
established. Cooperative production societies were formed under the super-
vision of the company's agricultural department with a loan from the Ministry
of Cooperatives and Social Services (para. 8). Complications quickly devel-
oped. During the early stages of Chemelil's operations, the company went
through a series of changes in managing agents and there was a lack of con-
trol over the agricultural operations. There were also delays in the actual
start-up of factory operations so that farmers suffered severe losses of
income from their cane, particularly as their debt service was based on a
rigid schedule of payments per hectare of cane. Consequently, farmers were
discouraged and most of the cane fields reverted to fallow. In 1971, small-
holder responsibility at Chemelil was transferred to SBCU. The SBCU, with
3,800 ha of smallholder land in this area, made progress in increasing its
assistance to members and gradually became active in all three Sugar Belt
zones. Extension services, credit, land preparation, cultivation, harvesting
and transport assistance have been organized or provided directly by SBCU.
Even with external assistance (para. 10), however, SBCU has not been able
to meet farmers' requirement due to financial and management constraints.
Arrangements to use extension staff made available by the Ministry of Agri-
culture have not been very successful because of morale problems. To many
farmers, their share of the burden of KShs. 5 per ton to the Society and KShs.
1 per ton to the Union, when added to other services of KShs. 4 per ton for
harvesting, and KShs. 23 per ton for transport, leaves KShs. 72 per ton out of
gross proceeds of KShs. 105 per ton under 1976 prices. The farmer still has
to pay for cultivation credits thus further reducing his income. According
to Chemelil management, the typical outgrower in the Nyanza Sugar Belt nets
as much profit per hectare per year using low input-output practices as
the nucleus estates using high input-output techniques; Government's new price
policy will greatly reduce this problem in 1977 (Annex 4). With the impending
phase-out by 1978 of external assistance, the cooperative effort will be
severely handicapped as the extra burden on farmers will again reduce their
incentive.



ANNEX 3
Page 11
32.       All of Government's efforts in the Sugar Belt, whether it was
through the Sugar Settlemnt Organization or the SBCU, or through the medium
of cane pricing adjustments, were designed to provide the farmer with suf-
ficient income from his land by economically producing cane that could
adequately supply the factories in the area. This objective has not been
fully achieved and it is imperative that in the future, an organization be
established which can effectively and efficiently assit the farmer at a cost
that would bring him returns sufficiently attractive for him to continue to
grow and care for his cane. As no subsidies are envisaged, this objective can
only be achieved if the services are provided to farmers on a cost-effective
basis and full account is taken of all costs of production and transport in
the adjustment of cane prices. This was the thrust of outgrowers' cane
development at Mumias which now has about 7,800 ha farmed by 5,100 smallholders
in addition to its own nucleus estate. From the beginning, the company had
been providing land preparation services, selected treated seed cane, fertili-
zers on credit, and the technical guidance throughout the life of the crop.
The farmers are under individual contract with the company which also provides
harvesting and transport services. With this arrangement and satisfactory
farmer cooperation, cane yields have reportedly been averaging over 91 t per
ha per crop, resulting in farm incomes of over KShs. 3,950 per ha, net of all
costs of services and materials provided by the company. With this mode of
operation successfully established, a separate outgrowers company is now being
formed which will reduce the burden on the sugar company without completely
divorcing it from its outgrowers source of cane supply. A similar pattern is
anticipated for South Nyanza (Annex 18).
Harvesting of Sugarcane
33.       The climatic conditions in most cane growing areas in Kenya give
rise to prolonged growing periods ranging from 20 to 22 months for plant cane
(12 months at Ramisi) and from 16 to 18 months for ratoon cane to reach
maturity. A cycle of 1 plant and 3 ratoon crops is common at Ramisi and is
expected in Mumias. In the Sugar Belt, although fourth ratoons have been
recorded in most areas, a cyle of 1 plant and 2 ratoon crops is preferable.
Harvesting of cane is carried out throughout the year except for short periods
when the factories shut down for annual repairs for a period of about 2 to 6
weeks during the month of May or June. Cane is cut by hand and the farmers
arrange for these services either themselves (Muhoroni, Miwani) or with the
assistance of the factory (Mumias and Chemelil). SBCU also provides these
services to its member societies. The cane cutters are paid about KShs. 8/t;
a cane cutter can cut up to 2 t per day. In the case of burnt cane, the rate
is KShs. 4/t as each cutter can cut up to 4 t per day because of the absence
of trash. However, the measurement of weights at the field is generally a
matter of guesswork. Cane is piled in 6-8 t heaps.
34.       In the Sugar Belt, harvesting schedules are generally not properly
planned and coordinated with the factories, except recently at Chemelil after
a Chemelil/SBCU committee was created for this purpose. In Mumias, harvest-
ing follows a pre-determined schedule. Problems arise when harvesting is
unscheduled such as when a fire occurs and burnt cane has to be cut, trans-
ported and milled within 24 hours. The frequency of cane fires in the Nyanza
Sugar Belt area, especially during the dry months, has been increasing. In
1974, about 2,120 ha of cane in Miwani, Hluhoroni and Chemelil were reportedly



ANNEX 3
Page 12
damaged by fire; 1,362 ha were damaged during the first three months of 1975.
Organized prevention and control measures are necessary to minimize losses,
especially of immature cane. Despite the considerable risks, supervised
burning of cane in outgrower areas would be beneficial to farmers due to
reduced harvesting costs and should be investigated (Annex 18).
Transport of Sugarcane
35.       Cane is transported from the fields to the factory mostly with
tractor-trailer systems or trucks. Part of Miwani's harvest is hauled by
rail. While the large-holding farmers in the Sugar Belt generally have their
own transport equipment, the small-holding farmers rely partly on the factory
or SBCU, the Sugar Settlement Organization, or private transporters. SBCU
can transport only 35% of the cane production of its member societies. The
farmer is charged on the basis of weight and distance; KShs. 21/t for up to
10 km; KShs. 23/t from 11-16 km; KShs. 26/t from 17-24 km, and KShs. 28/t
above 24 km. In the Miwani/Chemelil zones, a tractor/trailer with a 6 t load
averages 3 trips per working day of 14 hours and an average radius of 16 km.
Loading of the trailers in the fields normally takes about half an hour,
while the waiting time at the factory ranges from 1-2 hours. A driver who is
paid KShs. 16 per day with a bonus of KShs. 3 per trip in excess of 3 trips,
is assisted by about 6 helpers for loading. These 6 helpers are paid a total
of KShs. 3/t loaded.
36.       The main problem in the Sugar Belt area has been the organization
of the use of transport facilities. In February 1975, the KSA appointed an
"Ad Hoc Committee on Sugarcane Transport" to investigate the cane transport
situation in the Sugar Belt. This Committee consisted of representatives
of the Chemelil, Miwani and Muhoroni factories, the Sugar Settlement Organ-
ization, SBCU, and the Muhoroni Sugarcane FCU Ltd. (since then dissolved).
In a report submitted in July 1975, this committee stated that there were
a total of 331 cane transport vehicles operating in the Sugar Belt as shown
below:
Cane Transport Vehicles Operating in the Sugar Belt,
July 1975
Tractor/Trailer   Lorry        Total
Chemelil Estate          22             5           27
Miwani                    10             -          10
Muhoroni                   -             -           _
Nyando Estate             -              6           6
Sugar Settlement          -             -            -
Organization            8                          8
SBCU                     11             -           11
All Others               152          117          269
Total              203           128          331



ANNEX 3
Page 13
Most of the vehicles were owned by independent farmers and by individuals or
partnerships in the transport business. In Miwani, there was found to be a
complete lack of factory/outgrowers coordination or any form of control of
transport within the zone. Each transporter functioned individually as
requested by outgrowers. Because of insufficient supply of cane, Miwani
accepts whatever cane is delivered to the factory for crushing. In Chemelil,
the factory and the SBCU exercised some control over cane transport. The
private business transporters entered into a contractual arrangement to
transport cane from a specified area within a specified time. Farmers with
their own transport facilities also obtain allocated quotas to supply the
factory with cane as determined by a milling committee. In Muhoroni, factory
management is beginning to be involved in determining harvesting and trans-
porting schedules with SBCU which owns transport facilities and has affili-
ated private transporters operating within the Sugar Settlement Organization
together with Government vehicles. Muhoroni farmers have had particular
problems with private transport contractors who cannot be relied on to ser-
vice all growers in less favorable locations.
37.       At an estimated sugarcane production of about 930,000 t in the
Sugar Belt in 1975 and an average daily feed to the mills of some 3,100 t,
assuming a milling period of 300 days/year, 258 units would have been theo-
retically required (2 daily trips of a 6 t tractor/trailer unit) to carry
the cane. Even allowing for out-of-service units, the existing transport
fleet should have been able to provide timely service with proper schedul-
ing. The transport problems in the Sugar Belt will likely continue unless
remedial steps are taken. With the cost of transporting farmer's cane
already absorbing 25% of the factory gate price of cane, growing inefficiency
in transportation could further reduce the farmer's margin. There is a need
for an efficiently managed organization in each factory zone to handle the
cane transport needs economically and to assure that cane supply requirements
of factories are met. The ownership and operation of the transportation
units by the factory, as is the case at Mumias, would assure standardization
of units and contribute to the factory's efficiency in unloading operations
at the caneyards.
Manpower Development
38.       The three Government majority-owned sugar factories are all managed
by managing agents engaged by the Government (para. 25 to 27). In each of
the existing factories, including the private ones, the number of expatriate
personnel handling key positions range from 8 to 25. These are clear indica-
tions of a dearth in management and technical talent with practical sugar
industry experience in the country. To bridge this gap, the managing agents
are committed to develop local personnel in their factories to gradually
assume positions presently occupied by expatriates. Each of these factories
have developed their own programs for training of staff and have been grad-
ually reducing the number of expatriate personnel originally employed.
39.       The training program at Muhoroni is classified into four cate-
gories, namely, training on the job for operators and attendants, "in-house"



ANNEX 3
Page 14
supervisory training, apprenticeship and skill improvement and management
courses. Training on the job does not involve classroom instruction. This
is actual instruction given the operator on how to operate the machine or
equipment he is assigned to. The employee is already hired and receives
regular wages but is placed on a probationary basis from 2 to 6 months while
undergoing training. The supervisory training includes subjects on job
safety, job relations, job methods, and job instructions and is held twice a
year, with 2-hour sessions for a whole week. Separate sessions are held for
foremen, headmen and overseers. Apprenticeship and skill improvement train-
ing is for craftsmen welders, electricians, etc., at an Industrial Training
Center, and for technicians at the Kenya Polytechnic. Industrial Training
Centers, located in Kisumu, Niairobi and Mombasa, are vocational training
centers operated by the Ministry of Labor. As many as 160 trainees can be
accommodated at any given time. The Kenya Polytechnic is a more advanced
form of training in science or engineering subjects. This school has an
annual enrollment of about 2,800 students. The costs of sending trainees
to these programs are recoverable from the training levy of KShs. 2/ton of
sugar. The management courses are 2 - 3 day seminars held at least once a
year for managers. These courses are organized by the Federation of Kenya
Employees, a private organization. The subjects covered in these seminars
include personnel, production and financial management.
40.       The manpower development programs at Chemelil and Mumias are ap-
parently better organized than at Muhoroni. Their programs are based on a
periodic analysis and review of training needs encompassing all departments
from managerial levels down to the headmen and drivers. The requirements for
training are determined also for individuals as they are hired, which con-
sider their specific backgrounds and experience, in relation to the positions
for which they are hired. The recruitment process, therefore, plays an
important role in the direction and planning of the training programs.
41.       There are several types of programs of training carried out at
Mumias by two full-time training specialists in addition to the operational
managers who also hold specialization courses for employees in their respec-
tive departments. Eight Kenyan managers have been sent so far for further
training in UK, Australia or Managa in Swaziland. Graduate management
trainees are also attending practical engineering courses at the East African
Railways in Nairobi. Formal training is arranged for some employees, using
the facilities at the Kenya Polytechnic. Seven employees are presently
attending courses there and four more were scheduled in 1976. There are also
40 apprentices in various trades enrolled at Kisumu, Nairobi, or Mombasa
Industrial Training Centers.
42.       The experience of Mumias when it started recruiting staff in 1973
gives an indication of the availability of qualified candidates and the
nature and extent of training required. For the agricultural operations,
1iumias had no difficulty recruiting adequate field staff up to and including
supervisor level. However, diploma or university graduates with management
potential to start at salary levels for 'the field staff could not be engaged



ANNEX 3
Page 15
because salaries were considered by the applicants to be too low. Tractor
drivers were readily available but transport assistants were not. Factory
staff for processing and laboratory were available except pan boilers and
supervisors. The engineering positions were difficult to fill because of
non-competitive salary levels. Well-trained staff for the accounting posi-
tions were difficult to find and the company resorted to taking in bright
and trainable applicants.
43.       Farmers' training is conducted in Farmers Training Centers of the
Ministry of Agriculture, although subjects pertaining to sugarcane cultiva-
tion are seriously neglected. In Nyanza Province, such centers are
located in Kisii, Maseno and Homa Bay.

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
MARKETING AND PRICING OF SUGAR
A. Distribution
1.        The physical distribution of sugar to regional wholesale depots is
the responsibility of the Kenya National Trading Corporation (KNTC); local
distribution is in the hands of the private trade. Established in 1965, KNTC
is one of a number of commercial institutions which are wholly-owned by the
Industrial and Commercial Development Corporation (ICDC) under the Ministry of
Commerce and Industry. At the beginning, sugar was the only commodity traded
by RITC, but its current trading operations extend to about 50 items, includ-
ing salt, edible oils, rice, textiles, cement, etc. Despite this expansion,
sugar still accounted for about 76% of KNTC's total sales volume in 1975.
KNTC is reimbursed for its expenses in the form of a fixed commission related
to the volume traded (para. 15).
2.        The Ministry of Commerce and Industry is responsible for estimating
sugar requirements, procuring sugar locally and through import, allocating of
sugar supplies, and enforcing regulations governing the marketing of sugar.
The Chief Accountant of the Office of the President, who handles all of the
Ministry's accounts, carries out all financial transactions related to KNTC's
trading activities and maintains the national sugar account. The national
sugar account is a central fund into which all proceeds from the sale of
sugar to wholesalers by KNTC are deposited, and from which all sugar payments
for purchase, handling, and distribution to KNTC warehouses are made.
3.        Each month, the Ministry determines the amount of sugar to be
shipped by each factory to each of KNTC's 18 depots. This monthly provisional
allocation order which is sent around the middle of the month preceding ship-
ments, is determined on the basis of stocks on hand reported by the KNTC
depots, and the estimated consumption for the forthcoming month. The order
contains instructions for shipment, usually by rail, with the freight charges
payable from the sugar account; copies are sent to the Chief Statistician,
East African Statistics Department; Chief Accountant of the Office of the
President; General Manger of KNTC; Chief Traffic Manager, East African Rail-
ways; and the station master of the destination station. The provisional
allocations are frequently changed and the factories informed mainly by
telephoned instructions. These changes, which are made in close cooperation
with KNTC in Nairobi, have occasionally caused difficulties for the factories
as their limited storage capacity has threatened to halt factory operations
when sugar had to be stored longer than expected. As soon as the sugar, in
bags of 100 kilograms, is received by the various KNTC depots, KNTC head-
quarters notifies the Ministry of Commerce and Industry which instructs the
Chief Accountant in the Office of the President to make payments to the
factories. KNTC depots sell on a cash basis to 353 private wholesalers
throughout the country who are authorized by the Provincial Trade Development
Officer of the Ministry of Commerce and Industry. The proceeds are credited



ANNEX 4
Page 2
to a local subaccount of the national sugar account. Wholesalers sell sugar
to retailers without control on a customer basis. KNTC has no information on
these retailers and does not monitor retailers' stock movements.
4.        The Ministry of Commerce and Industry has a monopoly on sugar
imports for household consumption. Sugar required by industry for beverage
production, canning, etc., is imported directly by the industrial users;
these industries generally require highly refined sugar not produced in
Kenya. Imported sugar for household consumption is channeled through the
Kenya Farmers' Association (Coop), Ltd., in Mombasa who act as a wholesale
distributor to KNTC. To minimize transport costs, imported sugar is sold
primarily in the Mombasa area. Outside Mombasa, preference in the shipment
of refined import sugar is given to areas serving the tourist industry.
Although Kenya produces only mill-white sugar, it imports refined sugar for
household consumption as well as industrial use as mill-white sugar is not
traded internationally and domestic refining of imported raw sugar is com-
plicated by the lack of refining equipment of most local sugar factories and
the locational disadvantage of the factories.
5.        The financial results of KNTC in 1974 and 1975 were satisfactory.
KNTC's net assets increased to KShs. 12.6 million, or by 31.5%, in 1975;
profits after taxes rose by 7.1% to KShs. 3.6 million. Gross profits from
its trading account amounted to KShs. 17.0 million in 1975, or 21.7% above
the 1974 level. Sugar accounted for 76% of total sales in 1975 and contrib-
uted 69% to gross trading profits net of commission charges. KNTC's finan-
cial statements for the fiscal years 1974 and 1975 are presented in Table 1.
6.        The distribution system through KNTC has advantages and disadvan-
tages. The fact that KNTC is a specialized multi-commodity trading agency
with an established infrastructure is definitely an advantage. On the
negative side are such factors as the lack of "active selling," KNTC's
organizational distance from the sugar industry and occasionally poor and
inefficient execution by KNTC and the Ministry of Commerce and Industry of
their assigned functions. Since KNTC is merely a commission agent in the
system it does not have sufficient incentives to strive for maximum effi-
ciency. The Ministry of Commerce and Industry, on the other hand, is not
properly equipped to carry out its functions as the nerve center of the
system. The Ministry lacks the capability for basic market research and
analysis for medium and long-term planning purposes, and for monitoring
nation-wide the current supply/demand and stock balances as a basis for
allocating sugar on a monthly basis. Organizing timely transport of sugar
to all locations in the country is a recurrent problem. The sugar account
is not kept current enough to serve as a management tool. However, a major
reorganization of the marketing system at this time is not warranted as the
alternatives are not sufficiently superior, a change would be costly, and
there is room for raising efficiency under the present system. Government
would undertake to address the problems, which are recognized, and imple-
ment remedial measures; a plan for improvement, covering at least the areas of
sugar market research and analysis, domestic sugar allocation, sugar transport,
storage, and the national sugar account, would be submitted to the Bank by
December 31, 1978. KSA would be able to assist in this effort and provide
partial financing (Annex 17, para. 26).



ANNEX 4
Page 3
B. The Market for Sugar
The Period 1955-75
7.        Production.  The production of sugar in Kenya reached a record high
of 164,000 t in 1974, slightly above the 1975 output of 161,000 t. The expan-
sion from a total production level of 17,000 t in 1955 occurred in steps coin-
ciding with the establishment of new factories, i.e., Muhoroni and Chemelil in
1967-70 and Mumias in 1973. In 1975, the three Sugar Belt companies produced
59% of the country's sugar output, Mumias 35% and Ramisi 6%.
8.        Consumption.  Sugar consumption is estimated to have increased from
55,000 t in 1955 to 225,000 t in 1974. Over the same period per capita
consumption rose from 7.9 kg/year to 17.6 kg/year, and total population from
7.0 million to 12.8 million. In 1975, per capita consumption fell to 14.6 kg
and total consumption to 194,000 t due to sharp increases in prices (para.
14). The increase in sugar consumption over the past two decades was gradual,
and the drop in 1975 was the only significant interruption of this trend. In
comparison, per capita consumption elsewhere in 1972/73 was as follows:
World Average                          19.2 kg
Cuba                                  67.6 kg
U.K.                                  49.4 kg
U.S.A.                                47.9 kg
Algeria                                19.2 kg
Kenya                                  16.7 kg
Sudan                                 16.0 kg
Tanzania                               7.8 kg
9.        Imports.  From 1955 to 1967 imports, which continuously exceeded
domestic production of sugar, averaged 59,000 t/year. While for the period
1968-75 domestic production exceeded imports in all years except one, the
average level of annual imports remained high at 55,000 t. The aggregate
supply and demand picture for sugar for the period 1955-75 is shown in Table
2. The monthly supply and disposition of sugar for the period January, 1974
to September, 1976 is depicted in Table 3.
Projections for the Period 1976-90
10.       Production.  With the expansion of the Mumias factory to 76,000 t/
year in 1976 and full production expected in 1978, and the minor improvements
currently undertaken in the other existing factories, total sugar production
would be about 189,000 t/year from 1979 onwards. With the planned new invest-
ments during the 5-year period 1977-81 at Mumias and the new areas in Nzoia
(60,000 t) and South Nyanza (90,000 t), total yearly sugar production would,
it is estimated, increase to 386,000 t by 1985 and reach 417,000 t from 1990
onwards. Additional investments to improve the output at Ramisi, Muhoroni,
Chemelil and Miwani would increase Kenya's sugar production to approximately
491,000 t in 1990 (Table 4).



ANNEX 4
Page 4
11.       Consumption.  Kenya s population is projected to increase from 13.3
million in 1975 to 18.6 million in 1985. Per capita consumption of sugar
declined further in 1976. This decline, tentatively estimated to amount to
16% below the 1975 level, reduced consumption to less than 13.0 kg/person.
The development of future demand depends largely on Government's price policy
and changes in household incomes. If consumer prices were pegged at levels
such as to allow an annual growth in per capita consumption of 3.5-4.5%, total
demand of sugar would increase to about 327,000-355,000 t by 1985, and to
464,000-524,000 t in 1990 (Table 5). Assuming an annual per capita income
growth of 2.8% and an income elasticity of demand of 0.4, such consumption
levels could only be achieved if the real price of sugar to consumers (i.e.
after adjusting for inflation) were reduced by about 2-3% annually throughout
the period considered. However, if a policy of maximizing domestic consump-
tion were pursued, assuming no subsidies to either producers or consumers, the
resulting demand would considerably exceed these projections in 1985, but be
nearly identical in 1990 (Table 6). Government's demand projection of 25.8
kg/person in 1985, representing an extrapolation of the historic trend up to
1974, seems unrealistic with respect to costs of production, general economic
conditions and the underlying demand elasticities for sugar.
C. Prices
12.       Price formation in the sugar industry is entirely administrative
rather than the result of interaction of free market forces. Upon recommen-
dation by the KSA, the Ministries of Agriculture, Finance and Commerce fix
all prices and margins, usually for a period of one year. The basis of KSA's
recommendation are costs of production, processing and distribution, and a
margin to provide a fair return at all stages in the marketing channel.
Prices are generally uniform throughout the country, although provincial
government officials may make adjustments to allow for differences in trans-
port costs within a Province.
13.       Sugarcane Prices.  The price of sugarcane delivered at factory gate
was KShs. 52/t from 1969 to 1973, KShs. 62/t in 1974, KShs. 92/t in 1975 and
KShs. 105/t in 1976. As a proportion of the ex-mill price of sugar, the
price per ton of cane decreased from 5.7% in 1969 to 4.6% in 1976; as a pro-
portion of the retail price of sugar, it decreased from 3.4% to 2.3% (Table
7). Cane prices paid by the Ramisi factory have averaged about KShs. 2.00/t
less than prices paid by the other companies. Prices actually received by
farmers generally are considerably less than the official price on account of
transportation costs, levies and other charges being deducted. In 1976, for
example, transport charges in the Sugar Belt ranged from KShs. 21/t to KShs.
28/t, depending on distance, and cooperative levies totaled KShs. 6/t, re-
sulting in a farm gate price of KShs. 70-78 per ton of cane before deducting
other charges related to production. Net returns to farmers are shown in
Annex 20.
14.       Sugar Prices.  The price received by the factories was raised from
KShs. 1,230/t to KShs. 1,860/t in March, 1975 and to KShs. 2,300/t in January,
1976, or by 51.2% and 23.7%, respectively. As a proportion of the retail



ANNEX 4
Page 5
price, it dropped from 59.3% in 1966 to 51.1% in 1976. The retail price was
raised from KShs. 2,400/t to KShs. 3,500/t in 1975, and to KShs. 4,500/t in
1976, or 45.8% and 28.6%, respectively. These sharp increases in prices,
prompted largely by high international prices (Annex 19) and mounting import
requirements, severely depressed domestic consumption of sugar (para. 11).
Comparative retail prices for selected capital cities are shown below.
Retail Price in KShs./Kg
Mexico City                                    1.46
Brasilia                                       2.74
London                                         2.93
Canberra                                       3.11
Nairobi                                       4.50
Paris, Rome, Washington                       4.57
Bonn, Ottawa, The Hague                       4.76
Copenhagen                                    4.94
Dar-es-Salaam                                  5.00
Brussels                                       5.86
Buenos Aires, Stockholm                        6.59
Tokyo                                          7.50
Source: USDA, Foreign Agriculture, August 9, 1976
15.       Margins.  The cost to the Government of carrying stocks and of
transporting sugar from points of supply to KNTC depots increased from KShs.
49/t in 1974 to KShs. 70/t in 1975/76. KNTC was allowed a margin for storage
and handling of KShs. 18/t from 1969 to 1974, KShs. 38/t in 1975 and KShs.
32/t in 1976. Wholesalers' margins increased from KShs. 34/t in 1969-74 to
KShs. 40/t in 1975 and KShs. 45/t in 1976. The retail margin was nearly
doubled from KShs. 128/t to KShs. 250/t in 1975, and remained unchanged in
1976.
16.       With an estimated ratio of 15.9 t of cane per ton of sugar experi-
enced in 1966, the factory margin in that year was KShs. 92/t of sugar; the
value of cane represented 90% of the ex-mill price of sugar. In 1976, the
ratio of cane to sugar had declined to about 10:1, resulting in a factory
margin of KShs. 1,250/t and a share of cane in the ex-mill price of sugar of
46%.
17.       Taxes and Levies.  An excise duty, collected at the factory gate,
of KShs. 441/t of sugar was in effect over the past decade, but was raised to
KShs. 1,000/t in June 1976. Sugar excise revenues for 1970-76 are shown
below:

Page 6

Souce: Ministry of Finance and Planning
18.       Since the retail price of sugar is uniform regardless of the source
and cost of supply, an equalization levy on domestic production had been
imposed for some time to compensate for the higher cost of imported sugar.
Theoretically, the levy would be derived by pooling all revenues and expendi-
tures in the national sugar account, and determining the amount per ton sold
that would balance the account. In practice, such a balance has not been
achieved. The equalization levy was KShs. 100/t for 1969-74; it was raised
sharply with rising costs of imports to KShs. 500/t in 1974, KShs. 800/t in
1975 and KShs. 1,362/t in 1976. With those levies, the sugar account incurred
a deficit in four years and a surplus in two between 1970 and 1975, and the
accumulated funds in the account as of June 30, of each year fluctuated
between KShs. 15.5-82.9 million for the period. With the high 1975 and 1976
levies, however, revenues during the 11-month period of July, 1975 - May,
1976 greatly outpaced expenditures, and the accumulated funds in the sugar
account reached KShs. 313.1 million as of June 1, 1976 (Table 8). As in view
of the reduced domestic demand for sugar and falling world market prices
these funds were not expected to be required to compensate for high-cost im-
ports, part of the funds were transferred to General Revenue to help finance
the 1976/77 national budget.
19.       Import Prices.  The Kenyan Government concluded four sugar import
contracts, totaling 40,250 t, in October 1975 for delivery betwen December
1975 and June 1976. The price ranged from KShs. 3,196-3,440/t c & f Mombasa.
The cost of insurance averaged about KShs. 55/t, resulting in an average
import price of KShs. 3,410/t c.i.f. Mombasa. With port charges amounting to
KShs. 108/t, the landed cost was KShs. 3,518/t, before duties. Import prices
from 1980 onwards are forecast at KShs. 3,959/t c.i.f. Mombasa in constant
March 1977 prices (Table 9).
20.       Price Policy.  The relative deterioration of the price of sugarcane
with respect to the factory and retail price of sugar is likely to have been
a disincentive to cane producers and to have contributed to the shortages of
cane in the Nyanza Sugar Belt. The major impact of the relative decline in
cane prices is likely to have been the concomitantly increasing financial
risk to farmers associated with droughts. Farmers are apparently shifting



ANNEX 4
Page 7
to a low input-low output production regime (i.e., low levels of cash inputs
and longer replanting cycle) when, in their view, the expected net returns in
a normal rainfall year are not sufficient to compensate for possible losses
or prolonged indebtedness resulting from a drought year. A significant in-
crease in cane prices, to the equivalent of 50-55% of the ex-mill sugar price,
would stimulate outgrowers' production and provide an immediate response to
alleviate the problem of underutilization of sugar factories. With a (1976)
factory margin of KShs. 1,250/t the cane value would have to be in the range
of KShs. 1,250 - 1,528/t of sugar, or KShs. 125.00 - 152.80/t of cane in 1976
prices, to achieve the indicated cane to sugar value ratio, assuming 10% ex-
tractable sucrose; for 1977, this range has to be adjusted for inflation.
Based on the Project's financial analysis, a price of about KShs. 155/t would
be desirable after 1979 (Annex 20). The price proposed by KSA for 1977 is
KShs.150/t, but Government is expected to adopt a price of only KShs. 132/t.
It was agreed at negotiations that the 1977 price would be fully adjusted for
inflation in 1978 and 1979, and that an incentive margin of at least 6% would
be added after adjustment for inflation in each of those years. Government
would review the prices of sugarcane and sugar annually and submit to the Bank
a report on the outcome of the reviews.
21.       The factory margin obtained in 1976 was expected to greatly improve
the financial results of the sugar companies and considered adequate by
company managers. KSA is proposing a factory price of KShs. 2,900/t for 1977,
implying a factory margin of KShs. 1,400/t. The evaluation is based on a fac-
tory price of KShs.3,050/t from 1980 onwards, which is not inconsistent with
Government's long-term pricing policy. The margin and operating costs of the
factories would be reviewed, and factory prices adjusted annually.
22.       Trade margins, accounting for 7.3% of the retail price in 1976
appear reasonable, if not on the low side, and will require upward adjustment
to at least compensate traders and KNTC for cost increases due to inflation.
23.       The 1976 retail price of sugar is high by historical Kenyan and by
international standards. However, as long as there exist domestic shortages
it is appropriate to discourage consumption through the price mechanism within
reasonable limits. The apparent average 1976 consumption level of about 13 kg
per capita can be considered reasonable on a temporary basis, particularly
when compared with Tanzania.    A decrease in the real price of sugar would
again result in higher consumption. Only a gradual reduction is recommended,
however, to restrict the growth to a level which is consistent with the growth
in production expected from new investments. Such a policy would minimize
import requirements until 1980 when Kenya would most likely become self-
sufficient if the current plans for expansion in domestic production, i.e.
four projects, were successfully implemented.
24.       After 1980, the potentially rapid increase in the domestic supply
of sugar, which itself is an uncertainty, could only be absorbed within the
country for a few years and only if the consumer price of sugar were dras-
tically reduced. By 1982 or 1983, no further reductions in price would be
possible, however, without resorting to subsidies, and surpluses would most
likely develop. The data in Table 6 show that these surpluses are estimated
to be at least in the range of 40,000 - 90,000 t in the peak year, depending



ANNEX 4
Page 8
on the price elasticity of demand, and would occur over a period of five
to ten years. The surpluses would exceed these levels to the extent that
Government continued to levy an excise tax on sugar. The disposal of sur-
pluses represents a risk factor as, for example, the projected export parity
price for South Nyanza (factory gate) is just about equal, or slightly below,
the recommended ex-mill price of sugar produced under the Project. However,
the South Nyanza Sugar Company would have a comparative disadvantage in
production for export among Kenyan sugar companies on account of location
and cane yields and, hence, exports might still be profitable for other
companies. Consequently, and in view of the modest magnitude of the projected
surpluses; the desirability of establishing some buffer stocks above the
projected requirements for consumption; the uncertainties surrounding some of
the proposed investments; and the fact that a market analysis like this, i.e.
based on Kenya's overvalued currency, understates the country's comparative
advantage in sugar production, the risk for this Project associated with the
marketing of the resulting output is not considered large enough to pose
serious problems.
25.       To assure appropriate and timely action, it was agreed at negotia-
tions that Government would keep the projected production and consumption
of sugar in Kenya under continuous review and that a report of such a review,
including anticipated investments in the sugar industry over the following
three-year period, would be submitted to the Bank annually; the report
would also describe plans for marketing all sugar estimated to be produced
in Kenya over the next three-year period. It was further agreed that the
Bank would be informed of and given an opportunity to comment on any plans
to promote or invest in substantial additional sugar milling facilities.
February 20, 1977
