KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
THE PROJECT AREA - GENERAL DESCRIPTION AND CLIMATIC FACTORS
Location, Roads and Population
1.        The proposed Project area is located 50 km south of Kisii in South
Nyanza District of Nyanza Province. South Nyanza District is situated just
south of the equator and is bordered by Tanzania and Lake Victoria. It is
an agricultural area which is mostly devoted to subsistence crops and livestock
grazing and only marginally to cash crops. Part of the District is under
forest maintained by the Forestry Department. The peasants are generally
considered as better farmers than those in the Province's Sugar Belt which
is located east of Kisumu, the Provincial capital. The natural vegetation
consists of grass and scattered trees (savannah) and strips of riverine
forest along some of the streams. The Gucha river and its tributaries Sare
and Oyani drain the area. The Sare river flows through Awendo, the main
township in the3area, and close by the proposed factory site. Its dry-spell
flow is 0.112m per second which easily meets the factory, township and down-
stream water requirements and offers some potential for irrigation.
2.        The gross hectarage of the proposed Project area is approximately
77,000 ha situated roughly within a 16 km radius of Awendo between Rongo in
the north and Oyani market in the south. Part of this (3,410 ha) would be
reserved for the factory and the nucleus estate, the remainder would be
potential outgrowers' area. The nucleus estate would have a net cane area of
2,650 ha. In the outgrowers' area of which 51% is suitable for cane growing,
about 15,850 ha of cane area would be needed eventually, which would be about
51% of the suitable land.
3.        The A-1 national highway traverses the area from north to south and
passes near Awendo. This high-standard, well-aligned road has a 5.5 m
bituminous carriageway with one meter wide shoulders and generally two meter
wide side ditches. The other existing roads in the area are all of a gravel
standard and are generally good near the A-1 highway, but deteriorate grad-
ually with the distance from the A-1. A number of tracks also cross the
area, their passabilitiy depending on the vehicle type and the season. Most
of the roads and tracks have a satisfactory alignment. However, at present,
some of the bends are too sharp and some gradients too steep for cane trans-
port. Most of the roads and tracks are considered to be a good basis for the
construction of sugar transportation roads.
4.        The total population in the Project area is approximately 95,000
people, or 123 persons/km . This compares with a population density of 116
persons/km  for the District as a whole, and 306 persons/km  in the neigh-
boring Kisii District. The average family size is seven persons. Awendo
township is a small market place and trading center. Of the total of about
13,600 families in the Project area, 500-600 would be displaced by the



ANNE.; 5
Page 2
Project's nucleus estate. The displaced people, who would be fully compen-
sated for the loss of their land and immovable property, would be given
hiring preferency by the sugar company, acquire land within the outgrowers
part of the Project area and participate in the outgrowers program, or move
outside the Project area with Government assistance, if requested. The
predominant tribe in the area is the Luo.
Land Tenure and Type of Farming
5.        In the Project area, about 20% of the land is under cultivation
and approximately 20% is fallow. The large portion of uncultivated land is
due to the relatively large average farm size of six hectares, the favorable
growing conditions under which enough subsistence crops for the farm family
can be produced on a fraction of the total holding, and the absence of
commercial infrastructure to dispose of surplus crops. Practically all land
is owned by its occupants. Land adjudication has been virtually completed in
the area designated for the nucleus estate, and is expected to be completed
in 1977 for the 50% of the outgrowers area which has not yet been covered.
6.        The major crop in the area is maize, followed by other subsistence
crops such as millet, cassava, sweet potatoes, beans and groundnuts. Sisal
plants are used for marking farm and homestead boundaries, and in some places
eucalyptus trees are grown near homesteads. The poorly drained areas are
mostly used for grazing, although maize and sugarcane are also grown in these
areas. The number of cattle in the area is about 500,000. Sugarcane is the
main cash crop in the Project area with a total area of several thousand
hectares. Maintenance of the cane is extremely poor and, consequently,
yields are low due mainly to the low prices paid by the jaggery factories,
the only commercial outlet. The level of agricultural technology employed
in sugar production is low; shallow bullock ploughing is a general practice,
drainage and weed control are rarely done and harvesting is irregular. In
1970, a tobacco project was started just south of the Project area; 900
farmers participated in the project in 1975. British American Tobacco Com-
pany buys the leaves and provides the farmers with seedlings and advice.
Within the Project area, the Migori-Uriri area may be designated as a tobacco
production area, but at present it is not anticipated that more than 200 ha
will be used. Tobacco is a very profitable crop for farmers.
Rainfall
7.        Rainfall records exist for five stations in the Project area and
for two in the vicinity, but most of these records are for short periods of
time and their reliabilitiy varies greatly. The rainfall statistics for the
Project area are thus very scanty as regards both area coverage and, with one
exception, lengths of records, and provide a weak though useable basis for
assessment of the incidence and severity of the "dry spells" common to the
area. Of the stations in the Project area, only the record for Uriri,
located about 5 km south of Awendo, the geographic center of the Project
area, and about 100 m higher in elevation than most of its productive land,
is of sufficient duration for analysis (32 years); and even this record



ANNEX 5
Page 3
contains a number of gaps and some questionable entries. The available
monthly and yearly rainfall data for Uriri for the years 1956-75 and, for
comparison, yearly values during the same period for Chemelil, located in
the Sugar Belt about 50 km east of Kisumu, are given in Table 1.
8.        The Project feasibility report, which is the only document contain-
ing extended discussion of rainfall and evaporation and their relationships
to crop yields in the Project area, provides the following summary:
"Rainfall in the (Project) area is fairly well distrib-
uted. There is, however, a surplus of rain from March to
May and deficiency from December to February. The cane
is likely to show stress in the dry spell, particularly
on soil Unit 3..."
This statement, and some of the underlying analysis, is inaccurate as briefly
outlined below. Aside from the facts that some of the data gaps were filled
by interpolation, that there commonly are two dry periods each year rather
than one, and that the statement:  "Rainfall...is fairly well distributed"
hardly seems an accurate description of an area that experiences a "deficiency
from December to February," there are several fundamental inaccuracies in the
discussion of rainfall presented in the feasibility report. Most important,
the average annual rainfall for the entire Project area is taken as the Uriri
rainfall multiplied by 1.1, and the dry spell (December-February) rainfall is
taken as equal to that of Uriri for the period. Disregarding the uncertain-
ties involved in obtaining these interpolation factors (1.1 and 1.0, respec-
tively), it is clear from inspection of the environment, review of rainfall
data and reference to the isohyets on Map IBRD 12431 (which indicate the
rainfall level that is exceeded three years in four), firstly, that average
annual "dependable" rainfall differences of 300 mm or more exist between the
southwestern and the northeastern portions of the Project area. Secondly, use
in the feasibility report's analysis of the factor 0.86 (instead of the
commonly used 0.7) for estimating the annual water requirement of cane from
pan evaporation data (from Migori) yielded an unrealistically high value
(i.e., 1,643 mm) which is not consistent with experience elsewhere. Thirdly,
there is not a valid basis for assuming that under ideal conditions the
potential yield in tons per ha per year is equal to the average annual rain-
fall in centimeters. Fourth, the "carry-over" (water storage) capacity of
these soils, while appreciable, is not as large as assumed (150 mm) mainly
because the rooting depth of cane is not very great in most soils of southwest
Kenya. Lastly, in using the Uriri rainfall data no allowance was made for the
fact that rainfall increases markedly with elevation in southwest Kenya, and,
as Uriri is about 100 m higher than most of the Project area, its rainfall
undoubtedly is higher than that of most of the Project area.
9.        Analysis of the available rainfall data indicates the following:
(a) the average annual rainfall at Uriri is 1,350 mm; the
range in average rainfall three years in four for the
Project area as a whole is 1,150 - 1,350 mm, increasing
from southwest to northeast;



ANNEX 5
Page 4
(b) there is a "severe" dry spell of 3 months or more in
the Project area at least one year in five (in which the
rainfall is no more than about half the consumptive use
requirement for the period);
(c) whereas the standard deviation of the average annual
rainfall 1/ is about 20% (i.e., is within 20% of the mean
two years in three) the standard deviation of the monthly
values exceeds 50% (i.e., the deviation from the mean
exceeds 50% at least one year in three) except in March,
April, September and October, and averages about 40% in
these months;
(d) there seems to be no significant difference in average
monthly or yearly rainfall, pan evaporation, consumptive
use requirements or incidence of severe dry spells between
the Project area and Chemelil and Muhoroni in the Sugar
Belt, but the years of deficiency in the two areas seldom
correspond.
10.       The implications for the growing of sugarcane of the rainfall
pattern in the Project area are summarized below. Precise yield estimates
are presented in Annex 7.
(a) in about one year in five availability of water is 10-20%
below the average, representing a mild constraint;
(b) lack of water is a severe constraint one year in five when
the deficiency is much greater than 20%;
(c) these deficiencies would reduce cane yields about 15% and
35% respectively, or the "drought factor" on average
reduces cane yields about 10% per year over a five-year
cycle; and
(d) the apparent "resistance to drought" of cane in south-
western Kenya is most likely the result of overestimating
its water requirements.
Temperature
11.       There are few and scattered temperature records in the Project
area. It is known, however, that the temperatures are on the low side for
cane. This is an unfavorable factor in that the plant crop requires about
20-22 months and the ratoons 18-20 months to mature; however, it is favor-
able in that owing to the relatively cool weather the percent of sugar in
1/   For Uriri, Rapogi and Migori (a total of 71 years of records).



ANNEX 5
Page 5
the juice is relatively high and the sugar content decreases slower than is
usually the case with delay in harvesting after maturity, and processing
after cutting.
Other Climatic Factors
12.       Hailstorms occur in the autumn, perhaps one year in two or three,
in scattered areas but have little effect on the production of cane. Io
local data is available on wind, but the East African Mieteorological Depart-
ment indicates that severe thunderstorms with short wind gusts up to 150 km
per hour can be expected at intervals of many years. Local data on humidity
also is not available but, as judged from a small amount of open pan evapora-
tion data, the average humidity of the Project area differs little from
Chemelil and M4umias where no unfavorable effects are attributed to humidity
conditions. Sunshine duration has not been measured in the Project area but
can be safely assumed to average between 7 and 8 hours per day as in most
places in South Nyanza District where it has been measured; this sunshine
duration is sufficient for good cane growth.
December 30, 1976

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
SOILS AND DRAINAGE
A. SOILS
Surveys and Descriptions
1.        The entire Project area of approximately 77,000 ha as well as
various parts of it have been surveyed at different times and intensities for
different purposes; and the geology, topography, vegetation, soils, climate
and agricultrue have been described in more or less detail in: (1) "Soil
Survey of the East Konyango Area," 1961, Department of Agriculture, Kenya;
(2) "Proposed South Nyanza Sugar Project, Phase I Investigations," 1974,
Mehta Group; (3) "Climate, Physiography and Land Use of Southwestern Kenya,"
1974, Agriculture University, Wageningen, the Netherlands; (4) "Migori-
Kihancha Regional Master Plan - Water and Land Utilization," 1975, Viak EA;
(5) "Kenya Sugar Industry Expansion Study," 1975, Tate and Lyle; (6) "South
Nyanza Sugar Project, Phase II Investigations," 1975, Mehta Group, and other
reports. Since (6) constitutes the "Final Feasibility Report" for the Pro-
ject, it is the primary source of the information in this Annex, the other
reports and field inspections being used for substantiation of the conclusions
and recommendations included herein. Map IBRD 12431 was adapted from the
original provided in the feasibility report.
2.        Air photos at a scale of 1:8,000, flown in 1975, were used for
mapping the soils of the proposed nucleus estate (3,420 ha), and Kenya
Topographic Survey maps (1967) at a scale of 1:50,000 provided the base for
mapping the soils of the outgrowers area which amounts to about 74,000 ha.
According to the feasibility report, the
"Occurrence and variation of soils were checked with in-
spection by auger at approximately 150 m in the Nucleus
Estate and 400 m in the outgrowers area. Pits were dug
to the depth of 1 to 2 m... The profile horizons were de-
scribed using FAO (1960) terminology and guidelines with
slight modifications. In all 96 profiles were studied.
Samples representative of each horizon of all the selected
profiles were taken ... for analysis."
Almost all the profiles studied were located in the nucleus estate area. The
chemical and physical characteristics of 16 profiles (57 samples) of the
soils judged suitable for sugarcane are summarized in the following paragraph
and their nutrient status in para. 9.
3.        The major portion of the Project area is at an elevation of 1,400
to 1,460 m (4,600 - 4,800 ft.) above sea level, and the area slopes toward
the northwest. The Project area is traversed by a number of small to moderate



ANNEX 6
Page 2
sized streams, including the Sare River, and is largely a severely dissected,
ancient peneplain consisting of a large number of very narrow to narrow
valleys, mostly separated by gently to steeply inclined land; accordingly not
much of the land is flat, about 85% of the area having a slope greater than
1%. The geology is moderately complex, consisting of mixtures of both acidic
and basic pre-cambrian primary and secondary rocks, including rhyolites,
andesites, sandstones, dacites, dolerites, basalts, tuffs and agglomerates.
Under normal conditions, the acidic parent materials weather to dark reddish-
brown, moderate to fine textured, non-swelling, slightly to moderately acidic,
well leached soils, low in calcium and magnesium but moderately high in
organic matter and in nitrogen. The more basic rocks weather into dark brown,
finer textured, moderate swelling, less acidic, less well leached soils
generally higher in calcium, magnesium, organic matter and nitrogen. Movement
of the finer soil materials downslope (erosion) and its weathering together
with mixed rocks in the wet lowland areas has resulted in the formation of
dark brown to black, very fine textured, moderately swelling, poorly drained,
slightly acidic, nutrient-rich soils, mottled red, blue and green in the
reduced (gleyed) subsoil.
4.        In addition to the effects of parent material, differences in local
topography have helped produce a definite sequence of these soils as follows:
on the ridge crests and upper slopes are found reddish or brownish, well-
drained soils of variable depth over weathered rocks or gravel; on the slopes
the soils are browner, finer, deeper, less well-drained, generally over mixtures
of coarse gravel and mottled clay; and in the valley bottoms the soils are
deep, dark brown to black, very fine textured, poorly drained, generally with
thin gravel layers interspersed with waterlogged, gleyed (mottled red-orange-
blue-green) clays. Although approximately two-thirds of the soils of the
Project area are cultivable, only about 20% is cropped annually, the cultiv-
able lands that are steep, rough, shallow or poorly drained mainly being used
for grazing. The soils are unusually high in clay content and in organic
matter for tropical soils; have moderate water holding capacities, low bulk
densities and moderate to low permeabilities and are not very erosive.
Suitability for Sugarcane
5.        A fairly detailed survey has been made of the soils of the nucleus
estate, and soils are separated into 11 classes (called Units), the first six
of which are judged suitable for profitable production of sugarcane under the
proposed Project conditions. Of the many possible characteristics that dif-
ferentiate soils (e.g., color, texture, productivity, mode of origin, depth,
clay type, structure, slope, thickness and sequence of horizons, drainability,
fertility, etc.) only need for drainage and depth of soil profile have been
used as soil classification criteria in the feasibility report. Soil units
1, 2, and 3 are well-drained and units 4, 5 and 6 are poorly drained but
would be provided with drainage facilities under the Project; about 90% of
these soils also require leveling to prepare them for cane production. The
principal difference between the soils of each of these two groups of three
in addition to need for drainage is depth to rock or gravel (i.e., potential
storage of moisture and/or rooting depth). A systematic classification is
as follows:



ANNEX 6
Page 3
Soil Unit
Profile Depth            Well Drained       Poorly Drained
Deep                             1                  4
Moderately Deep                 2                   5
Shallow                         3                   6
6.        The soils of the outgrowers area, having been surveyed in much less
detail, were separated in the survey into only three arable classes (called
Groups). Of these, only the first two Groups (I and Tb) are judged suitable
for production of cane by outgrowers and only Group I is proposed for inclu-
sion in the Project. Group I includes the above soils Units 1 and 2 (well-
drained, deep and moderately deep), whereas Group Ib includes soil Unit 3
(i.e., well-drained but relatively shallow and hence having a high drought
hazard). The Group II soils, consisting of soil Units 4, 5 and 6 which
would require drainage, and the Group Ib soils have been excluded from
consideration for production of cane in the outgrowers area. The remainder,
Groups III, ITIb, IV, and V, are considered unsuitable for any sort of
cultivation.
7.        The soil Units and their suitability for cane production in the
nucleus estates area are described in the feasibility report as follows:
Unit 1 - "Very deep, red to brown clay loams or sandy clay overlying clay
or sandy clay, free of stones and gravel at least to 120 cm depth,
occurs on ridge crests and upper slopes (0-4%), pH 5.2-5.8, low
level of N and P, no drainage limitation: very suitable."
Unit 2 - "Deep, red to brown clay loams or sandy clay surface over clay
or sandy clay, free of gravel and stones at least to 75 cm depth,
occurs on ridge crests and upper slopes (0-5%), pH1 5.1-6.1, low
levels of N and P, gravel and rock below 75-100 cm, no drainage
limitation: suitable."
Unit 3 - "Slightly shallow red to brown sandy clay loam or sandy clay loam
surface over sandy clay, free of gravel and stones at least to 50
cm depth., occurs on upper slopes (more than 3-4%), pH 4.6-5.7, low
to very low levels of nutrients and water holding capacity, gravel
below 50-75 cm depth, no drainage limitations: fairly suitable."
Unit 4 - "Very deep, dark gray-brown sandy clay loam or sandy clay over
coarse textured center overlying sandy clay or clay, gravel
often present in subsoil, found on lower ridge slopes, pH 4.8-
5.7, low levels of nutrients, subsoil permeable, poorly drained,
incursion of seepage and perched water table above impermeable
subsoil:  suitable-needs drainage."
Unit 5 - "Very deep, dark sandy or silty clay loam over poorly drained
mottled clay subsoil often with plinthite gravel and pebble
beds, occurs on lower ridge slopes, valley floors and higher



ANNEX 6
Page 4
lying flat lands, pH 4.8-6.0, low level of nutrients, low per-
meability, poorly to very poorly drained: suitable-needs effec-
tive drainage."
Unit 6 - "Very deep, complex alluvial soils of variable texture with
poorly permeable subsoil, local occurrence, poorly drained:
suitable-needs drainage."

Nutrient Status and Fertilizer Response
9.        According to the feasibility report, the "available nutrients K,
Ca, Mg, Mn and P were determined by (the) mass analysis method of Mehlich et
al," and the analytical results are interpreted thus:
"The soils in general are deficient in nitrogen and phos-
phorus. Barring Soil Unit 1 and 2, others generally have
low levels of potassium. Calcium levels are also low in
Unit 3 and 4 soils."
Two cane variety and three fertilizer trials were carried out in 1976 on some
of the better soils of the Project area. Early results of the variety trials
and two of the fertilizer trials are given and discussed in the feasibility
report. These results and field inspections clearly indicate that no statis-
tically significant response has yet been obtained to any fertilizer addition;
and, moreover, the cane yield levels show that the soils on which the trials
were located are well supplied with nutrients.
10.       There are several possible reasons for the different conclusions
obtained from analysis of soil samples (which indicate the soils to be
deficient in nutrients) and from the field fertilizer trials: (1) the
Mehlich method is not suitable for assessing the nutrient status of these
soils, (2) soils similar to those used in the fertilizer trials were not
analyzed, or (3) the analytical results were not correctly interpreted. It
is clear, however, that the fertilizer trials were located on very fertile,
lightly cropped or virgin soils, and that the fertilizer requirements of
cane in this area cannot yet be deduced from the results. If continued
sufficiently long, until the excess nutrients have been used up, the ongoing
trials should give realistic fertilizer responses. Meanwhile, it will be
necessary to estimate fertilizer requirements from (a) experience elsewhere
(i.e., Mumias, Sugar Belt), (b) from new trials on local, heavily cropped
soils, and (c) from a more suitable method of soil analysis. In the mean-
time, to ensure good yields, particularly in the nucleus estate, moderate
amounts of nitrogen and phosphorus should be used (Annex 7).
Soil Constraints
11.       On the nucleus estate, it is proposed in the feasibility report
that sugarcane be grown on all the arable soils - i.e., on soil Units 1 to
6. As discussed above, soil Units 4, 5 and 6 require drainage to be able to



ANNEX 6
Page 6
produce at profitable levels; and in addition, soil Units 3 and 6 are rela-
tively shallow - i.e., have relatively low water storage capacities. There-
fore, in drought periods cane yields in soil Units 3 and 6 would be more
severely affected than on the deeper soils (Annex 5). As regards drainage,
the feasibility report proposes that facilities be constructed and maintained
on the nucleus estate to ensure that soil Units 4, 5 and 6 will be as produc-
tive as Units 1, 2 and 3 (para. 21). Only an irrigation system would alleviate
the moisture availability constraint imposed by the shallower soils but no
irrigation is being proposed for the present. The yield decrement associated
with low water storage in soil Units 3 and 6 is taken into account as a
drought rather than as a soil factor (Annex 7).
12.       The soils proposed for production of cane in the outgrowers area
would, at least in the initial development period, ideally include only Group
I soils (i.e., soil Units I and 2) and these offer no signficant constraints
to production provided that they are reasonably well managed. If, for social
or other reasons, Government or the sugar company decide that soil Groups lb
and II will be used for cane production, constraints will be signficant and
costs of development higher or yields lower than projected. It is recognized
that there may be local rainfall runoff problems on lands at the foot of long
slopes, some erosion hazards on the steeper slopes, seedbed preparation
problems and temporary waterlogging in the finer soils, etc., but these also
are taken into account in the estimate of anticipated yields.
Soils Research
13.       No soils research of consequence to cane production is being
conducted in Kenya despite the fact that research workers, agronomists and
nucleus estate managers agree that the physical properties of soils have an
important influence on cane yields and production, and that soils research is
needed to establish the specifics of these influences and the means and costs
of possible ameliorative measures. For example, the specific reasons for the
following are not known: differences in cane rooting depth and hence in re-
sistance to drought in soils of similar texture; why deep subsoiling results
in striking increases in yield in some locations but not in others; why cane
yields are sometimes or in some places influenced by clay type, bulk density,
soil aeration and/or drainability, groundwater level, etc., and not in others.
Conclusions and Recommendations
14.       Soil Survey.  The intensity of the soil inspections, the accuracy
of the soil Unit separation (para. 5 and 7) and the amount of supporting
analytical data are satisfactory for Project appraisal and implementation
in the nucleus estate area. In the outgrowers area soil quality on average
probably is about as evaluated in the feasibility report, but the soil survey
data is not sufficiently detailed and the soil separations are unreliable in
too many instances to provide a satisfactory basis for Project implementation.
Therefore, the outgrowers area would be resurveyed with greater care and in
greater detail. This would provide information useful for production of cane



ANNEX 6
Page 7
as well as other crops and data on the kind, extent and location of soil-
associated crop production constraints. Survey activities could, if deemed
expedient, be limited only to plot by plot examination of outgrowers' land
offered for cane production; but this, because it would have to be done
piecemeal, would cost as much as a semi-detailed survey of the entire area
and it would not be as widely useful to the farming community or the nation.
15.       Availability of Soils for Cane Production.  As indicated by the
discussion and tables in paras. 5-7, all the arable soils in the nucleus
estate but only the Group I soils in the outgrowers area are proposed for
cane production. It is estimated that there are more than 25,000 ha of Group
I soils in the outgrowers area within a 16 km radius of the proposed factory
site. However, not more than half of this amount would be available for cane
production because (1) not all farmers on these soils would grow cane, (2) it
is expected that individual cane contracts would generally be of a size that
can be managed by the farm family, (commonly 2 ha), and (3) the cane must be
grown in blocks 10 ha or larger in size to facilitate land preparation,
harvesting and cane hauling which would be done by the factory.
16.       A factory with an annual processing capacity of 600,000 tons of
cane is proposed for the initial stage of Project implementation. This, at
the anticipated yield levels, would require the entire output of the nucleus
estate (2,650 ha) plus the production from about 9,500 ha (37%) of the Group
I soils in the outgrowers area, targets which can be reached with ease. The
further proposal to ready the factory by Project year 7 for an annual pro-
cessing capacity of 850,000 tons raises some questions regarding the soils on
which to produce this amount of cane. That is, the nucleus estate plus half
the Group I soils in the outgrowers area as presently surveyed, (i.e., half of
25,000 ha) would produce only about 760,000 tons per year at 64 and 48 tons
per ha, respectively; production would be 730,000 t with an outgrowers' yield
of 45 t/ha.
17.       Based on the very conservative assumptions (a) that not more than
half of any farm holding would be available for growing cane, (b) that there
would be no factory-independent growers of cane, and (c) that yields would
not increase significantly in the next 10 to 15 years, the remaining 90,000-
120,000 tons could be obtained in various ways. The possibilities include
(1) increasing the radius of the Project area slightly to include 1,800-2,500
ha more Group I soils, or extensions into neighboring Kisii District which
has favorable growing conditions, or (2) including as cane-producing lands
both the Group II and Group lb soils within a radius of about 6 km of the
factory; the Group II soils would require drainage facilities and then the
deeper ones would produce 48t/ha/year, and Group Ib soils and the shallowest
Group II, owing to the greater drought risk, could be relied upon for an
average yield of only about 36t/ha/year. On the other hand, production would
reach 850,000 tons in 12 years on the estate and the Group I soils if either
(a) about 58% of the Group I soils were used for sugarcane instead of 50%
and (b) if cane yields increased about 5% in the 12-year period; or if in
addition to 50% of the Group I soils, about 10% of the Group Ib and Group II
soils were used for cane (either by independent producers or developed ap-
propriately by the Project authority).



ANNEX 6
Page 8
18.       Fertility Trials.  The ongoing fertility trials will not, at least
in the next five years or so, provide information on the fertilizer require-
ments of cane in the Project area. Also, the lack of significant response
to P and K in lowland soils generally, increases in cane yields in the Sugar
Belt as a result of gypsum applications, and response to single but not to
triple superphosphate in the Mumias area indicate that the need for P and K
is not well understood, and that sulfur and/or calcium deficiency may be a
problem in the Project area soils.
19.       Under the Project, fertilizer trials would be initiated on Project
land that has been cropped continuously for 10 years or more as part of the
national sugar research program; other analytical methods of estimating soil
fertility would be correlated with field and greenhouse trials in the Project
area and elsewhere; and the possibility of sulfur and/or calcium deficiencies
in the sugar-growing area would be investigated.
20.       Soils Research.  Despite the obvious relationships between the
growth and production of sugarcane and the physical properties of the soils
upon which the cane is grown, little if any relevant soils research is now
being carried on in Kenya. A comprehensive sugar research program should
include an investigation of the effects of the physical properties of soils on
cane yield, and the methods of modifying these properties and effects, e.g.,
drainage, subsoiling, etc. (para. 13). Funds and suitable personnel would be
provided.
B. Drainage
21.       The feasibility report recommended construction of drainage facil-
ities for all of the poorly drained soils in the nucleus estate (soil Units
4, 5 and 6 which comprise about a third of the estate) but has proposed none
for the outgrowers area. The designs and specifications proposed for the
field, "cross" and main drains are satisfactory for a surface drainage sys-
tem only, despite statements to the contrary in the report such as: "The
network of.. .drains have been designed to drain away all surplus water from
the waterlogged soils..." and "the soils are likely to respond to drainage
measures, thus increasing root penetration" - statements which imply a
subsoil drainage system. Rotary ditchers mounted on 4-wheel drive tractors
are recommended for construction, and manual labor is proposed for finishing
the drains. The drains are designed to handle 70% of a 3-hour rainfall of 70
mm or 45 litres per sec per ha, a runoff which is estimated to occur once in
5 to 10 years. The infield drains which would be about 0.5 m wide and 0.5 m
deep and installed at every 10th to 20th furrow in the poorly drained cane
fields, would empty into the cross drains. The cross drains, which would be
designVd to collect the runoff from 20 ha of cane, would be about 0.75 m deep
(0.6 m in cross sectio~), would have a slope of about 1% and a maximum capac-
ity of about a cumec (m per second), and would empty iito the main drain.
The main drains would be 1.0 to 1.2 m deep (about 1.5 m in cross section)
have slopes of 1 to 5%, and be designed to collect the effluent from 4 of
the cross drains (i.e., from 80 ha). The main drains in turn would empty
into nearby small streams or natural drainage ways, some of which would
require considerable cleaning or deepening.



ANNEX 6
Page 9
22.       A typical drainage layout, therefore, would consist of 8 plots of
cane of 10 ha, each provided with cross drains emptying into main drains.
Taking the local topography into account, the average length of cross drains
needed for such an 80 ha unit is estimated at 3,200 meters (40 m/ha) and of
main drains 1,000 m (12.5 m/ha).  The volume of excavation required for 3con-
struction of the cross and the main drains is estimated to be 1 and 2 m  per
meter of length, respectively, or a total of 65 m per ha for both. The
original feasibility analysis was based on the assumption that the main drain
requirements for the uplands would be the same as for the lowlands (i.e.,
12.5 m/ha) but that the cross drain requirements for the uplands would be  3
only 5 m per ha; also, that the cost of excavation would be KShs. 2.0 per mi
including trimming and dressing by manual labor; improvement of the natural
waterways is estimated to amount to "20% extra work." Accordingly, the
original average cost estimated for both the cross and main drains was KShs.
125 per ha and a total of KShs. 332,000 for drainage of the entire nucleus
estate area.
23.       Subtracting the "20% extra work" (i.e., KShs. 25 per ha), the
drained area works out to be 2,656 ha, almost exactly the area proposed for
production of cane in the estate area. However, the entire area of the
nucleus estate, 3,410 ha, must be drained (not just 3the cropped area); also
the cost of excavation would be KShs. 3.0-3.5 per m rather than KShs. 2.0.
Including these changes the appraisal estimate of the cost is KShs. 509,000
for the excavation; adding "20% extra work" would increase the total for
drainage of the estate area to KShs. 640,000 (i.e., about US$ 22.60 per ha
gross or US$29.00 per ha of cane).
24.       Both construction of the field drains, which would be required
repeatedly every 5 years and is estimated to cost KShs. 140 per ha, and
cleaning the cross and main drains, which is indicated at KShs. 50 per ha
per year, are considered to be operating costs. Detailed cost estimates
are shown in Annex 15.
February 20, 1977

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
LAND DEVELOPMENT AND ALRIuTURAL PRACTICES
Land Clearing and Cane Field Preparation
1.        The Project area would be divided into a nucleus estate, and an
outgrowers area surrounding it. The nucleus estate, with the exception of
the factory site and residential compound, a number of townships and some
small pockets of unsuitable land, would be developed as a compact factory-
operated cane area. The net cultivated area would be 2,650 ha, of which 50
ha would be set aside for nurseries (para. 9). The outgrowers area would
increase gradually and eventually occupy a total cane area of almost 15,900
ha. It is expected that the total cane area would amount to 9,700 ha
after five years and to 18,505 ha after eleven (Table 1). If the factory
were not expanded beyond the 60,000 t annual capacity in Year 7, the plant-
ing of new areas would stop early in the seventh year and the ultimate cane
area would be about 12,300 ha.
2.        A nucleus estate is considered necessary especially during the
first years so that the factory would rot be completely dependent on the
supply of outgrowers cane, the supply of whlich has generally proven to be
less reliable.  Eventually, the outgrowers' share would increase from about
54% of total cane supply in the first two milling seasons (Years 4-5), to
around 80% from the seventh milling season onwards. The nucleus estate would
be developed over a period of four years, the outgrowers area over a period
of 7-11 years. In order to develop the total required sugarcane area, ma-
chinery and implements would be purchased for developing 2,200-2,300 ha per
annum (para. 20).
3.        In general, the outgrowers would use only part of their holdings
for sugarcane production, the other part being planted to subsistence or cash
crops, used for grazing or left under fallow, Given an average family size
of seven persons, it is estimated that approximately two hectares of sugarcane
could be maintained by each family.  A ninimum production unit size of 10 ha
is necessary for efficient land preparation, harvesting, and transporation.
Therefore, at least five neighboring farmers would have to contribute land to
form each production unit.
4.        Land clearing at the nucleus estate would be conducted mechanically
by chain felling, wind rowing and burn-nco Outgrowers would be required to
clear their own land as an indication of their Intent to join the sugarcane
enterprise.  After land clearing, preparatlen of all land for cane growing
would be conducted by the Agricultural Department of the Company. This
includes ripping, ploughing, harrowing and furrowing. In order to obtain
optimal conditions, these practices would be done mechanically, in both the
nucleus estate and the outgrowers' area. Since land preparation in the high



ANNEX 7
Page 2
rainfall months would be difficult in part of the Project area and the re-
sults of such tillage inferior, the months of March, April, May and November
would have to be excluded from land preparation practices. The Comiany would
pay for the development of the nucleus estate, while all development costs
in the outgrowers area would be borne by the outgrowers (Annex 18).
5.        Ripping would be necessary for removing stumps and heavy roots and
would help loosen the subsoil. On the nucleus estate, ploughing would be
done by a heavy Rome plough, followed by two harrowings; most of this work
would be conducted by crawler tractors. In the outgrowers' area, however,
where most of the soils designated for cane production are well-drained,
ploughing and harrowing would be done by hydraulic, mounted implements on
wheel tractors, which are more mobile than crawler tractors. Furrowing would
also be performed mechanically with disc bedders. The plant lines would be
uniformly spaced for easy mechanization of other cultural practices, such as
weeding and hilling-up.
6.        The land development and cane planting schedule for the first 15
years, as presented in Table 1, is based on a 5-year crop cycle including one
plant crop and two ratoons. The number of ratoons would be reconsidered in
due course, taking into account yield experience and economics. In Chemelil,
for instance, four cuttings (one plant crop and three ratoons) are common
practice at the nucleus estate.
Cane Production Schedule
7.        Average cane yields are estimated at 64 t and 45 t per ha per annum
for the nucleus estate and outgrowers area, respectively. In a 5-year cycle,
the three crops grown would be one plant crop of 22 to 24 months, and two
ratoons of about 20 and 18 months, respectively.  A ratio of 100:85:70 between
the yields of plant crop, first and second ratoon, respectively, is assumed.
Approximately the same ratio is found in Chemelil (100:84:71), whereas the
Mumias ratio is slightly different (100:80:74). Yield estimates for the plant
crop and ratoons derived as shown in Appendix 1, are as follows:
Average
Plant Crop    1st Ratoon   2nd Ratoon   per year
Nucleus Estate           125           107          88            64
Outgrowers Area           88            75          62            45
8.        Based on these assumptions and on the land preparation schedule set
out in Table 1, cane production in the nucleus estate and outgrowers areas
would be as presented in Table 2. From the 6th Project year onwards, cane
production would come up to the factory capacity of 530,000 tons per year
(i.e., 2,000 TCD over 265 milling days). Between the 7th and 9th year, the
factory capacity would have to be extended to 850,000 tons (i.e. 3,000 TCD).
Full factory capacity would be reached in the 12th year.
Cane Varieties
9.        The dominant sugarcane variety grown in the western part of Kenya
is C0421. In many cases, however, the plantings appear to be mixed with at



ANNEX 7
Page 3
least one other variety, probably NCO310. C0331 is grown to a lesser extent
in some fields of the Sugar Belt; it is considered more drought resistent
but has a higher fiber content than C0421. Other varieties are still in an
experimental stage.
10.       A variety trial in Mumias showed NCO376 to be a promising variety
for the region with a 10% higher cane yield than the standard C0421. In a
trial conducted by the National Sugar Research Station the NCO376 had even a
25% higher yield than C0421 and also a better juice quality and a slightly
lower fiber content. NCO376 also may be more drought resistent than C0421.
A number of other varieties in the same or other trial fields appeared to
yield as well as or better than C0421. None of these, however, have been
included so far in the experiments in South Nyanza.
11.       Well-chosen varieties can increase yields significantly.  Further-
more, it is recommended that more than one variety be planted in the area.
If only one variety were grown the variety-specific susceptibility to pests,
diseases and/or other adverse conditions would increase the vulnerability of
the enterprise. A part (10-20%) of the commercial estate should be planted
with promising new varieties from the onset of the Project; cane seed avail-
ability, however, may be a limiting factor in that respect.
Cultural Practices
12.       Planting of cane would be done manually unless mechanical planting
proves to give better results, as labor-intensive agriculture is preferable
for both political and social reasons. Accurate planting with close super-
vision would have favorable effects in the entire crop cycle of five years.
Due provision would be made for the filling of gaps in the plant crop by
planting excess seeds in each cane row. It would not be possible to plant
throughout the year owing to the lack of sufficient moisture for germination
and initial growth. It may be assumed that only the months of March, April,
August, September and the first half of October would be suitable for planting.
13.       Seed for planting would be obtained from 12-month old cane in local
nurseries. The nurseries would be located in a number of places throughout
the nucleus estate and outgrowers area in order to minimize the transport
costs of the seed cane. The total area of nurseries would not exceed 395 ha
in any year. After cutting the seed cane, the ratoons of the nurseries would
be included as part of the plantation. The nurseries in their turn would
be planted with cane seeds derived from hot-water treated mother seed cane
fields. These would be planted at the nucleus estate and would not occupy
more than 50 ha. Based on a need of seven tons of seed for planting one
hectare and a planting season of 4.5 months per year, the capacity of the
hot-water treatment plant (located near the factory) should be about three
tons per day. The seed for the nurseries in Project Year 1 would be obtained
from Kibos, Mumias and Chemelil. As a precaution against soil-borne fungus
diseases, cane seed should be dipped in a fungicide solution (0.5% solution
of Aretan-3) as a standard treatment for all plantings. Approximately 650 gr
of the fungicide would be used per hectare.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
14.       Weeding would be conducted frequently and regularly until the canopy
closes. Depending on the terrain, weeding would be done either manually or
mechanically. Chemical weed control may be considered at a later stage when
the practical and economic implications can be more accurately determined.
Some of the weedings would be combined with the hilling operations of the
young cane (para. 13). Planting, gapping and weeding would be done with
hoes; for fertilizing, gunny bags and tins should be made available to the
laborers. Since fertilizer bags would be reused, gunny bags have not been
separately costed.
15.       Cane growth has not shown significant responses to fertilizer
applications in the experiements conducted in the area apparently because the
soils are rather fertile (Annex 6). Therefore, only light dressings to cover
possible deficiencies have to be given. The evaluation is based on average
requirements over a period of several crop cycles, however, and, as such, does
not represent a management recommendation; the assumed applications would thus
be on the high side at the beginning of the Project period, and possibly too
low in later years. In the plant crop, all fertilizer would be applied at the
same time as planting; in the first ratoon, fertilizer would be applied soon
after harvesting the plant crop. In the 2nd ratoon, nitrogen would be applied
in two dressings, the first after harvesting the first ratoon, the second
after 5 to 6 months. The following quantities have been assumed in the
Project cost estimates:
Calcium Ammonium         Single Super-
Nitrate (20%N)      phosphate (20%P205)
(kg/ha)
Plant Crop                   100                  300
1st Ratoon                  200                   300
2nd Ratoon                  400                   300
16.       Hilling is a cultural practice which ultimately reverses the
profile of the cane field; cane which is planted in the furrow eventually
grows on the ridge.  This gives a better anchorage of the cane stool and
improves drainage and aeration. Hilling would be done in two stages, either
manually or mechanically by tractor-drawn ridgers. This also helps control
weeds in the interrows (para. 14). Hilling would only be conducted in the
plant crop, not the ratoons.
17.       In the outgrowers area, the trash left after harvesting would be
moved from the cane lines into the interrows. This accelerates development
of the shoots of the ratoon crop, suppresses weed growth, promotes soil
moisture conservation and may help increase the organic matter content of
the soil; it may reduce the available nitrogen, however, and increase fertili-
zer requirements. In the nucleus estate, where the cane would be burned prior
to harvesting, no trash raking would be needed. Interrow ripping (sub-soiling)
of the ratoon crop in both the nucleus estate and outgrowers area, would only
be considered if cane growth is impeded by relatively impervious hard pans.
18.       Attention would be given to the possibility of using oxen by out-
growers for certain cultural practices, for example, weeding and hilling-up.
Peasants in South Nyanza are currently using oxen as draught animals and much
of the tedious work could be conducted relatively cheaply.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
Pest and Disease Control
19.       Nowhere in Western Kenya are cane pests and diseases considered a
serious threat to the industry. Those which do occur in the region in order
of significance are the following:
(a) Smut disease, caused by the fungus Ustilago scitaminea.
It can be kept within reasonable limits by roguing infected
cane stools in an early stage.
(b) Ratoon Stunting Disease, caused by a virus, is potentially
a serious disease and can decrease ratoon yields signifi-
cantly. As a precaution, hot-water treated seed cane and
sterilized knives should be used in all new plantings.
(c) Brown spot, caused by the fungus Cercospora longipes
occurs throughout the area. Economic losses due to this
disease, however, are negligible.
(d) Wild pigs occasionally cause some damage in cane fields
but cannot be considered as a generally occurring pest.
(e) Termites occasionally infest cane fields but can be
controlled chemically.
(f) Army worm is also found, but control is not considered
worthwhile since the damage inflicted to the cane is
negligible.
(g) Both top and stalk borers do occur, but under the present
conditions the damage is of no importance; if they do
become a threat variety selection would provide the best
control.
Operations and Machinery Implements Requirements
20.       Development.  The following land development operations would be
conducted:
(a) Chain felling would be restricted to the nucleus estate
only. Since 40% of the area is either cultivated or under
fallow, only 60% of the area would have to be cleared.
Two crawler tractors and one 4-wheel drive tractor (120-
150 HP) with bulldozer blade would be needed, two for
pulling the chain and one for assisting in uprooting heavy
trees. The capacity of a unit of three tractors is 0.3 ha
per hour.
(b) Piling (wind rowing) would follow chain felling. This
would be done by crawler tractors or 4-wheel drive trac-
tors equipped with rake blades. The capacity per tractor
is 0.3 ha per hour.



ANNEX 7
Page 6
(c)  Ripping would be conducted in both the nucleus estate and
the outgrowers area. Either crawler tractors or 4-wheel
drive tractors (120-150 HP) equippped with ripper shanks
with a penetration depth of 70 cm would be used. The
capacity per tractor is 0.4 ha/hour.
(d) Ploughing would be conducted at the nucleus estate with
crawler tractors (120-150 HP) and a Rome plough, in the
outgrowers area with 4-wheel drive tractors and hydraulic
mounted ploughs. The capacity per tractor is 0.3 ha/hour.
(e) Two harrowings would follow ploughing. The same tractors
would be used as for ploughing; tillage harrows would be
used. The capacity per tractor per harrowing is 0.7
ha/hour.
(f) Furrowing would be the last mechanical field preparation
practice, apart from digging in-field drains (Annex 6).
In the nucleus estate, crawler tractors (60-70 HP) with
ridging bodies would be used, in the outgrowers area,
4-wheel drive tractors with ridging bodies would be used.
The capacity is 0.8 ha per hour.
21.       Land preparation would be done eight months per year (para. 4).
With 24 actual work days per month and 12 working hours per day (2 shifts of
6 hours), 2,300 hours of land preparation time would be available per year.
In the nucleus estate, all land preparation could be carried out with 5
tractors during the first Project year; in the outgrowers area, one tractor
would be sufficient. Some overcapacity of the tractors would be utilized in
preparing part of the area of the second Project year. The number of hours
necessary for land preparation as well as machinery requirements are pre-
sented in Table 3 for the nucleus estate and in Table 4 for outgrowers.
22.       Maintenance.  Maintenance of cane fields after planting would
consist mainly of weeding, fertilizing and hilling. The outgrowers would
carry out weeding and fertilizing practices themselves in their own cane
fields but the work would be supervised by personnel of the Agricultural
Department of the Company (Annex 18). At the nucleus estate, weeding and
hilling could be done in combination manually and mechanically. A wheel
tractor, equipped with tines for weeding and ridgers for hilling could be
used in cane not higher than 1 meter (5 to 6 months old). After that,
weeding could be done manually until the canopy is closed (approximately
1 year). In the evaluation, only manual weeding has been assumed. In case
of maximum mechanization of the plant crop, two hillings and three and four
weedings would be required. Since hilling can be considered as a weeding
in most instances, only two hillings and two weedings would be necessary.
In the ratoons, three to four weedings would be carried out. The capacity
of the machinery is 0.8 ha per hour for hilling and 1.6 ha per hour for
weeding. A planting and ratooning schedule is included in Table 1.



ANNEX 7
Page 7
23.       Replanting.  Replanting would start in Project Year 6, with plough-
ing, harrowing and furrowing to be carried out prior to replanting. The
machinery and machinery capacity would be the same as in the first planting
(para. 20). The area to be replanted, assuming development to a factory
capacity of 850,000 tons of sugarcane per year, would be as follows (in ha):
Project Year            6             7           8            9             10
Nucleus Estate           85          915         900           700            -
Outgrowers Area          75       1,500        1,500        1,675         2,200
Labor Requirements
24.       Manual labor would be required for planting, fertilizer application,
gapping and weeding, where this is not done mechanically. In the outgrowers
area, the farmers would carry out these activities in their own plots and,
moreover, they would perform trash raking after harvesting (para. 17). The
various practices would require the following labor:
Mandays Required Per Hectare
In 3-crops (5 years) cycle:
Planting              15                   15
Fertilizing            2                    8
Gapping                5                    5
Weeding                15 (per weeding)  270 (at 6 weedings per crop)
298
Planting, gapping and fertilizing of the plant crop would be conducted during
4.5 months in each year (para. 12). At 24 working days per month, the total
number of days available for these practices is 4.5 x 24 = 108 per year.
Fertilizing in the ratoon crops would be done during 10 months or 240 days
and weeding during the entire year or 288 days. The number of laborers
needed by the nucleus estate for carrying out the various treatments is
presented in Table 5; an outgrowers labor budget per hectare is shown in
Table 6. In those tables, other activities such as cleaning drainage ditches
(Annex 6), cane harvesting and transport (Annex 8), and roads construction
and maintenance (Annex 12) are not taken into account.
Agricultural Research
25.       Company Research.  Agricultural research for sugarcane in South
Nyanza would be conducted by a well-qualified Company agronomist, assisted by
an assistant-agronomist. Overseers and laborers would perform the special
treatments in experimental fields, collect cane samples for maturity tests,
assist in the hot-water treatment of cane seed, etc; even though some of these
activities do not constitute "research", responsibility for them would be
given to the Company agronomist and all related activities and costs have been
included under research for purposes of the evaluation. The work of the
agronomist and his staff would include:



ANNEX 7
Page 8
(a) Establishing experimental fields, making the necessary
observations and interpreting the results. Priority would
be given to variety and fertilizer trials and the monitoring of the
state of nutrients of the cane fields.
(b) Advising the Agricultural Manager on all agricultural
matters and on agricultural problems that may arise.
(c) Conducting maturity tests in collaboration with the
factory for the harvesting program.
(d) Supervising meteorological observations.
(e) Carrying out the hot-water treatment of cane seed.
(f) Supervising and coordinating with the National Sugar
Research Institute a soil survey to be carried out for each
prospective outgrowers plot to ascertain the suitability
for cane growing.
(g) Cooperating with the National Sugar Research Institute in
investigating the possibilities and economics of irri-
gation.
26.       National Research.  The Kibos Sugar Research Station is one of the
several research units for which the Director of Research of the Ministry of
Agriculture is responsible. Performing an important function in sugar indus-
try development, sugar research should receive more attention through the ac-
tive involvement of KSA to make it more responsive to the needs of the in-
dustry. Towards this end, the Kibos National Sugar Research Station, to be
renamed the National Sugar Research Institute, would receive additional ve-
hicles, equipment and staff, including three internationally recruited experts
for a period of two years, and cooperate with KSA staff in the formulation
of its future research program. A comprehensive sugar research program,
acceptable to the Bank, would be submitted to the Bank by July 1, 1978; sub-
sequent revisions would be available to the Bank's supervision missions for
inspection.
27.       The Institute would be responsible for research on cane varieties,
cane yields, soils and fertilizers, climatic factors, pests and diseases and
weeds and related activities designed to improve sugarcane productivity
economically in all cane-growing areas in Kenya. Its research program (para.
26) should focus particularly on drainage/tillage/fertilizer interactions,
variety screening, chemical weed control, the reasons for rapidly declining
yields in ratoons; and the effect of soil properties on yields (Annex 6). It
would also provide advisory services to Agricultural Departments of sugar
companies and, through regularly scheduled semiaars with agricultural personnel
of the companies, provide an open exchange of information on cane growing
practices.



ANNEX 7
Page 9
28.       To be headed by a Research Director, the research staff would con-
sist of a plant pathologist, entomologist, agronomist, agricultural engineer
(irrigation/drainage), soils scientist, and a chemist. A production econo-
mist would advise on the economic aspects of research projects. Through the
Agronomist of the Kenya Sugar Authority, the Institute's Research Director
would endeavor to incorporate experimental activities carried out by the sugar
companies into a coordinated national program. a proposed organization
structure is shown in Chart World Bank - 16463, and Project costs are presented
in Annex 15, Table 18.
Agricultural Extension
29.       Almost 8,000 individual farmers would participate eventually in
the production process. The major decisions concerning outgrowers cane
production would be made by the Company, and extension activities would be
limited largely to familiarizing farmers with the terms of their cane growing
contract and supervision to insure their adherence to contract terms. Infor-
mation on all aspects of cane growing such as planting methods, fertilizing,
weeding and other cultural practices, pest and disease control and harvesting,
all aimed at improving sugarcane yields would be made available to farmers
through the staff of the Outgrowers Division of the Company's Agricultural
Department (Annex 18). The cost of these services would be fully recovered by
the Company (Annex 20). An extension specialist, to be provided under the
Project for the Kenya Sugar Authority would coordinate extension activities on
an industry-wide basis (Annex 17).

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
ESTIMATION OF SUGARCANE YIELDS
Background
1.        Although sugarcane has been growing for years in the Project area
by smallholders, primarily for jaggery production, neither cane nor sugar
yield data have been gathered. A few variety and fertilizer trial fields,
established in recent years, are now in either their first or second season.
These fields are located in only some of the soil types and have not yet
been tested through drought periods which occasionally occur in the area.
The cane yields of plant crops and ratoons in these trial fields were about
9 t per ha per month and the present 18-month old ratoon crops have a good
stand despite weed problems in the early stage of these ratoons. The trial
fields indicate that the area has a good potential for cane growth. However,
the experimental results obtained there cannot be used as a basis for estimat-
ing the actual cane production of the area as the number of trials is too
limited; and cane yields calculated from a relatively small trial plot are
always considerably higher than the actual yields from large fields.
2.        In the feasibility report, the expected yield was based primarily
on evapo-transpiration figures. Several adjustment factors were then ap-
plied, accounting for soil suitability, management in the nucleus estate and
the outgrowers areas, consecutive ratooning, and an additional "contingency"
reduction factor. Ultimately, cane yields of 72.5 and 53 tons per hectare per
annum for the nucleus and for outgrowers areas, respectively, were derived.
Derivation of Yield Estimates
3.        An accurate estimation of yields is hindered by insufficient data
on climatic conditions, especially rainfall. Rainfall may be the main
limiting factor for cane production but other factors are also important,
including physical and chemical soil conditions (Annex 6), temperature and
sunshine, pests and diseases, sub-optimal varieties, and management. The
effects of the various factors in decreasing Project area yields below the
generally accepted potential cane yield of about 10 t per ha per month (120
t per year) are as follows:
Decreasing Effect in Percent
Outgrowers Area
Nucleus Estate       Group I      Group lb
Soils        Soils
Drought                          20                20           25
Management                       10                20           20
Varieties                        10                10           10
Pests and Diseases                2                 5            5
Temperature and Sunshine          5                 5            5
Soil                              0                 0            5
47                60          70



ANNEX 7
Appendix 1
Page 2
4.        Drought will be the primary limiting factor and the main cause of
reducing the yields of rainfed cane (Annex 5); an average yield decrease of
20% has been assumed, except for the shallower soils in the outgrowers area
for which a 25% decrease is more probable. Management, i.e., cane husbandry
in a broad sense, in an outgrowers area usually is of lower standard than in a
nucleus estate; under the Project, management is also likely to be sub-optimal
in the nucleus estate at least in the first five years of development due to,
among other things, new and sometimes unforeseen conditions and the relatively
inexperienced laborers and headmen. Management effectiveness would improve as
experience is being gained. The main cane variety grown in Western Kenya is
C0421, a 25-year old, well-known Indian variety. This is a suitable variety,
but other varieties with higher yielding capacity, better juice quality and
lower susceptibility to adverse conditions are becoming available (Annex 7,
paras. 9-10).
5.        The incidence of pests and diseases is relatively slight, and where
appropriate control measures have been practiced, the economic losses in cane
yield have been virtually nil. Nevertheless, a slight reduction in yield in
the nucleus estate and a somewhat higher reduction in the outgrowers area can
be expected. Temperature statistics are not available for the area but, be-
cause of the altitude, the temperature is relatively low and may decrease cane
growth to some extent. Characteristics of the selected soils are generally
favorable, particularly as the soils would be reasonably well tilled, drained
and fertilized, and no adjustment to the base yield estimate of 10 t/ha/month
is necessary; such adverse characteristics as do exist are primarily drought-
related and adjusted for in the drought factors, except for the shallower
outgrower soils for which a negative effect of 5% is explicitly recognized
(Annex 6).
6.        Application of the yield-decreasing factors results in the following
cane yield estimates:
Nucleus Estate: 10 t/ha/month - 47% = 5.3 t/ha/month or 64 t/ha/year
Outgrowers Area:
Group I soils: 10 t/ha/month - 60% = 4.0 t/ha/month or 48 t/ha/year
Group Ib soils: 10 t/ha/month - 70% = 3.0 t/ha/month or 36 t/ha/year
Only Group I soils are recommended to be used. However, if because of social
pressure or other reasons, Group Ib soils are accepted for cane growing within
a limited radius from the factory, the yields on such soils would adversely
effect the overall outgrowers average. To account for this possibiity, an
outgrowers average of 45 t/ha/year has been used in the evaluation, which
implies a maximum of outgrowers cane originating from the shallower Group
Ib soils of 25% of total outgrower cane supply. As such a large proportion
is not anticipated, the yield estimate contains a considerable slack element.



ANNEX 7
Appendix I
Page 3
The actual calculation of the yield averages over plant crop and ratoon crops
is described in Annex 7, para. 7.
7.        Future cane yields in both the nucleus estate and the outgrowers
area could be increased by correcting or ameliorating the effects of some of the
adverse factors. For example, management and variety selection could be
improved. On the other hand, if cane production were increased to beyond
one million tons per year, less favorable soils would have to be included for
cane growing, and the soil factor in the outgrowers area would become more
significant. Among the alternatives for expansion are the implementation of
a rather extensive drainage system in the badly drained areas, or extension
of the outgrowers area beyond the 16 km radius or into Kisii District.
On balance, yields could possibly be increased eventually to 70 - 75 t/ha/year
for the nucleus estate and to about 55 t/ha/year for the outgrowers area. It
is not likely that these yields would be reached for 10 - 15 years, however.
8.        Sugar production estimates are based on a factory recovery of 10%
during the first five operating years of the factory, and of 10.5% thereafter.
(Annex 20). An average recovery of 11% is being obtained at Mumias, and of
9% at Chemelil; the difference is mainly due to the time lapse between
harvesting and processing at the two companies, i.e. management factors.

ANNEX 8

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
HARVESTING, HANDLING AND TRANSPORT
OF SUGARCANE
Harvesting Schedule
1.        The cane production schedule is presented in Annex 7, Table 2.  All
cane, including the outgrowers cane would be harvested and transported by
staff of the Agricultural Services Division of the Company's Agricultural
Department (para. 8). Harvesting would be carried out throughout the year
with the exception of the months of March and April, when rainfall is too
high for proper harvesting.
2.        At the nucleus estate, the cane would be burned prior to harvesting.
In the outgrowers' area, however, no such burning would be carried out or even
allowed in order to avoid deliberate burning by the farmers and consequent
irregularities in the harvesting program. Harvesting of burnt cane is
easier than harvesting of fresh cane; therefore, the cutting capacity for
burnt cane, based on experience in Chemelil and other cane-growing areas is
estimated at 2.5 t/day per laborer, including stacking in the field. In the
outgrowers area, the cutting capacity would be 1.25 t/day per laborer. Cane
knives (machetes) would be provided for all cane cutters. As it is assumed
that all cane would be cut by factory laborers the number of knives required
would increase from 1,375 in Year 4 to over 2,450 in Year 13 (including 10%
as reserve).
3.        There would be roughly 265 effective milling days per year (Annex
10); the initial capacity of the factory would be 2,000 tons cane/day (TCD);
if extended in Years 7-9 the capacity would increase to 3,000 TCD. The daily
tonnage of cane to be harvested in the nucleus estate and the outgrowers area,
the number of harvesting days, the annual tonnage harvested and the number of
laborers required are presented in Table 1. The daily tonnage to be harvested
is based on full factory capacity. The temporary reduction in milling days
in Years 7-8 shown in Table 1 is due to the assumed expansion in milling
capacity in those years, as with a 2,000 TCD capacity and 265 milling days,
i.e., 530,000 t theoretical annual capacity, the expected production of
559,000 t could not be processed. The actual phasing of factory expansion
would be determined by the Company's experience with cane production, crushing
performance of the installed mills, and effective number of annual operating
days.
Equipment Requirements
4.        Cane would be transported in various ways.  Hauling the cane out
of the fields would be done either by 3-ton self-loading and self-off load-
ing trailers or, if the soil conditions allow, by tandems of 8-ton Hilo
trailers; transloading would be needed if self-loading trailers were used.
The appraisal report is based on the assumption that 50% of the cane could
be transported without transloading; a more precise estimate could be made
only after completion of the soil survey and used as a basis for determining
the proper mix of transport equipment.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
5.        Nucleus Estate.  The cane would be hauled out of the fields in two
shifts of 6 hours. A round-trip for a self-loading trailer between the
field and the transloading station is estimated to average 20 to 25 minutes
(loading, 5-10 minutes; driving, 10 minutes; off-loading, 5 minutes); this
would permit 30 round-trips per day per tractor. A round-trip of a tandem
of two Hilo trailers between the field or transloading station and the
factory is estimated at 90 minutes (loading, 20 minutes; driving, 40 minutes;
off-loading 30 minutes); this would make 8 round-trips per tandem possible.
The required number of tractors, self-loading trailers and Hilo trailers in
each year is listed in Table 2.
6.        Outgrowers Area.  As would be the case in the nucleus estate, two
shifts of 6 hours each would be organized for cane haulage. A round-trip
of a self-loading trailer is estimated at 30 minutes; this slightly longer
time takes into account the somewhat lower quality of the roads. In total,
24 round-trips would be possible per day. A round-trip (average 25 km) of
a Hilo tandem is estimated at 140 minutes (driving, 90 minutes; loading, 20
minutes; off-loading, 30 minutes), making 5 trips per day possible.  The
number of tractors, self-loading trailers and Hilo trailers required each
year is listed in Table 3.
7.        Each self-loading trailer would be drawn by a 45/50 H.P. wheel
tractor, a tandem of two Hilo trailers by a 65/90 H.P. wheel tractor.  In
the nucleus estate, harvesting generally would be carried out at two sepa-
rate locations throughout the harvesting season; in the outgrowers area,
the number of locations would increase from 2 in Year 5, to 3 in Years 6,
7, and 8, and to 4 from Year 9 onwards. At each location at least two
flex-boom loaders mounted on wheel tractors (capacity 22.5 t/hour) would
be required; one loader would be stationed in the field for direct loading
of Iilo trailers, and one at the transloading station. Also required would
be one 65/90 H.P. wheel tractor equipped with a winch for pulling out
bogged-down tractors. These assumptions imply an eventual annual factory
capacity of 850,000 tons of sugarcane per year.
Labor Requirements for Harvesting and Transport
8.        The Company would conduct the entire process of harvesting, handling
and transporting of the cane in order to make the process as efficient as
possible. The outgrowers area would also be harvested by factory-employed
laborers. This system has been implemented in Mumias, where outgrowers fre-
quently participate in harvesting their own cane fields while being temporarily
employed as laborers by the factory. The cost of providing these services
would be fully recovered by the Company (Annex 20). The number of laborers
employed in cane cutting is included in Table 1; these laborers would be em-
ployed for 10 months each year. The number of tractor drivers and turn boys,
each assisting three tractor drivers, required for the two daily shifts is
included in Tables 2 and 3.
January 20, 1977
