KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
TURNKEY FACTORY CONTRACT
1.        A contract was signed on July 15th, 1976, providing for the supply,
erection and commissioning of a sugar factory complex at Awendo. The parties
to the agreement were The Mehta Group International Ltd., Maschinenfabrik
Buckau R. Wolf Aktiengesellschaft, Buckau Wolf New India Engineering Works
Ltd., and Agrima Project Engineering and Consultancy Services, Ltd., (these
four parties collectively being the Seller), the South Nyanza Sugar Company,
Ltd. (the Buyer) and the Government of the Republic of Kenya (guarantor of the
Buyer's performance). The main provisions of the contract are given below.
2.        The Seller is to erect a factory and related facilities for the
manufacture of mill-white sugar by the double sulphitation process. The
factory is to have the capacity to crush 2,000 tons of sugar in a 22 hour
working day, and is to be capable of expansion to a crushing capacity of 3,000
tons in a 22 hour working day. The Seller is to be responsible for the
carrying out of all the civil works necesary for completion of the factory,
employing local firms and workers where possible, and obtaining competitive
quotations for the supply of materials and performance of works other than
works and machinery erection to be done by the Seller or foreign sub-
contractors.
3.        The contract price is 75,584,621 Deutschmarks (DM).  Of this DM
64,381,496 is payable in foreign currency, and the remaining DM 11,203,125
is payable in Kenyan currency, at the rate of exchange ruling at the date
of the agreement to the Mehta Group. No price revision provisions are
applicable to that part of the contract price payable in foreign exchange.
The amount payable in Kenyan currency is subject to revision of changes
in the costs of materials, wages, transport and taxes. The calculation of
such price revisions is to be made by a firm of International Chartered
Accountants with an office in Nairobi, and is to be binding on all parties.
4.        All taxes, duties, fees, impositions or other revenue charges
raised now or in the future by the Kenyan Government or any of its agencies
(including those on the importation into Kenya of machinery, goods and
materials required for factory construction) are to be borne by the Buyer.
Exceptions are duties, taxes and fees on goods or services procured within
Kenya, and income and profits taxes payable by local firms or persons.
5.        An initial payment of 10% of the foreign currency element of the
contract price is payable as a condition precedent to the Effective Date
of the contract, i.e. October 15, 1976 (para. 7). A further 10% is payable
pro-rata to the F.O.B. value of shipments by irrevocable, transferable and
divisible letters of credit to be opened by the Buyer within 12 weeks of the
date of the contract. The remaining 80% of the foreign cost element is
payable with interest, at 8% net of tax (calculated from the twelfth month
after the Effective Date of the contract) in half yearly installments. The



ANNEX 9
Page 2
first installment of the principal is payable 42 months after the Effective
Date of the contract, and the first installment of interest is payable 36
months after the Effective Date. These installments are to be secured by
promissory notes lodged with the designated banks within 12 weeks of the date
of signing of the contract with irrevocable instructions that they should be
handed to the Seller as provided for in the contract. The contract provides
that the promissory note in respect of an installment of the principal amount
is to be handed over when the sum of the FOB value of shipments (corrected by
a factor given in the contract) equals or exceeds the amount of the promissory
note. The promissory notes for interest are to be handed over simultaneously
with the corresponding promissory notes for installments of the principal.
Thus, after the shipment of all the goods needed for the factory, the Seller
is to be in possession of all promissory notes for principal and interest for
the foreign exchange element of the contract. It is understood that the
Seller may discount, assign, transfer or otherwise deal with the promissory
notes before their actual due date.
6.        An initial payment of 10% of the Kenyan currency element of the
contract price is to be paid to the Mehta Group as a condition precedent to
the Effective Date of the contract. The balance of 90% is to be paid quar-
terly in nine equal installments, the first installment being due 3 months
after the Effective Date. The installments are to be secured by promissory
notes issued in Kenyan currency drawn by the Buyer in favor of Mehta, and
lodged with a bank in Kenya to be nominated by Mehta with irrevocable in-
structions that they are to be handed over to Mehta on their respective due
dates.
7.        The contract became effective on October 15, 1976 when the contract
had been signed and approved by all parties, the precedent conditions relat-
ing to initial payments, letters of credit and promissory notes had been met,
and a finance agreement had been completed between the Buyer (as borrower)
and a financial institution in the Federal Republic of Germany (as lender)
for the credit financing of 80% of the cost of supply of Buckau Germany.
8.        The Completion Period of the factory is 36 months following the
Effective Date of the contract, and during this period the plant is to be
erected and commissioned. The Buyer, within 4 weeks of the Effective Date of
the contract, is to put the Seller in vacant possession of the site for the
factory and related facilities, and is to indemnify the Seller against claims
by other persons to rights in the sites. The Buyer is to assist the Seller,
wherever necessary, to procure immigration permits, water rights, construc-
tion permits and all other licences and approvals required for completion of
the factory. The Buyer is also to assist in procuring the prompt issue, as
and when needed, of import licenses and approvals for machinery, goods and
equipment required for the factory, except where such goods and materials
are produced in Kenya at competitive prices, and to meet the Seller's re-
quirements as to quality, quantity and delivery dates.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
9.        All machinery for the factory is to be manufactured in accordance
with the best standards of the Federal Republic of Germany, India and the
United States. No second-hand equipment or parts are to be supplied. The
Buyer is entitled, during the whole period of manufacture, packing and
shipment, to have the quality of the machinery inspected by a consultant,
retained at the Buyer's expense.
10.       When the installation of the plant is complete, the Seller is to
give 14 days written notice to the Buyer. The expiry date of this notice is
to be the Operational Date, unless there is a substantial defect or omission
in the plant, in which case the defect or omission must be made good and
further notice given as before. Within three months after the Operational
Date, the Seller is to give notice to the Buyer that sugarcane has to be
supplied. The Buyer, within 14 days, is to provide sufficient supplies of
sugarcane to enable the Seller to carry out serviceability tests. On
completion of the serviceability tests, the Seller is to give 7 days notice
to the Buyer for the provision of a sufficient number of skilled personnel
and a sufficient quantity of cane to commission the plant. The date of
expiration of this notice period is to be the Commissioning Date, and from
that date the Buyer is to assume control of and responsibility for the plant.
I1.       Machinery supplied by the seller is to be guaranteed for a period
of 12 months from the Operational Date against all defects in design and
manufacture, and the Seller is to replace as far as necessary all machinery
and components which during this 12-month period become unserviceable due
to defective design or manufacture or as a result of faulty installation.
During the guarantee period, the machinery and plant is to be maintained by
adequately skilled personnel employed by the Buyer in a proper way, and in
accordance with the Seller's instructions.
12.       The Seller is to warrant that the Plant will be capable of process-
ing 2,000 tons of cane per 22 hour day. This warranty is to apply only when
the cane has a fiber content not exceeding 15%. In the event that the fiber
content exceeds 15%, the daily tonnage is to be reduced proportionately to
the excess of fiber content. The Seller is also to warrant that the milling
machinery will extract at least 94% of the cane sugar content, based on a
12.5% fiber content and a ratio of Pol % cane to Fiber % cane of at least
0.9, taking into consideration an imbibition rate of at least equal to twice
the fiber percentage. These warranties are to be applicable only if a
continuous and regular supply of good cane is available, if a regular supply
of all products and raw materials required for plant operation is available,
and if plant and machinery is properly operated and maintained.
13.       In order to ascertain the fulfillment of the output and extraction
warranties, the following steps are to be taken. Within three months of the
Commissioning Date, on a date specified by one week's written notice by the
Seller, the Buyer is to carry out, under the supervision of the Seller's
specialist personnel, full testing of the plant for a period of 48 continuous
hours. The output and extraction warranties are to be deemed fulfilled if,
during that period of 48 hours (or during any such subsequent period if the



ANNEX 9
Page 4
warranted performance is not achieved in the first period), the specified
quantity of production is produced on an average basis, the warranted ex-
traction percentage is achieved and mill-white sugar is produced throughout
the period. For the purposes of the tests, stoppages or slowdowns not im-
putable to the Seller are not to be taken into account.
14.       In the event that the Operational Date is later than the expiry of
the Completion Period, the Seller is to be liable to pay damages of 1/4% of
the price of the plant for each and every complete period of 14 days that the
Operational Date is beyond the Completion Period. If the Seller is unable to
fulfill the warranties as to output and extraction within 3 months from the
date when the Buyer complies with the conditions set out in para. 12, the
Seller is to be liable to pay damages. For any shortfall in production, the
Seller is to pay 0.1% of the price of the plant for each 100 metric tons by
which the actual production falls short of warranted production. For an
extraction rate less than 94%, the Seller is to pay 0.1% for each 0.5% by
which the actual extraction rate falls short of the warranted rate. The
total damages payable are not to exceed 5% of the purchase price of the
plant.  If the Buyer is unable to fulfill the conditions in para. 12 within
3 months of the Commissioning Date, the Seller shall not be required to pay
damages in respect of the output and extraction warranties.
15.       On the Operational Date, the Seller is to deliver to the Buyer a
guarantee by designated banks that the Seller will meet the obligations under
the agreement, provided that such liabilities are less than 5% of the purchase
price.

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
SUGAR FACTORY
LOCATION AND SUGAR MANUFACTURING PROCESS
A. Sugar Factory Location
1.        The proposed factory site would be in the center of the nucleus
estate and the outgrowers area to minimize the transport requirements for
sugarcane. The site would be about 48 km south of Kisii and approximately
960 meters north-northeast of the Sare river bridge across the A-1 National
highway. It would occupy a slightly sloping area between contour altitudes
of 1,430-1,450 m and be at a distance of about 120 m from the Sare river.
The factory compound would be about 175 m in length and 145 m wide, with all
manufacturing facilities located within its premises.
2.        Orientation.  The prevailing wind direction would govern the
location of the smoke stack in relation to the surrounding installations.
Despite the use of fly-ash arresting equipment, the constant dispersion of
the minimal amount of escaping ashes and the odor of smoke would cause dif-
ficulties detrimental to factory operation, apart from the annoyance to
nearby housing areas. About 70% of the year the wind blows from a south-
easterly direction. Accordingly, the orientation of the whole complex would
have the chimney located on the southeast side of the factory and would thus
prevent contamination of sugar with ashes at the exposed handling system.
Because of only minimal precipitation of ash dust in the factory the hazard
of fire would be reduced, and a high degree of cleanliness would be achieved.
The housing facilities would be established on the up-wind side of the
factory.
3.        Water Supply.  Although3the factory would have a recirculation
cooling water system, the 2,000 m per hour of water that sugar processing
would demand at an operating level of 2,500 TCD would need substantial
replenishment capacity, arising from losses and the progressive fouling of
the recirculating water. The availability of surface water supply therefore
becomes a vital factor in the selection of facto5y site location. The Sare
river has a miniium flow volume of about 0.112 m per second. This is equiv-
alent to 403.2 m , or 403,200 litres per hour, and would be adequate to sup-
ply the factory requirements. The river water would need simple fluming for
delivery to the factory water system. For acquiring the required gradient,
river pumps would be supplied for lifting the water to the delivery channels.
4.        Elevation.  Factory floor elevation would be at a contour level
of 1,434 m and the Sare river bed is at 1,424 m, leaving a 10 m elevation
difference. The possibility of factory flooding by river water seems remote.
In order to eliminate possible hazards from heavy rain waters cascading from
the hill towards the river and through the factory site, an adequate drainage
system would be constructed.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
5.        Geological Conditions.  A geological survey of the site was limited
to several test bores. Pits were dug at several locations on the proposed
factory sites. The soil samples show that the subsoil composition is loose,
brown, silty clay to a depth of one meter and below, w1ich is lightly plastic.
The compression strength of unconfined soil is 20 t/m as determined by tests
at the depth of one and one-half meters. Tests further indicated that the
strength of the soil rapidly decreases when soaked with water, indicating a
less static subsoil condition. The riverbed is black granite, revealing the
presence of a hard pan at the level of 1,424 m. The critical foundation
would thus have to be designed with a wide slab for distribution of load on
the supporting soil above the hard pan.
6.        Transport Access.  The proposed factory location would be about 600
m from the A-1 National highway, and relatively easily accessible even at the
initial stages of site preparation. The approach and the site, however, are
on a gradual slope. In consideration of the expected traffic volume, the
access road would have to be designed to minimize the slope up to the factory
site to limit prime mover tractive power losses.
7.        Environmental Impact.  A sugar factory, being a food manufacturing
plant, employs relatively few chemicals in its process. Industrial lime, the
most extensively used chemical, is disposed with the filter mud. Possessing
considerable properties as fertilizer, this mud can be applied in the cane
fields. The effluents of the factory are more of an annoyance rather than a
pollutant. Nevertheless, equipment and facilities would be provided for
controlling the effluents. Air pollution would be from the unburned bagasse
dusts or fly ashes that would be carried by the combustion gases. Cyclone-
type fly ash collectors would be incorporated in the boiler supply for
arresting the bagasse dust from smoke. These ashes would also be disposed of
as fertilizer. The contamination in cooling water would be the entrained
sugar solution in the condensed vapors of the processing vessels. Equipment
for separating the sugar solution in vapor would be supplied with the evap-
orator and vacuum pans. But there would be the constant leaks of sugar
solution out of equipment and piping systems which would accumulate in the
factory drains; facilities would be provided for the treatment of this waste
water. The treatment plant would consist of a mixing chamber and a series of
evaporation ponds.
B. The Sugar Manufacturing Process
8.        Factory Capacity.  The principal factor determining the capacity
of the factory would be as follows:
a)   Number of Milling Days.  These are the net operational
days in one year taking into account the effective working
days, holidays and seasonal repairs. The calculation is
as follows:



ANNEX 10
Page 3
Number of weeks per year                  52 weeks
Number of repair weeks per season          8 weeks
Available weeks                           44 weeks
Effective milling days per
week (Based on the normal
18-hour stoppage for week-
end repairs and cleaning)               6.25 days
Available milling days
= 44 x 6.25                               275 days
Holidays per year 1/                         10 days
Net operational days                        265 days
Actual experience in Kenya indicates that, with fewer shut-
downs for repairs and favorable weather, up to 300 net
operational days can be achieved. Project evaluation is,
therefore, based on the possibility of an extended mill-
ing season with a maximum of 285 days in a peak year
(Annex 8).
b)   Cane Production Schedule.  The cane production schedule
for the Project is shown in Annex 7, Table 2. The fac-
tory and the Company's agricultural operations would be
planned such that up to 2,000 t of cane could be processed
per day up to about Years 7-9, and 3,000 t thereafter.
9.        Cane Handling System.  Cane transport would be by a tractor-trailer
system consisting of units of a tractor and two 8-ton trailers (Annex 8).
Trailers would be weighed for determining the cane load before entering
the unloading area. The unloading would be either by the hoist or by the
crane. The slings, upon which the cane is piled, would be lifted with the
cane out of the trailer hold and either dumped into the factory feeder car-
rier or stock-piled in the gantry area. The feeder table would be installed
with the discharging end elevated and would have a revolving kicker. The
feeder table would also be equipped with a speed-regulated drive for attain-
ing a more uniform and even loading of the feeder carrier. Likewise, the
feeder carrier would have similar features for further maintaining an even
feeding into the first cane preparatory equipment.
10.       Cane Preparation and Extraction.  Cane juices of high sugar content
are held in cells which are enclosed by fibrous walls. Breaking these walls
1/   It is assumed that Company employees would observe all holidays.



ANNEX 10
Page 4
for the displacement of juices is performed by the initial shredding of the
cane stalks, thence by the traditional series of crushing. The revolving
knifing set and the defiberizer would prepare the cane into a shredded mass
which would pass through the mills for the extraction of the juices. To
assist in maximizing the squeezing of the juices from cane, water would be
added to reduce the sugar content in the juice that would be reabsorbed by
the fibers during the passage through the last crushing.
11.       In the milling tandem of four mills, the first two mills would
extract the more concentrated juices. Termed as mixed juice (raw juice), it
would be pumped directly into processing. Low in sugar, the juice from the
succeeding mill would be recirculated to the preceding mill for soaking the
fibers under the principle of lixiviation or maceration. Likewise, the juice
from the last mill would be returned to the preceding mill. Water would be
applied as maceration to the fibers entering the last mill for diluting the
reabsorbed sugar solution in the fibers or bagasse.
12.       Juice Heating and Liming Method.  The mixed juice as it comes from
the milling plant contains suspended matters such as fine bagasse (bagacillo),
gums, albumin, wax, coloring matter, and particles of clay, sand, etc. Sub-
jected to heat to a temperature of about 102-104 0C and dozed with milk of
lime, most of these impurities are caused to coagulate and precipitate.
Mixed juice is treated with lime prior to heating which reduces the incidence
of inversion of succrose into non-sugar. A retention period of at least 20
minutes is essential for the lime to completely combine in the chemical
reaction with the acids, phosphates and impurities of the juice. Otherwise,
the uncombined lime would precipitate on the tub walls of the juice heaters
and evaporators as scales, thereby diminishing the heat transfer performance
of the affected surface. Hence, there is need for a large mixing tank in the
process employing the simple defecation.
13.       Sulphitation Process.  The production of mill-white sugar would be
by the doublesulphitation process with the mixed juice undergoing fractional
heating.  From the first heating to about 770C, the mixed juice would be
limed and sulphitated to the required pH range of 7 to 7.1 in a continuous
flow system. This treated juice would then be heated to the boiling tempera-
ture of about 102 0C for decantation in the continuous clarifier.
14.       Clarifier and Vacuum Filter.  The continuous separation of the
clear juice from the mud would take place in the clarifier. The precipitate
mud would be pump-withdrawn from respective compartments of the clarifier and
would be filtered in a continuous revolving drum-type vacuum filter. The
separated filtrate would be recirculated with mixed juice and the filtered
mud disposed by means of a belt conveyor into dump trucks for delivery to
cane fields as fertilizer.
15.       Evaporating Station.  The clear juice withdrawn from the clarifier
would be pumped into the evaporating station. In a multi-effect evaporator
the clear juice or clarified juice would be concentrated through a series of



ANNEX 10
Page 5
boiling and evaporation into syrup with a consistency of about 65% or 690
brix. 1/ The second sulphitation treatment would be applied to the syrup in
the continuous mixing chamber where SO2 gas would be introduced at the bottom
for counter-current contact with the syrup.
16.       Sugar Boiling and Crystallization.  The sulphitated syrup would be
delivered to the pan floor for boiling into the three massecuite system. The
first of this system is the "A" massecuite 2/ which would be boiled from the
"A" strike. 3/ The "A" strike would be developed from a footing of seed magma
on syrup and light ("A") molasses from the second curing of "A" massecuite.
The strike would be dropped as "A" massecuite and would have an average
purity of 86-88. The second massecuite would be the "B" which would be
boiled as "B" strike. The cut-over grain from the grain strike would be
developed on "A" heavy molasses, which would be from the first curing of "A"
massecuite. The "A" heavy molasses would be washed prior to boiling and
building up of "B" massecuite. For the third or "C" massecuite, the strike
would start with graining on the mixture of syrup and "A" heavy molasses to
a purity of about 760.  The boiling would develop the grain which would be
hardened with hot water and syrup. The grain would be divided in which the
first cutting would be transferred to form the footing of "B" strike as
mentioned above. Only the required portion would be left for building up the
"C" strike on "A" heavy molasses and would be dropped as "C" massecuite. The
excess grain would be transferred to a storage crystallizer for use on
subsequent boilings.
17.       Curing and Purging Process.  The "A" massecuite would undergo
double curings on the centrifugal machines. On the first, the "A" heavy
molasses derived would be pumped to the pan for building up the "B" and "C"
strikes as described above. Similarly, the "B" massecuite would have double
purgings on the centrifugal machines. "B" heavy molasses would be separated
on the first curing and would also be pumped to the pan floor for boiling the
"C" strike. The sugars from the first purging of "A" and "B" massecuite
would be combined and made into a magma for the second curing. The purging
centrifugal machines for the "A" and "B" magma would be the batch-type, fully
automatic units which would be equipped for hot water washing, steaming, etc.
This curing would produce the commercial grade mill-white sugar. The separated
"A" light molasses, as mentioned earlier, would be used for developing the
first strike.
1/   Brix - percent by weight of solid matter as indicated by a hydrometer
graduated in degree brix.
2/   Massecuite - the mixture of crystals and mother liquor or molasses,
discharged from the vacuum pan.
3/   Strike - the mixture of syrup of high grade molasses with seed or
crystal in the process of boiling the massecuite in the pan.



ANNEX 10
Page 6
18.       From the pan, the "C" massecuite would be discharged into the
crystallizers for the required cooling. The crystallizers would be the
water-cooled type with the pipe cooling elements. During the cooling period,
the "C" massecuite would be supersaturated and thus would gain further
crystallization from the mother liquor. Properly cooled, the "C" massecuite
would also be double cured. The derived "C" light molasses would be brought
to the pan floor for "C" strike boiling. The first sugar would be made into
magma and would subsequently be purged. The curing centrifugals would be the
continuous-type centrifugal. The second cured "C" sugar would be remelted
and/or would be used as seed for footing of the "A" strike as described above.
As for the molasses so derived and termed final molasses, it would be weighed
and pumped to tanks for commercial disposal.
19.       Sugar Handling and Storage.  The sugar would be conveyed from the
centrifugal station to the wet-sugar elevator. A grasshopper conveyor would
be used which is standard equipment for commercial sugar handling at the
centrifugal station. Prior to delivery into the warehouse, the sugar would
be dried and cooled, and subsequently weighed in 100 kg gunny bags.
February 20, 1977

MANAGEMENT AGREEMENT
1.        Under an agreement (Agreement) between the South Nyanza Sugar Com-
pany Limited (SNSC) and The Mehta Group Internation Limited (Mehta), signed
on July 15, 1976, Mehta was appointed "Manager and Technical Adviser" of
SNSC. SNSC, incorporated in the Republic of Kenya has its registered office
at the Ministry of Agriculture, Kilimo House, Nairobi. Mehta, incorporated
in Bermuda Islands, has its registered office at Argus Insurance Building,
Wesley Street, P. 0. Box 2016, Hamilton.
Terms and Conditions
2.        The following is a synopsis of the agreed terms and conditions:
(a) the Agreement is for the duration of ten years,
commencing with the date of signing; 1/
(b) Mehta is obliged to provide competent and efficient
management for SNSC and perform the services stated
in Schedule A (para. 3);
(c) Mehta may arrange visits of specialist advisors with-
out prior approval of SNSC;
(d) the Chairman of Mehta is appointed Director, without
remuneration, of SNSC;
(e) Mehta's remuneration is as follows:
(i) annual fee of US$135,000 up to the commis-
sioning date of the factory (3 years);
(ii) annual fee of US$99,000 after commission-
ing of the factory; plus 5% of operating
profits of SNSC before depreciation, in-
terest and taxes, subject to a minimum of
US$46,800 per year;
(iii) provision (ii) above is to be reviewed after
3 years from date of signing;
1/   Effectiveness was conditional upon the signing of another agreement
for the supply, erection and commissioning of the sugar factory;
that agreement was signed concurrently with the management agreement.

(iii) attending to all matters concerning the conduct
of the SNSC including day to day management and
statutory requirements;
(iv)  instituting a system for the warehousing, marketing
and distribution of the products of the SNSC;

Page 5
(v) planning and control of all purchases to secure
the most advantageous terms, optimum inventory
levels and uninterrupted production;
(vi) institution and maintenance of operating, financial
and management accounting systems, reporting pro-
cedures and controls;
(vii) formulating and implementing, with the approval of
the Board, management policy with the objective of
achieving a high level of performance in all depart-
ments;
(viii) submitting to the Board for its approval annual
capital and revenue budgets;
(ix) submitting periodically to the Board all such
reports and statements (including financial

(x) operating on behalf of the SNSC all bank accounts
as may be necessary in connection with its busi-
ness;
(xi) effecting on behalf of SNSC all insurances as are
considered necessary;
(xii) generally supervising the upkeep, maintenance
and timely replacement of all SNSC assets.

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
PROJECT AREA ROADS
1.        In order to implement the Project and operate the Company effi-
ciently, a good road infrastructure for the transport of inputs such as
seedcane; planting, weeding, fertilizing, and harvesting; and for the trans-
port of harvested cane, processed sugar, and sugar by-products is essential.
Towards this objective, a number of non-classified and classified roads
would be improved or newly constructed to reach the plantations within the
nucleus estate and the numerous outgrowers plots extending up to, or slightly
beyond, 16 km from the factory.
2.        A detailed ground study including existing road conditions, drain-
age requirements, possible realignments and general soil types has been com-
pleted, covering about 53,000 ha of the proposed Project area located within
a radius of 13 km from the factory site. Based on the results of the study,
construction of the following roads is recommended:
Non-classified      Classified
(km)
Access tracks                          350.0
Access roads                           150.0              40.8
Feeder roads                           80.0               31.5
Collection roads                        18.0              14.0
Factory access road                      1.2
Total               599.2              86.3
Since the proposed Project area excludes some of the originally surveyed area,
particularly around the western fringe inside the 13 km radius, and includes
as yet unsurveyed areas up to, or slightly beyond, the 16 km radius in the
northeast and south (Miap IBRD 12344) an allowance has been made in the cost
estimates for the survey and construction of an additional 50 km of access
tracks. The proposed construction program thus comprises a total road dis-
tance of 735.5 km. The recommended standard of the roads varies, with access
tracks having the lowest specifications and collection roads generally the
highest. The factory access road, connecting the A-1 National highway with
the factory, would be of exceptionally high standard to enable it to carry the
anticipated traffic of 456 vehicles (mostly cane transport) per day. There
would be one bridge crossing the A-1. A breakdown of the roads by construction
standard is shown in Table 1. About 188 km of non-classified roads and
tracks, located on the nucleus estate would be owned and maintained by the
sugar company, consisting of:  access roads, 26 km; feeder roads, 41 km;
access tracks, 120 km; and the factory access road, 1.2 km. The resulting
cost to the Company would be KShs. 15.2 million (Table 2).



ANiNEX 12
Page 2
3.        The function of access tracks would be to link individual cane
plots with the road network. In most instances, the tracks would connect
with access roads, although some plots would be close enough to higher-
standard roads to be linked directly. Access roads would form suitable
links between access tracks and the higher-standard feeder roads and com-
prise the lowest standard of road. Feeder roads, in turn, would connect
access roads with collection roads, which provide direct access to the fac-
tory. Map IBRD 12344 depicts the proposed network with the exception of
access tracks, whose location would be determined in the course of develop-
ment of the nucleus estate and outgrowers' cane fields. The following would
be major factors to be considered in the alignment of the various road net-
work components:
(a) the topography of the area;
(b) the location of the factory site;
(c) the utilization of existing roads wherever possible;
except
(d) a preference to avoid use of the A-1 National highway
for cane transportation;
(e) the transportation system to be adopted for trans-
porting cane to the factory;
(f) the requirement for the gradient of the roads not to
exceed 10%; and
(g) the recommendation for the roads or tracks to pass
within a maximum distance of 2.5 km from each cane
plot, and to average around 1-1.5 km.
4.        The general topography of the area is a gently rolling series of
ridges and valleys, forming an intensive natural drainage system which would
require provision for frequent watercourse crossings. The area is traversed
by three major rivers, the Gucha, Sare and Osani, all of which flow from
southeast to northwest into Lake Victoria. The major rivers are perennial
while the majority of the numerous minor streams is seasonal. Although road
alignments would be determined so as to reduce the number of crossings, the
construction of at least two new crossings of both the Osani and Sare rivers
would be necessary. The latter would give direct access to the factory.
Numerous culverts would be required to accommodate both road cross-drainage
and minor streams. The soil structure is generally a catenary which varies
in a predictable manner with height above streams or river level.
5.        The A-1 highway, linking Kisumu via Kisii to Suna near the Tanzania
border, is a bitumen road which was constructed in 1967 for speeds of 80-100
km/hour. In addition, there are some fairly gcod existing roads mainly in



ANNEX 12
Page 3
the outgrowers area, which could be satisfactorily used by incorporating them
into the proposed network. Some extension and upgrading of these roads would
be required to ensure year-round usage. As, to the extent possible, tractor-
trailer units would not be permitted to use the A-i highway, a crossing would
have to be provided either by an underpass or a bridge. Extensive foundation
investigations would be necessary before a final choice is made, although
an underpass, while more expensive, would be preferable from an operational
point of view.
6.        With the development of the Project, general economic growth of
the area would be stimulated and considerable secondary traffic generated.
The latter is estimated at 15% of normal traffic per year for light vehicles
and 10% per year for heavy vehicles up to two years after completion of the
road network in the vicinity of Awendo. All traffic would increase by an
average of 10% per year on the A-1 highway. The Ministry of Works (Roads)
estimates that the heaviest traffic load would be on the Awendo-Rongo section
of the A-1 highway and be as follows:
Class of Vehicle                  Vehicles per day by 1981
Cars                                   370
Light goods                             630
Medium goods                             90
Heavy goods                              68
Buses                                  200
1,358
These estimates are based on the anticipated use of 10-t trucks for the trans-
port of sugar and molasses and include supply vehicles to the factory as well
as the growth in private car ownership. Considering such heavy traffic, the
Ministry of Works expects that once the factory is in full operation, a study
for the possible strengthening of the A-1 highway would have to be undertaken.
7.        All bitumen roads, gravel roads involving large quantities of
earthwork and major structures would be the responsibility of the Mlinistry of
Works and carried out on a contract basis as these operations would require
the use of specialized equipment and manpower. There is a sufficiently large
construction industry with idle capacity in the country to assure proper
construction at competitive prices. Other construction work would be the
responsibility of the sugar company and undertaken mainly by the Company's
Road Construction and Maintenance Unit.   The establishment of such a unit
within the sugar company is proposed because (a) the exact alignment of access
tracks and access roads can only be determined after the selection of farmers
and soil surveys on their land have been completed, which would be a gradual
process extending over a number of years and make tendering difficult, (b)
these stretches are scattered and close coordination between the agricultural



ANNEX 12
Page 4
activities and the construction of access roads and tracks is eeded, and (c)
involvement of the Company in construction and subsequent assumptic. by the
Company of maintenance responsibilities is expected to result in superior
maintenance compared with alternative arrangements. To the extent that the
Company could not implement its share of the roads construction program with
its own construction unit, it would also employ local contractors. Loans for
the roads construction program from the World Bank and the African Development
Bank would be disbursed to Government, and Government would use the funds
to pay private contractors and the Company for their services and on-lend to
the Company that portion related to the construction of roads on the nucleus
estate to be owned by the Company. Approximately 10% of the roads construction
program would be completed in Year 1, 40% each in Years 2 and 3, and 10% in
Year 4.
8.        It is anticipated that the roads and tracks constructed by the
Company would be maintained by the Company, with the remainder being the
responsibility of the Ministry of Works. It would be preferable for the
Company to own the roads construction equipment to facilitate timely replace-
ment, although a possible alternative would be for the Ministry of Works to
acquire the equipment and then lend or lease it to the Company. A list of
equipment required by the Company is presented in Table 3. The cost of
construction and maintenance of roads under the Project is included in Annex
15, Table 16.

HOUSING AND SOCIAL AMENITIES
1.        Because of lack of suitable accommodations in the vicinity of Awendo
town near the factory site, and in conformance with Government policy requir-
ing industrial enterprises to house their permanent staff, the Project would
establish a housing compound for Company employees. Six different types of
houses, with architectural designs approved by Government, would be con-
structed as shown below:
Number of                  Designated
Housing Units       Type     Occupants              Description        Living Area
6             A  -  Senior managerial        3 bedrooms plus            2
and technical staff       amenities          138 m
4             B  -  Other managerial         3 bedrooms plus            2
and technical staff       amenities           102 m
35             C  -  Supervising staff        2 bedrooms plus            2
amenities           70 m
57             D  -  Skilled clerical and   2 bedrooms plus              2
technical staff           amenities           56 m
473             E  -  Clerical and semi-       I bedroom plus             2
skilled staff             amenities            27 m
255             F  -  Unskilled laborer        1 bedroom plus             2
830                                              amenities            19 m
This total does not include the General Manager's supergrade residenc&, which
would be additional. The number of units required has been estimated on the
basis of expected employment by the Company. Full housing provision has been
made for all staff to be accommodated in A-D type houses. In arriving at re-
quirements for houses of types E and F, it has been assumed that 10% of the
clerical and semi-skilled staff, and 15% of the permanently employed unskilled
laborers would reside with their families outside the Company's housing com-
pound or jointly occupy Company houses with other staff.
2.        The houses would be built near the factory.  When deciding the lo-
cality, wind direction, which is southeasterly about 70% of the time, would
be taken into account to avoid, to the extent possible, exposure to the smoke
and odors from the factory. The compound would also be provided with street
lighting, water and sewerage facilities, streets, and furniture for houses
of types A-D. The Company would be the owner of all facilities in the com-
pound, while for administrative purposes the community would belong to the



ANNEX 13
Page 2
of types A-D. The Company would be the owner of all facilities in the com-
pound, while for administrative purposes the community would belong to the
township of Awendo. There is a large construction industry in Kenya which
would be capable of implementing this part of the Project under the super-
vision of the Company's Management. Building materials would largely come
from local sources; imports would consist mainly of steel products.
3.        While a large proportion of the Company's plantation workers would
have their own accommodation close to the nucleus estate, a relatively large
number would originally reside beyond reasonable commuting distance. Further-
more, about 500-600 families would be displaced through the development
of the nucleus estate and would have to be resettled; those joining the
work force at the nucleus estate would be without homes. For these laborers,
the Company would establish rondavels in various stratigically located areas
within or adjacent to the nucleus estate for which the amount of KShs. 5.4
million (US$650,000) has been allowed in the Project cost estimates. Under
the proposed site and services approach the Company would arrange for pre-
paration of the sites and provision of necessary infrastructure on a grant
basis. Prospective employees would obtain building materials on credit
from the Company which would bulk-procure them since there are not sufficient
traders in the area. In case employment with the Company is terminated, the
Company would compensate terminees for their input, settle any outstanding
debts and resell the unit on credit to new employees. Alternatively, the
occupant family could remain if payment of all outstanding debts by the family
can be arranged.
4.        Under the Project the Company would also establish a dispensary,
mother-and-child center, Company store, and facilities to conduct social and
sporting activities for Company staff. Minor medical needs of Company em-
ployees and family members of senior staff would be met by a dispensary
equipped with a six-bed general ward, examination tables, an emergency room,
an operating theatre for minor surgery and a sterilizing and dressing room.
The medical staff would consist of one full-time doctor, two nurses and two
nurses' aides. Family members of Company staff at non-senior levels would
have to rely on public health facilities. Although there are health posts in
Awendo and Rongo, most people requiring major medical attention would be
referred to Migori General Hospital. This hospital, 20 km south of Awendo, is
presently being expanded to a 200-bed capacity and can be reached easily
by road. The Project would furnish an ambulance to provide transport free
of charge.
5.        A mother-and-child center for pre-schoolers would be built, consist-
ing of one large and two small classrooms, a kitchen with pantry to provide
snacks during the day as well as a central courtyard with sand pit. The
center would be operated by one teacher with two assistants. The Company
would not engage in operating a primary school as the Maringa Primary School
would have jurisdiction over Project area residents. The Chemelil Sugar
Company contributed funds to the construction of an additional school and if
necessary, the South Nyanza Sugar Company would persue a similar course in



ANNEX 13
Page 3
the future should the Government be unable to provide adequate failities.
For the education of children of internationally recruited staff the Company
would provide financial assistance.
6.        A Company-operated store would be set up to provide a convenient
outlet at reasonable prices for basic food commodities and other essential
items such as clothing and household goods. A cafeteria would be provided
on the factory premises which would be fully self-supporting without Com-
pany subsidies. A social club would provide a suitable place for social
gatherings and Company staff entertainment, such as movies and dances.
Attached to the club would be a football ground and playing fields to stage
tournaments and other sports and athletic events. Administratively, the
medical and social services would be under the responsibility of the Indus-
trial Relations Officer in the Company's Personnel and Industrial Relations
Department who would be supported by two assistants.
7.        In order to facilitate implementation of the Project, the con-
struction of some of the housing facilities as well as other infrastructure
had to commence early, and it is proposed that related expenditures up to
US$20,000 equivalent be eligible for retroactive financing, provided the
Bank's procurement guidelines have been followed. A summary of cost esti-
mates is included in Annex 15, Table 17.

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
STAFF TRAINING
1.        The availability of trained staff has been a constraint in the es-
tablishment of industries in Kenya, and the sugar industry has been no ex-
ception. Despite the training programs instituted by new companies in the
past, existing companies had lost from 30 to 70 skilled staff members each
time a new factory was commissioned, who had to be replaced with less-skilled
personnel. But even with these transfers the new companies had to fill some
positions with untrained employees (Annex 3). In view of the expected rapid
expansion of the sugar industry, it is necessary to embark upon a systematic
and comprehensive training program to satisfy future manpower needs.
Coordination at the National Level
2.        Because of the large number of disciplines and persons involved,
national coordination of the training effort is required. Towards this
objective, the Kenya Sugar Authority would establish the position of Training
Officer with authority to liaise with Government Ministries, the training
officers of the sugar companies, and educational and training institutions and
develop a training program that would meet the industry's long-term manpower
needs. This would require first the completion of a detailed survey of staff
availability in all disciplines employed by the industry. Projected future
needs of existing and new companies would then be aggregated on a year-by-year
basis and matched against likely availabilities. Apparent projected manpower
deficits would provide the basis for the formulation of a master industry
manpower development plan. KSA would submit an acceptable plan to the Bank
before December 31, 1978.  The personnel costs for these activities are
included in Annex 15, Table 20. Draft terms of reference for KSA's Training
Officer are included in Annex 17, and for the Company's Training Officer in
Annex 18.
Training of Company Staff
3.        Of the estimated 920 permanent staff to be employed by the Company,
36 would be initially internationally recruited (Table 1). At least one-half
of all staff would require some form of training. An estimated 320 persons at
different levels, from managerial positions down to skilled or semi-skilled
technicians, would require formal training of varying types preparatory
to the actual start of manufacturing operations. Training programs would
also have to be organized to prepare for an eventual assumption by Kenyans
of managerial and technical positions initially filled by internationally
recruited staff (para. 17). The foregoing programs could be categorized in
terms of a time frame and in terms of the various types of programs for
specific expertise to be developed. The estimated costs of training are shown
in Annex 15, Table 19. For estimating the Company's future costs of training,
it has been assumed that a continuous outlay, equal to 25% of total training
cost for Years 1-3, would be required.



ANNEX 14
Page 2
4.        The training program for Years 1-5 would aim at enabAing Company
staff to start each phase of the various operations as scheduled. fhe
agricultural operations would start early, since sufficient quantity of cane
must be grown in both the nucleus estate and outgrowers' areas to be ready
for harvest at the start of factory operations, tentatively scheduled in
Year 4. Anticipated training requirements for each of the four Company De-
partments are outlined below.
5.        Agricultural Department Training.  Among the staff to be hired
locally at the middle management or technical levels would be the Outgrowers
Division Manager and the Assistant Agronomist. Candidates for these posi-
tions could be available in the existing factories, subject to proper clear-
ance. The training for staff in these posts would be in the nature of "on-
the-job" instruction by internationally recruited staff concerning Company
policies, plans and programs, administrative procedures, and sugar-growing
technology and husbandry. The training would be supplemented by observation
tours to other sugarcane-producing countries, possibly including Mauritius.
Such tours which would take approximately four weeks per country, are sche-
duled for Year 2.
6.        One local surveyor would be hired in Year 1 and three assistant
surveyors each year from Years 2 to 4. The surveyors would be given on-
the-job instruction on the agricultural development program, primarily by
the Agricultural Services Division Manager, who would be an Agricultural
Engineer.
7.        The section supervisors in the Nucleus Estates Division and
Outgrowers Division who would supervise routine agricultural operations
would normally have a degree or diploma in agriculture, with practical
training on farms. One supervisor would be required during Year 1, two
each in Years 2 and 3, and one each in Years 4 and 5. There would be no
difficulty in hiring staff for these positions from agricultural colleges
and, to a limited extent, from the private sector in Kenya. The initial
training programs for section supervisors would be an "in-house" program
to be carried out by the internationally recruited staff on the technical
aspects of cane husbandry, Company policies and administrative procedures and
supervision. Staff below supervising level in the Outgrowers Division,
who together with their supervisors would constitute the agricultural exten-
sion staff, would be drawn from existing extension services in the coun-
try and given individual on-the-job training or be sent to refresher courses
as required.
8.        There would be a total of six supervisors and assistant super-
visors in the Agricultural Services Division. The supervisor for the Con-
struction Section would have a Diploma in Agriculture, with familiarity in
mechanized farming methods. The Harvesting Section and Transport Section
supervisors would also have similar qualifications and be familiar with
transport equipment. Supervisors' training would be handled by the agri-
cultural engineer heading the Division. The Workshop Section supervisor



ANNEX 14
Page 3
would have a diploma in mechanical engineering, with special training and
practical experience in tractor repairs and overhauling, and the maintenance
of automotive vehicles. The candidate required would presently be holding
a responsible position, but not necessarily in the existing sugar factories.
For all supervisors, on-the-job instructions could be supplemented by actual
observations of similar operations in existing factories for about four weeks.
9.        The Department's overseers and headmen would be performing similar
functions, except that the headmen would be relatively junior. They would be
generally in charge of smaller groups of laborers assigned to routine agri-
cultural operations in the field. Their training would be carried out by
the respective Section supervisors with the Division head monitoring the pro-
gress of such training activities. The instruction would include the basic
techniques applicable to their functions, supervisory techniques, and admin-
istrative procedures.
10.       The largest group of employees in the Agricultural Department
that would require training would be the maintenance personnel and drivers or
tractor operators. There would be a total of 26 mechanics, electricians and
other craftsmen employed in Years 1 to 5. Of those, it is assumed that 20%
would have some previous training and practical experience. The remaining
mechanics, electricians, fitters, welders, and blacksmiths would have to
undergo basic training in their respective trades. This would have to be
arranged with the Industrial Training Center (ITC) at Kisumu. Operated by the
Ministry of Labor for manpower development, this Center handles training in
such trades as arc and gas welding, pipe fitting, mechanical fitting, machi-
nists, tools and dies, automotive mechanics, diesel engine mechanics, electri-
cians, etc. The Center could accommodate up to 160 students for 12-week
live-in courses at a cost of KShs. 300 per week, including tuition, board and
lodging. These costs are payable to the ITC, but once the Company starts
production and would be subject to a training fund levy of KShs. 2 per ton of
sugar, future costs of sending trainees to the center would be partially
reimbursed to the Company from this fund.
11.       For a continuous development program up to an annual factory capa-
city of 90,000 t, the total number of 256 drivers to be hired in Years 1-7
would provide for two-shift operations in the Agricultural Department's
transport operations which could last more than ten months of the year during
harvest time, and also meet the requirements of all other Departments of the
Company. Some candidates with driver's training and experience would be
generally available. However, because of the number required, and the pos-
sible non-familiarity of these drivers with Company equipment to be operated,
it would be necessary to have most of these drivers undergo a training pro-
gram. There would be two types of programs. For candidates with driving
skills, the program would consist of a two-week course on familiarization with
the operation of the equipment, preventive maintenance procedures, trouble
shooting, safety procedures, traffic regulations, peculiarities of trailer
operations, and Company administrative procedures. The familiarization with
the equipment possibly could be arranged with the equipment suppliers while
the rest of the instruction would be given by the Transport Section Supervisor.



ANNEX 14
Page 4
The drivers would have to pass written and practical tests to qualify. Can-
didates without prior driving skills would have to undergo similar instruction
as already qualified drivers; in addition, practical instruction would be re-
quired before they could apply for a driver's license. The Ministry of Labor
would provide qualified instructors. It would normally take three weeks for
a learner to acquire sufficient driving skill. Assuming that of the 256 dri-
vers to be recruited, at least 20% would have prior driving experience, the
total number to undergo instruction from Year 1 to 7 would be as follows:
Years       1    2     3      4     5     6      7     Total
To be hired                      10   17    16    99    50    16    48         256

The Department's training requirements are summarized in Table 2.
12.       Factory Department Training.  At the middle management level, the
following would be recruited locally:
I Civil Engineer
1 Assistant Civil Engineer
1 Assistant Electrical Engineer
A degree in the candidates' respective engineering fields would be necessary,
together with practical experience. Extensive experience would be of parti-
cular importance for the Civil Engineer who would take charge of civil engi-
neering maintenance in the factory and be available for consultations on
other Company activities. The experience of these engineers need not be in
a sugar factory. The engineers' training would be handled by the Chief En-
gineer and the Electrical Engineer and include familiarization with plant op-
eration and maintenance, Company policies and administrative procedures. Be-
fore the actual start-up of factory operations, the engineers would undergo
a 12-week apprenticeship program in existing factories.
13.       The Company would require approximately 34 foremen, of which nine
would be internationally recruited and have extensive training and practical
experience in sugar factory operations. These would include three each of
mill foremen, boiler foremen, and crystallization foremen. The mill and
boiler foremen in the Milling Section of the Engineering Division would be
able to train the other foremen in their section and the various machine
operators and tenders, under the supervision of the Shift Enginee"rs.  The
f



ANNEX 14
Page 5
training would be on-the-job instruction on machinery operation. Additional
training assistance on actual machine operation and preventive maintenance
procedures would be available from the machinery suppliers at the start
of operations. The foremen would also undergo an 8-week apprenticeship
program in an existing factory before the Company's own factory starts opera-
tions. The Electrical Engineer would supervise on-the-job training of the
electrician foremen and telephone foremen and of the various machine operators
under these foremen. Electricians, winders, and linemen would undergo basic
training at ITC. The Mechanical Engineer would be responsible for the train-
ing of maintenance foremen and mechanics, turners/lathemen, welders, etc.
which would include a basic training course at an ITC. The construction
foreman in the Civil Engineering Section would have the necessary experience
and training when hired. He would be instructed regarding his responsi-
bilities and duties.
14.       The Processing Division would initially have seven internationally
recruited staff, who would be able to supervise on-the-job training of the
local foremen in the Division and the operators in each Section. The local
foremen would undergo a 12-week apprenticeship program with an existing
factory. The Control Division would be staffed initially with four interna-
tionally recruited experts, namely, the Chief Chemist and three Assistant
Chemists who would train the laboratory technicians. Six laboratory analysts
and three water analysts would undergo a 24-week apprenticeship with an
existing factory.
15.       One set of operators for one shift in each of the Milling and
Electrical Sections of the Engineering Division and the Clarification, Cry-
stallization, Centrifugal, Bagging and Water Supply Stations of the Proces-
sing Division would undergo apprenticeship programs in the existing fac-
tories for varying periods of 12-14 weeks. Details on the Factory Depart-
ment's training requirements are presented in Table 3.
16.       Finance and Administration Department Training.  There would ini-
tially be four internationally recruited staff in this Department, namely, the
Controller, Administrative Officer, Chief Accountant and the Cost Accountant,
who would meet most of the training requirements for local staff hired for the
Department. The staff in the General Accounting and Cost Accounts Division of
the Department would generally have the necessary accounting education back-
grounds, the majority being college graduates of Kenya universities with
majors in accounting subjects. The training program would consist of courses
in administration/finance for a small number of staff members (para. 17),
and on-the-job instruction in verifying, classifying, recording and report-
ing on financial and operational data. The administrative staff in the
Factory Department and Agriculture Department would likewise be included
in the training program of this Department to assure uniformity in admin-
istrative and accounting procedures.
17.       Personnel and Industrial Relations Department Training.  The De-
partment's Training Officer would plan, in cooperation with the Agricultural,
Factory, and Finance and Administration Departments all of the Company's
training programs and monitor the progress of these programs to assure that



ANNEX 14
Page 6
schedules are met, and organize proper arrangements with ITC and o'her sugar
companies for training outside the Company premises. The Personnel and
Industrial Relations Department and the Finance and Administration Department
would jointly nominate ten of their employees for courses of 8-10 weeks in
those disciplines where deficiencies become apparent during recruitment.
Scholarships and Special Programs
18.       The training of top-level staff to replace internationally recruited
staff has high priority in Kenya. Since such staff are expected to transfer
among sugar companies as vacancies arise, related staff development should be
considered outside the sphere of individual companies and the cost borne
either by the industry as a whole, or Government. In estimating Project
costs, provision has been made for a one-year overlap in appointments of
internationally recruited staff and designated local replacements in the Kenya
Sugar Authority and the National Sugar Research Institute. No such provision
has been made explicitely for Company staff as internationally recruited staff
employed there would remain much longer, and replacements would generally be
selected through promotion from the ranks.
19.       In addition to counterpart training by the internationally recruited
experts there would basically be four types of formal programs, specifically
to facilitate Kenyanization of top-level positions and, generally, to build up
a management cadre for the sugar industry, as follows:
Graduate business programs at foreign universities;
Sugar technology programs overseas;
Participation in international symposiums on sugar technology;
In-plant management development programs.
20.       Graduate business programs would involve overseas scholarships of
2-3 years. Mechanical or chemical engineering undergraduate backgrounds
would be preferred, and the candidates chosen on the basis of competitive
examinations, personality, and commitment to work with a sugar company.
Candidates accepted would be subject to their qualifying for admission in
reputable universities abroad. The estimated cost per student is US$10,000
per year including travel, tuition, fees, books and living expenses if ad-
mission is sought in a reputable business school in Western countries. How-
ever, there are universities of internationally accepted standards offering
similar courses at less cost, and the program should preferably include a
range of universities. With an expansion of the program by one student each
in Years 1, 2 and 4 and continuous replacement of returning students, and
assuming one two-year and two three-year cycles, there would be three gradu-
ates available after Year 5, and nine after year 10.
21.       Sugar technology courses normally take four years.  These courses
are offered in several sugarcane producing counries. Supplementary factory
apprenticeship programs would be arranged to provide practical training



ANNEX 14
Page 7
after graduation. Applicants would be at least graduates of high school,
and similar selection criteria as for business programs (para. 20) would be
applied.  The estimated cost is US$9,000 per student per year.  With an in-
phasing of the program of one student each in Years 1, 2 and 3, the return-
ing graduates would number two after five years, and six after ten years.
22.       Participation in symposiums and conventions of sugar technolo-
gists is recommended to keep industry personnel informed of technological de-
velopments.  Such one-week symposiums or conventions are generally held once
a year in all sugarcane producing countries with organizations of sugar tech-
nologists. Two-week international conventions are held once every three
years by the International Society of Sugarcane Technologists. Qualified
agricultural and factory staff would be nominated for participation by the
various sugar companies. The arrangement of scholarships and special programs
would be the responsibility of the Kenya Sugar Authority.
23.       The foregoing programs would be limited to a very small percentage
of total industry staff. To accelerate the upgrading process, it would be
necessary to have separate programs on company premises with broader partici-
pation of staff. There are a number of international consulting firms who
have expertise in improving management capabilities of staff at various
functional management levels. One approach found effective in several de-
veloping countries is a program combining lecture-discussions on principles,
methods and techniques, and supervised practical applications of these in
actual company situations. Towards the end of one-month lecture discussions
on management subjects (organization, production, supervision, management
accounting, and industrial engineering techniques, among others), staff
participants identify specific projects to be undertaken by separate task
forces and monitored by a steering group from upper management levels. These
specific projects focus on current problems of the company. To be effective,
the projects have targets that are measurable over a time frame, such as
reduction of unusually high inventory levels, low sugar processing recovery
rates, etc. Since such a program is not recommended for the South Nyanza
Sugar Company to start before the third year of factory operations, no al-
lowance has been made in Project cost estimates. An estimated 24 man-months
of consultant's time would eventually be required.
February 20, 1977

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
PROJECT COSTS
CONTINGENCIES
The Project Time Frame
1.        The calculations and phasing in the Appraisal report are based on
calendar years; the Project period is 1977-1981. All baseline costs are up-
to-date as of March, 1977.
Physical Contingencies
2.        A 10% contingency has been allowed on all Project costs except the
purchase of nucleus estate land, the sugar factory turnkey contract, the Com-
pany management fee, training not directly related to the Project, and Kenya
Sugar Authority salaries.
Price Contingencies
3.        Price contingency allowances have been applied to all Project
costs except the foreign currency portion of the sugar factory turnkey con-
tract, and the Company management fee for Years 1-3. The allowances are in
accordance with World Bank guidelines on the projected international inflation
pattern, supplemented by the mission's projection of increases in Kenya's
salary levels. The projected annual price increases are as follows:
Vehicles
Civil Works              and Equipment        Salaries
1976                   13%                          9%               10%
1977                   12%                          8%               10%
1978                   12%                          8%               10%
1979                   12%                          8%               10%
1980 onwards           10%                          7%                9%
4.        Based on these annual projections, the compound contingencies, which
have been added to baseline costs, have been calculated as follows:



ANNEX 15
Appendix 1
Page 2
Price Contingencies
Vehicles
Civil Works              and Equipment         Salaries
1977 (9 month)          5%                          3%                 4%
1978                   16%                         10%                13%
1979                   29%                         19%               24%
1980                   44%                         28%               36%
1981                   58%                         37%               48%
The percentages shown for 1977 account for price increases during the period
April - December, 1977. Contingencies for 1978-1981 have been compounded as
of mid-year for each of the respective years; for 1977, the percentages rep-
resent the mid-point of the period April - December.
5.        The "civil works" contingencies have been applied to land clearing
and land preparation operations, drainage works, the local currency component
of the sugar factory turnkey contract, roads, and housing and social amenities.
The "vehicles and equipment" schedule has been applied to vehicles, machinery,
equipment and materials; all recurrent costs, except salaries; agricultural
inputs; cane transport; and land compensation. "Salaries" contingencies have
been added to salaries, the management fee for Years 4-5, and the cost of
training and cane harvesting.
December 30, 1976

ANNEX 17
