KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
PROPOSED ORGANIZATION OF THE KENYA SUGAR AUTHORITY (KSA)
Introduction
1.        The Government's current development plans are directed towards
attaining self-sufficiency in sugar by increasing domestic production at
least cost to its economy, and to encourage small-scale agriculture. Towards
these ends, the Government intends to establish new factories, open up new
areas for cane production, and rehabilitate and expand existing factories and
cane areas. Improvements in the overall industzy organization are imperative
in order to provide the Government with an effective and efficient mechanism
to achieve its objectives (Annex 3).
2.        The Kenya Sugar Authority, as created in 1972, was intended to
be the organization to promote and foster the development of the industry.
However, KSA as constituted in 1973 and as it presently exists, only acts
as an advisory body to the Government in sugar industry development. But
even in this capacity, it does not have the staffing and facilities available
to effectively render the advisory services expected of it. The sugar indus-
try, in the meantime, is continually faced with numerous problems, the re-
medial measures for which require action at the national level. Under the
existing Government structure, the national efforts at rendering assistance
to the industry involve a number of agencies wizh little coordination and
widespread inefficiency.
3.        With the proposed reorganization, the KSA would be the national
body that could effectively provide advisory services to the Government in
all aspects of sugar industry development and, at the same time, be made
responsible for monitoring the implementation o- Government-authorized pro-
grams. The KSA would be revitalized, functioni g as a statutory board, but
with sufficient delegated powers to become an e fective organization for
promoting and fostering the development of the ougar industry. It would be
the bridge between the Government and the various sectors of the industry,
thereby unifying all efforts towards the same national goals. In broad
terms, KSA would advise Government on policies to be formulated and on
plans and programs required to achieve Government objectives, implement or
monitor the implementation of programs authoriz-ed, and compile the neces-
sary information on the progress of programs fo: the Government's informa-
tion and guidance for subsequent action.
Objectives and Functions of KSA
4.        It would be necessary to modify KSA's overall objectives and func-
tions to define its specific role in sugar industry development. These ob-
jectives and functions would be:



ANNEX 17
Page 2
(a) to assist the Government in the comprehensive plann-
ing and formulation of attainable programs of sugar
industry development consistent with overall national
objectives, policies and programs;
(b) to advise Government on the technical, financial and
economic aspects of projects for implementation;
(c) to effectively evaluate and monitor the progress of
implementation of projects and programs undertaken by
various agencies to assure that Government is promptly
informed of delays and problems and advised of action
required to achieve the targets in accordance with
plans;
(d) to recommend policies for Government consideration de-
signed to promote and foster industry development;
(e) to provide effective liaison between the various sugar
industry segments and the Government agencies concerned
with specific problems of the industry;
(f) to compile necessary, timely and relevant industry
statistics;
(g) to formulate and implement training programs in con-
sultation with appropriate institutions, and to
monitor training activities of the sugar companies.
5.        The Authority would determine the most efficient means by which
these objectives can be achieved. It would study and make recommendations
on the need to eventually raise its own revenue through imposition of levies
on sugarcane or sugar for financing its operations; borrow and on-lend funds
for development purposes; create and operate special funds for designated
purposes; employ and remunerate agents. These provisions would be clearly
specified and amplified, if necessary, as part of a gradual process of trans-
ferring increasing responsibilities to KSA from other agencies.
Structure and Organization
6.        Besides the Members of the Authority, headed by a Chairman, the KSA
organization would consist of a Chief Executive and staff in various functional
areas of expertise (Chart World Bank - 16461). The strengthening of staff
resources of KSA, together with the broadening of its objectives and functions,
would be the most important aspect of support p:ovided under the Project.
Organizational details are briefly sketched below.



ANNEX 17
Page 3
The Members of the Authority
7.        The overall responsibility for the management of the Authority
would be vested jointly in the Members of the Authority to be appointed by the
Minister of Agriculture in accordance with the Agriculture Act (Cap 318),
Kenya Sugar Authority (Amendment) Order 1977. The Members of the Authority
would be individuals who are directly or indirectly involved in sugar industry
activities and include representatives of:
The Ministry of Agriculture
The Ministry of Finance and Planning
The Ministry of Commerce and Industry
The Ministry of Cooperatives and Social Services
The Ministry of Lands and Settlement
The Provincial Commissioners
The Sugar Factories
The Sugarcane Growers
The Banking Industry or the Private Commercial Sector
8.        The responsibilities of the Members of the Authority would include the
planning, direction, and control of KSA operations, particularly in the
following areas:
Studies and recommendations for Government consideration
of plans and attainable programs designed to promote and
foster industry development, including, among others, the
establishment of new factories and production zones; expan-
sion, rehabilitation or improvement of existing factories
or plantations where economically feasible; accelerated
programs for the development of qualified manpower; effi-
cient marketing and distribution of sugar and by-products;
integrated assistance programs for farmers; research matters;
and such other programs as would contribute to increased
productivity;
formulation and recommendation of policies for Government
consideration concerning, among others, appropriate changes
in prices of sugarcane, sugar and by-products; practical
methods for payment of cane based on quality; type of sugar
to be produced; quality control of sugar and sugarcane;



ANNEX 17
Page 4
zoning or rezoning of production areas; cesses to be
raised; purchasing procedures; import or export of sugar;
and such other policies as would be consistent with overall
national policies and objectives;
advising Government on contract negotiations for new
projects, particularly on technical aspects of contract
proposals;
monitoring the implementation of Government-authorized
programs, particularly the construction of new factories
or rehabilitation or improvement of existing production
zones; infrastructure projects in sugar-producing areas;
integrated assistance programs for small-scale farmers,
etc.; and evaluating the results achieved; and
compiling relevant industry statistics; and special
inquiries.
The Membership would review actions taken by the Executive Committee and the
Chief Executive and would be responsible for the appointment and fixing of
remuneration of the key officers of KSA, and of auditors and other retained
lawyers or consultants as would be required in the conduct of KSA operations.
The Executive Committee
9.        With the size and composition of the Membership as proposed, it may
be difficult to have a quorum if it meets frequently. Therefore, an Executive
Committee (Committee) should be appointed consisting of 3-5 Members. The
Committee, acting on matters specifically delegated to it by the whole Member-
ship would also undertake such other functions as liaison with other Government
ministries on a permanent basis, and always act on behalf of all the Members.
The Chief Executive
10.       The Chief Executive, as the chief administrator of KSA, would
be responsible for implementing policies, plans and programs authorized by the
Members of KSA. Specifically, his duties and responsibilities would include:
recommendations of policies for long-range and annual
plans and programs affecting the sugar industry with
the cooperation of the sugar companies, farmers, re-
search staff, distribution units and other entities
in the industry;
preparation of annual plans and work programs for
the Authority's own operations;



ANNEX 17
Page 5
direction and control of KSA operations in accordance
with policies, plans and programs established by the
Members of KSA;
review of periodic reports monitoring the progress
of authorized programs for the industry and recommend-
ing measures for KSA action where necessary;
review of relevant industry statistics and submission
of comments and recommendations to the Members of KSA;
review of reports on special inquiries, and submission
of comments and recommendations for action by KSA; and
preparation of reports on KSA operations.
The Chief Executive would be assisted by expert staff in the areas of
planning and finance, agricultural production and organization, factory
technology, and manpower development.
Planning and Finance Staff
11.       To be staffed with a Planning Officer, a Financial Analyst and an
Economist/Statistician, this unit would be responsible for preparing industry
plans and programs with the cooperation of other staff of KSA, other Government
Ministries and of the sugar companies, sugar research staff, and KNTC. These
plans and programs, which would be for Government consideration, would be
submitted to the Chief Executive and the Membership as a whole and include the
following areas:
investments in new plants and essential infrastructure, and
other projects requiring budgetary support of Government
agencies;
substantial financing requirements for outgrowers'
cane production and major repairs, rehabilitation or
expansion of existing sugar companies; and investment
justifications;
major manpower requirements of sugar companies necessi-
tating national assistance for recruitment and training;
major acquisition needs for transport facilities and
farm machinery in support of agricultural operations
involving policy considerations of labor substitution
and requiring substantial financing outlays;



ANNEX 17
Page 6
12.       The Planning and Finance Staff also would assist the Chief Execu-
tive in formulating management information systems, forms and procedures
for compiling necessary and relevant statistics and in compiling and report-
ing useful information for KSA and Government use; provide advice on the
financial aspects of project or contract proposals; and determine investment
justifications on the basis of financial, economic or other criteria es-
tablished by KSA and the Government. At the request of the Chief Executive,
the staff would undertake analyses of the pricing of sugarcane and sugar;
undertake or recommend the engagement of independent firms to undertake
special investigations of projects, programs or sugar companies involving sub-
stantial Government funds; and perform such other functions as are delegated
to KSA by the Government and require the available expertise.
13.       The Financial Analyst, in addition to his duties as chief advisor
in financial matters, would devise systems for the effective monitoring and
evaluation of on-going investment projects and programs. Such systems would
involve selecting key project activities, defining monitoring indices for
these activities, instituting data collection mechanisms, preparing time
schedules for periodic on-site inspections, and evaluating the data and
visual impressions against pre-established criteria. The findings would be
summarized in progress reports to the Chief Executive. He also would be
responsible for initiating appropriate action to facilitate project imple-
mentation if requested by project management, or if his findings indicate
possible cost overruns or slippages.
Agricultural Staff
14.       An Agronomist and Agricultural Extension Officer would provide
agronomic and organizational advice to the Chief Executive aimed at improving
efficiency in cane production and would liaise in this effort with the National
Sugar Research Institute, Agricultural Departments of the sugar companies,
cooperatives, the Sugar Settlement Scheme and such other organizations as are
assisting outgrower cane producers or are promoting cane production. The
agriculutral staff would formulate programs for improving cane growing prac-
tices, and for improving existing organizations, methods and procedures for
outgrower management, extension and research requiring national Government
support.
15.       Working with KSA's Training Officer (para. 17), the sugar companies
and training institutions, the staff would develop relevant training programs
for agricultural sugarcane extension officers. On the basis of operational
reports received and periodic visits in the field, the staff would advise the
Chief Executive on the progress of sugar research and extension programs and
recommend action if necessary, inform him of favorable developments in the
production of sugarcane and formulate programs for dissemination of infor-
mation to the industry's research and extension staff. It would also advise
Government on the agricultural aspects of new development project proposals
and assist existing companies in resolving agricultural production problems.



ANNEX 17
Page 7
Factory Technology Staff
16.       A Sugar Technologist would be responsible for providing tech-
nical assistance in the planning of new factories and the rehabilitation of
existing mills to assure that project proposals are designed for economic and
efficient processing of cane. With the cooperation of existing factories, he
would undertake studies for the overall improvement in sugar quality; for
effecting savings in operational costs through increased efficiencies in cane
yard handling systems, power-generating systems, processing systems, practical
systems for quality-based payments for cane, preventive maintenance, equipment
standardization, etc.; and formulate programs for consideration by KSA Members.
Related to these studies would be a continuing review of manpower capabilities
in coordination with KSA's Training Officer (para. 17) and Training Officers
of factories for the purpose of designing and carrying out practical training
programs for industry factory technicians. The Sugar Technologist also would
identify plant capacity utilization problems requiring national attention
and assist the companies in resolving such problems. Furthermore, he would
formulate systems for quality control of sugarcane and sugar.
Manpower Development Staff
17.       In coordination with other KSA staff and the various Training
Officers of sugar companies, KSA's Training Officer would be responsible for
formulating industry-wide programs for the development of skill levels required
by the industry. Programs would be developed jointly or separately for:
development of managerial staff in all functional
areas;
overseas scholarships in business administration and
sugar technology;
supervisory training, including subjects in effective
supervision, work measurement and simplification
methods, cost reduction techniques, etc;
agricultural extension officers' training, including
technical aspects of cane growing and husbandry,
communications and instruction techniques;
sugar technology;
craftsmanship in different specialized trades, such
as machinists, mechanics, pan boilers, tractor opera-
tors, etc; and
specialized instruction in accounting at different
levels ranging from bookkeeping to machine accounting
operations, in financial analysis, financial manage-
ment, management accounting, and budgetary and cost
controls.



ANNEX 17
Page 8
The development of these programs would take account of such relevant aspects
as timing, length of programs, number of trainees, availability of qualified
instructors and facilities, and estimated costs and possible funding. To the
maximum extent possible, KSA's Training Officer would supervise the implemen-
tation of training programs.
Marketing and Procurement
18.       Depending on the nature and extent of programs eventually developed
and authorized by the Government on procurement for the industry and possible
improvements in marketing and distribution operations, there could be a
need for a separate Marketing and Procurement staff unit in KSA that would be
responsible for implementing and/or monitoring the programs authorized by
the Government. It is not foreseen under the Project that such a unit would
be immediately established. The marketing function would, at least for the
time being, remain with the Ministry of Commerce and Industry and with KNTC
(Annex 4). However, after it becomes apparent within a period of 3-4 years to
what extent KNTC's operations can be improved, the advisability of transferring
the entire marketing function to KSA would be reconsidered. In the meantime,
interaction between KSA staff and KNTC would take place which would familiarize
KSA to some extent with the area of marketing and subject present marketing
arrangements to a decisive test (para. 22).
Administrative Services Staff
19.       To be staffed with an Administrative Officer, six secretaries/
clerk typists, four drivers and three messengers, this unit would be respon-
sible for administrative support in terms of transportation, clerical, mes-
sengerial, accounting, and cashiering services.
Relationship of KSA with Other Government Agencies
20.       Some existing programs of various Ministries or agencies of the
Government affecting the sugar industry would likely continue to be admin-
istered by them. For instance, the Ministry of Works would still be the
agency that would implement construction of roads and bridges in sugar pro-
ducing areas or supervise such construction if undertaken by contractors.
The Ministry of Agriculture would continue to supervise sugarcane research.
With the cooperation of these agencies, KSA would monitor the implementation
of industry-related projects and programs. This monitoring function would
serve to keep track of the progress on development activities and assure that
completion schedules are met. There are instances, however, where the imple-
mentation of certain on-going programs could be delegated to KSA. Exten-
sion work for cane-growing farmers could be undertaken directly by the Agri-
cultural Department staff of the companies instead of some of the agencies
involved, with advice and monitoring by KSA. The Agronomist of KSA would
assist the National Sugar Research Institute in the formulation of its research
program (Annex 7).



ANNEX 17
Page 9
21.       KSA would also serve as the main body for collecting comprehensive
statistical information on sugar industry operations for use by other Govern-
ment agencies. These statistics would include volume and costs of the
production of sugar and sugarcane, sugar imports, molasses sales, sugar
sales and inventories, plant capacity utilization, fuel utilization, industry
employment, levies collected, farmers' credits, status of foreign obligations,
and such other relevant statistics as may be deemed useful.
22.       KSA would work with KNTC on programs for the improvement of exist-
ing distribution systems of sugar and sugar by-products (para. 18). Both
organizations would explore the possibility of establishing a marketing-
oriented services approach to the industry, especially for exports when
needed. Proposals would be made for Government's consideration and KNTC
would be the agency that would be responsible for implementation of auth-
orized programs, with KSA monitoring the progress of implementation.
Relationship of KSA with Sugar Companies and Farmers
23.       Sugar companies and farmers would be represented as Members
of KSA (para. 7). Upon delegation, sugar companies could be
used by the Government as the vehicle for implementing certain projects
with the KSA monitoring implementation to assure compliance with specified
guidelines. KSA would not be involved in the management of the companies.
It would, however, be available in a consulting or advisory capacity where
it would have the expertise. The companies and farmers would avail them-
selves of KSA assistance in formulating proposals for Government assistance
and use KSA as a channel of communication with Government on other matters.
The Government, through its representation on the Board of publicly-owned
companies would continue to exercise Board level management control over
those companies, but would utilize the services of KSA in independently
obtaining relevant information on the operations of the companies. Farmers
would be able to seek redress of grievances through the agricultural staff
of KSA.
Implementation of the Reorganization Proposal
24.       The proposed reorganization of KSA as described in this Annex was
accepted by Government at negotiations. It would be a condition of loan
effectiveness that Government had taken all actions necessary to expand the
membership of KSA (para. 7) and to authorize it to carry out the enlarged
functions (para. 4). It was agreed that KSA would have employed by
January 1, 1978 in addition to the Chief Executive and Economist/Statistian
already in post a Planning Officer, Financial Analyst, Agronomist, Agricul-
tural Extension Specialist, Sugar Technologist and Training Officer and
appropriate support staff.



ANNEX 17
Page 10
25.       KSA would also prepare an annual plan of operation which would
have to be consistent with the proposed objectives and functions and approved
by KSA Members. The Project provides for staff, vehicles, equipment and
operating expenses of KSA for the first five years of operation (1977-81). A
maximum of five experts would be recruited internationally, three for three
years and two for two years, should qualified local candidates be unavailable.
Draft terms of reference for these experts are attached as Appendix 1. The
Bank would be consulted on the appointment or replacement of the Planning
Officer, Agronomist and Sugar Technologist. A plan for future financing of
KSA would be prepared by July 1, 1979. Estimated Project costs are shown in
Annex 15, Table 20.
26.       It is envisaged that KSA would be required to conduct several major
studies in the near future, and the Project provides KShs. 3.1 million
(US$375,000) for this purpose. The major areas to be studied include:
(a) the utilization and marketing of molasses (and possibly other
sugar by-products) in Kenya, including alternative uses such
as alcohol production, livestock feed and export; and
(b) the development of incentive systems for cane producers, with
particular emphasis on profit sharing arrangements with sugar
companies, production bonuses, and payment for cane based on
quality.
Studies would also be required in connection with the sugar marketing improve-
ment plan to be prepared by KNTC by December 31, 1978 (Annex 4, para. 6)
for which funds could be channeled through KSA. In addition, expertise other
than that provided for in the current staffing plan is expected to be required
from time to time and would be made available through short-term consultancy
assignments or temporary appointments to the staff. Detailed outlines for
studies to be conducted and terms of reference for consultants to be engaged
would be prepared and submitted to the Bank for approval. The Bank would also
be consulted on the appointment of consultants or additional staff.
February 20, 1977

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
PROPOSED ORGANIZATION OF THE SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR COMPANY
Introduction
1.        The underlying concept of the proposed Company organization is
one which forms an integrated operation of all agricultural, processing and
administrative activities under centralized management. With the major
portion of the factory's supply of sugarcane grown by independent farmers, it
is essential that an organization properly equipped, staffed and managed be
available to render the required extension services, materials, credits and
related services to the farmers, to assure a sufficient and timely supply of
sugarcane. Consequently, the agricultural staff of the Company would be
responsible not only for cane production at its own nucleus estate, but also
for providing for their account integrated services to outgrowers in accor-
dance with "contract growing" arrangements to be mutually agreed on between
the Company and the individual farmers. These arrangements would be patterned
after those in effect at Mumias where the farmer agrees to make a specified
area owned by him available for cane-growing under close company supervision.
2.        The Company's factory staff would be organized along the lines
of three distinct operational activities: engineering, processing and opera-
tional control. Unlike the factory organization structure of most of the
existing sugar mills in Kenya, the proposed organization combines the mill,
electrical and civil engineering functions into an engineering division. This
division would be responsible for cane yard operation, mechanical extraction
of cane juice from the crushers to the stage when the juice enters the chemi-
cal processing phase, and for maintenance and repairs. The processing divi-
sion would be responsible for chemical processing activities from juice
clarification to the bagging station. The control division would be estab-
lished as a unit separate from the engineering and processing divisions with
responsibility for quality control and compilation of necessary operational
data on production.
3.        The Company's finance and administrative staff would be responsi-
ble for all financial and administrative aspects of Company operations,
including financial planning, financial accounting, operational cost control
and general administrative services. Staff concerned with personnel adminis-
tration would have responsibility for recruitment, testing, interviews,
salary and wage administration, industrial relations and training as well as
medical, security and employee welfare activities. The proposed organiza-
tional structure of the Company is depicted in Chart World Bank - 16462.
4.        While the Company would be eventually organized as described in
this Annex, there would be some variation of the structure during the pre-
operating period. The agricultural staff would already be organized as in



ANNEX 18
Page 2
the operating stage with agricultural operations starting ahead of the actual
start-up date of factory operations. The factory staff would, however, be
organized differently during construction. The qualified and experienced
engineers to be employed from the outset and absorbed into the factory organi-
zation proper at a later date, would be initially responsible for assuring
contractors' compliance with contract terms, specifications and schedules
and monitoring the progress of various construction activities and providing
the necessary certification of accomplishment that may be required for dis-
bursement procedures.
Detailed Features of the Proposed Organization
5.        The South Nyanza Sugar Company, Ltd., has been incorporated under
the Companies Act of Kenya. The governing body is a Board of Directors
with responsibility for the formulation and execution of Company policies.
The Board is to plan, direct and supervise operational activities as carried
out by the Company's Managing Agent, who is represented on the Board and acts
through a General Manager to be selected in consultation with the Board
(para. 38).
6.        The General Manager would be responsible for Company management
in accordance with policies, objectives, plans and programs established
by the Board. Specifically, he would have responsibility for meeting
production volume and quality targets; maintaining financial liquidity
and solvency; developing required manpower; safeguarding and properly
maintaining Company property; developing and maintaining harmonious rela-
tionships with the Kenya Sugar Authority and other Government authorities
and with the communities within the boundaries of the production zone.
He would be assisted in these functions by four main functional depart-
ments of the Company, namely, the Agricultural, Factory, Finance and
Administration and Personnel and Industrial Relations Departments. The
Departments' organization is described below.
The Agricultural Department
7.        This Department, to be headed by a Department Manager reporting
to the General Manager would have overall responsibility for:
producing cane economically in the nucleus estate and the
outgrowers areas in accordance with Company-established
plans and programs;
maintaining prescribed field management standards in
nucleus estate and outgrowers' areas;



ANNEX 18
Page 3
determining ways and means for economically improving
productivity of cane-growing areas;
providing appropriate materials and services to outgrowers
in accordance with contract-growing arrangements;
harvesting and transporting cane economically for the
nucleus estate and outgrowers areas in accordance with
predetermined schedules;
safeguarding and maintaining farm machinery and equipment
in accordance with prescribed preventive maintenance and
repair standards;
Department staff development;
maintaining harmonious relations with outgrowers; and
coordinating functions with other Company departments and
periodically advising the General Manager of significant
developments in the Department's operations.
These functions would be carried out by three main operating Divisions
and two Staff Units reporting to the Department manager, namely, the
Nucleus Estates, Outgrowers, and Agricultural Services Divisions and
the Research and Administrative Services Staff Units (Chart World Bank -
16464).
8.        The Nucleus Estates Division.  To be headed by a Division Manager,
reporting to the Agricultural Department Manager, this Division would be
directly responsible for the growing of sugarcane in the nucleus estate
in accordance with established plans, programs and field management standards.
Utilizing the services of the Agricultural Services Division, the Nucleus
Estates Division would assure that land is prepared and cultivated in pro-
grammed areas as scheduled. This Division would be responsible for growing
seed cane for its own planting purposes; planting designated areas as
scheduled; carrying out proper field maintenance procedures such as ferti-
lizer applications, weeding, etc.; preventing or controlling pests, diseases
and fire; and instituting such other protective measures against controllable
hazards as would be necessary for improved cane growth and productivity in the
nucleus estate area. In accordance with predetermined harvesting schedules,
the Division would initiate cane harvesting in the nucleus estate area,
utilizing the services of the Agricultural Services Division. In coordina-
tion with the Personnel and Industrial Relations Department, the Division
would assure that proper health and sanitation standards are maintained
in the nucleus estate housing areas for the prevention of communicable
diseases.
9.        For administrative purposes, the nucleus estate would be divided
into three geographical sections with approximately 880 hectares each to be
assigned to a Section Supervisor. Each supervisor would be responsible for



ANNEX 18
Page 4
the Division's functions in his assigned area. Four overseers and five to
six headmen would be assisting each Section Supervisor. The headmen would be
relatively junior staff but performing similar functions as the overseers.
The Division Manager and Section Supervisors would be responsible for comply-
ing with prescribed administrative procedures for periodically reporting
essential operational and financial data for purposes of cost control.
10.       The Outgrowers Division.  To be headed by a Division Manager,
reporting to the Agricultural Department MIanager, this Division would be
directly responsible for the growing of sugarcane in the outgrowers area
in accordance with established plans, programs, field management standards,
and the provisions of contract-growing arrangements with individual farmers.
This Division would initiate the survey, preparation, and cultivation of
individual outgrowers' fields as programmed, utilizing the services of
the Agricultural Services Division. The Outgrowers Division would be
responsible for the growing of seed cane in farmers' fields in each of
the four sectional areas to supply outgrowers in those respective areas.
It would purchase seed cane grown in farmers' fields, treat the seed cane
and distribute it for planting.
11.       The Division would instruct and supervise the outgrowers in
the proper care and maintenance of growing cane, provide prescribed ferti-
lizers, and herbicides, and supervise the application of inputs. The cost
of all supplies and services would be recoverable from the farmer at
harvest time, as stipulated in the individual contracts. In the event
a farmer is unable to comply with prescribed care and maintenance proce-
dures in his field, the Division would initiate undertaking such services,
utilizing staff of the Agricultural Services Division, if necessary, with
the costs to be borne by the farmer. The Division also would be responsible
for disease and fire prevention and control, and such other protective measures
as would be necessary against controllable hazards. In accordance with
predetermined harvesting schedules, the Division would advise the outgrowers
concerned and arrange with the Agricultural Services Division for the cutting,
loading and hauling of the cane to the factory. To minimize harvesting costs,
the Division's staff would investigate the possibility of permitting the
burning of outgrowers' cane under Company supervision on blocks of land where,
in the judgement of Company staff, cane fires would not pose any environmental
risks. The Division would be responsible for maintaining a harmonious and
cooperative relationship with the outgrowers.
12.       For administrative purposes, the outgrowers area would be divided
into four geographical sections with approximately 4,000 hectares each of
cane area or 18,500 hectares of gross area, to be assigned to an Agricul-
tural Supervisor. Each supervisor would be responsible for carrying out
the Division's functions in his assigned area. Two overseers and two
headmen would be assisting each Agricultural Supervisor. The Division
Manager and Agricultural Supervisors would be responsible for complying
with prescribed administrative procedures for periodically reporting
essential operational and financial data for purposes of cost control,
especially those affecting materials and services supplied to the out-
growers, for billing purposes.



ANNEX 18
Page 5
13.       The Agricultural Services Division.  To be headed by a Division
Manager, reporting to the Agricultural Department Manager, this Division
would be directly responsible for land surveying, clearing, and preparation;
road construction and maintenance; cane harvesting and transport services
for the nucleus estate and outgrowers areas; operation of a central motor
pool for all Company motor vehicles; and maintenance and repairs of Company-
owned farm machinery, implements, motor vehicles, and housing. In coordina-
tion with the Nucleus Estates and Outgrowers Divisions, the Division Manager
would plan, direct and control the service operations to assure that all
requirements are met as programmed, in accordance with prescribed policies,
standards, and Company procedures. He would operate the Division economi-
cally and assure that maximum utilization standards of equipment are
attained and that services are properly charged to users. Pertinent records
of equipment operating costs would be kept and appropriate schedules for
equipment replacement prepared. The Division Manager would be assisted
in these functions by four functional sections, namely, the Construction,
Harvesting, Transport and Workshop Sections, and by a Division Staff Office.
14.       The Construction Section, headed by a Section Supervisor, would
consist of the Survey Unit, the Land Preparation Unit, and the Road Con-
struction and Maintenance Unit, with the Survey Unit assisting the other
two units in surveying cane fields and roads and tracks, respectively,
to determine the exact boundaries, areas and alignments. The minimum size
of contiguous cane area to be serviced by the Company would be 10 ha.
Since individual farmers have generally less land than this, they would
have to make arrangements for neighboring lots to be consolidated to
reach the minimum acceptable size, requiring extensive surveying. At
the request of the Nucleus Estates and Outgrowers Divisions, the Land
Preparation Unit would undertake clearing, land preparation and cultivation
of nucleus estate and outgrowers areas. The cost of these services and of
land surveying would be for the account of the Nucleus Estates Division
or the individual outgrower concerned and proper records would be kept
for billing purposes. The Road Construction and Maintenance Unit would
undertake work for the Company's account based on job orders authorized
by the Department Manager.
15.       The Harvesting Section would be headed by a Section Supervisor
and be responsible for the cutting of cane in nucleus estate and outgrowers
areas. The cost of such services would be for the respective accounts.
The Section would be staffed with nine overseers who would take direct
charge of gangs of cane cutters in the fields.
16.       The Transport Section would be responsible for the transportation
of cane from the fields to the factory at the request of the Nucleus
Estates and Outgrowers Divisions. It would also be responsible for the
operation of a central Motor Pool for servicing personnel transport needs
of various departments of the Company. All Company motor vehicles would
be subject to control in a common pool and dispatched for use only on the
basis of authorized trip tickets. The Section's Cane Transport Unit would
have the necessary complement of overseers and headmen, drivers and turn-
boys, while the Motor Pool would have drivers only. All drivers would be



ANNEX 18
Page 6
responsible for the necessary preventive maintenance checks of their
vehicles or transport units and would report the need for repairs to the
Section Supervisor who would authorize service requests to the Workshop
Section.
17.       The Workshop Section would be responsible for maintenance and
repairs of all Company-owned farm machinery and equipment, cane transport
vehicles, motor cars and housing units. The cost of repairs would be
charged to the entity concerned for cost control purposes. To be headed
by a Section Supervisor, this Section would be staffed by a necessary
complement of mechanics, electricians, fitters, welders, blacksmiths,
carpenters, a mason, and a toolkeeper.
18.       There would also be a Division Staff Office, to be headed by
an Office Supervisor who would report to the Division Manager and be
responsible for all administrative aspects of the Division. These would
include assisting the Division in the compilation of administrative and
cost control reports for submission to the Department and in the prepara-
tion of its annual budget. The office would also handle trip tickets
authorizing dispatch of vehicles from the Motor Pool.
19.       The Research Staff Unit.  This unit, to be headed by an Agronomist,
reporting to the Agricultural Department Manager, would be responsible for
the agricultural research and experimental activities of the Company.
It would have trial fields in various locations and be responsible for
liaison and coordination with KSA and the National Sugar Research Institute
and for advising staff in the Nucleus Estates and Outgrowers Divisions on
the results of experiments. The unit would carry out the necessary maturity
tests for cane in various areas using the facilities of the Control Division
of the Factory Department for laboratory analysis and inform the Nucleus
Estates and Outgrowers Divisions of impendent harvesting dates. The work
force would consist of an assistant agronomist and three overseers, with
the necessary complement of laborers.
20.       The Administrative Services Staff.  To be headed by an Adminis-
trative Officer reporting to the Agricultural Department Manager, the
Administrative Services Staff would perform administrative duties, review
and compile prescribed reports from the field divisions, assist in bud-
getary preparations, and attend to all clerical work in the Department.
The Factory Department
21.       This Department, to be headed by a Department Manager reporting
to the General Manager would have overall responsibility for:
operating the factory efficiently to process cane received
from the nucleus estate and outgrowers areas into sugar of a
quality in accordance with Company-prescribed standards;



ANNEX 18
Page 7
maintaining the factory in excellent operating condition
through proper maintenance in order to maximize utilization
of plant capacity;
applying adequate procedures for maintaining a high level of
industrial safety in coordination with the Personnel and
Industrial Relations Department;
complying with Government-prescribed regulations and standards
applicable to food processing industries;
developing capabilities of department employees and promoting
industrial peace;
coordinating functions with other Departments and periodically
advising the General Manager of significant developments in the
Department's operations.
The functions of the Department would be carried out by three main Divisions,
namely, the Engineering, Processing and Control Divisions (Chart World Bank -
16465). Each of the Divisions would be geared to operate three shifts a
day. The three main Divisions would be distinct responsibility centers,
each with a clear functional specialization.
22.       The Engineering Division.  To be headed by a Chief Engineer
reporting to the Factory Department Manager, this Division would be
responsible for the mechanical phase of factory operations, including
unloading the cane at the cane yard, feeding the cane into the mill, crush-
ing, and extracting the juice. It would also operate the boiler plant
and electrical powerhouse for generating the required power. In order to
minimize production interruptions due to plant shutdowns, the Division
would have control over the factory's electrical, mechanical and civil engi-
neering maintenance personnel. In coordination with the Personnel and
Industrial Relations Department, the Division would arrange for external
staff training programs and for implementing effective methods of on-the-job
instruction. It would also formulate industrial safety procedures.
23.       There would be four Sections of the Division, namely, the Milling
Section and the Electrical, Mechanical, and Civil Engineering Sections. The
Milling Section would be in charge of operating the cranes for unloading the
cane, feeding the cane to the mill, running the mill, and generating steam
power. The other three Sections would operate the electrical utilities and
perform the maintenance and repair tasks. Each of the stations in the mill,
namely cane yard, crushing, boiler and boiling house equipment would be manned
by shift crews of machine operators or tenders. The utilities maintenance
Sections would also have crews on hand for each shift not only for carrying
out routine inspection and preventive maintenance but also to handle emergency
repairs when necessary so that stoppages would be minimized. During annual
shutdowns, both the machine operator crews under the shift engineers and the
utilities and maintenance crews would be utilized for undertaking repairs.



ANNEX 18
Page 8
24.       The Processing Division.  To be headed by a Processing Superin-
tendent reporting to the Factory Department Manager, this Division would be
responsible for the chemical processing operations of the plant from the
point where the juice enters the clarifiers and evaporators, to crystalliza-
tion, curing and centrifugals and the bagging station where the sugar is
packed and transferred to the warehouse, and for the operation of the water
supply and effluent treatment systems. There would be four distinct stations,
namely, the Clarification/Evaporation, Crystallization, Curing/Centrifugation
and Bagging Stations. Since the quality of the finished product depends not
only on the performance of the equipment at this stage of manufacture but
also on the efficiency of the operators manning each station, personnel with
adequate training and practical experience would be required to man each
station. Each of the stations would be headed by a foreman reporting to the
Processing Superintendent through an Assistant Superintendent.
25.       The Division would carry out effective on-the-job training, espe-
cially since in this area of plant operations there is no substitute for
practical experience. It would also be responsible for maintaining health
and sanitation standards and for observing industrial safety procedures.
The training and safety programs would be coordinated with the Personnel
and Industrial Relations Department.
26.       The Control Division.  To be headed by a Chief Chemist reporting
to the Factory Department Manager, this Division would be responsible for
quality control. In accordance with standard procedures, it would periodi-
cally analyze cane juice at specific stages of the process to determine
the quality of cane and juice and the efficiency in processing, and
immediately suggest adjustments to be made for improving quality if
necessary. It would compile the required production volume and quality
statistics for reporting purposes, including cata on cane received, juice
extracted, sugar bagged, molasses produced, ard on recoveries and losses.
The Division would be staffed with assistant chemists, laboratory techni-
cians, scalemen for measuring cane and juice, and clerks recording produc-
tion at the bagging station.
27.       The Factory Office.  To be headed by an Administrative Assistant
reporting to the Factory Department Manager, this staff unit would be
responsible for handling all administrative matters of the Department,
including the preparation of reports on factory operations for submission
to the General Manager and other Departments, especially the Finance and
Administration Department. It would keep time, process requests for
materials, assist the Divisions in the preparation of annual budgetary
plans, and support the Engineering Division in the preparation of drawings
and specifications.
The Finance and Administration Department
28.       To be headed by a Controller reporting to the General Manager,
this Department would be responsible for:



ANNEX 18
Page 9
preparing consolidated operational and financial plans for
the Company in coordination with the various operating and
service departments;
compiling prescribed operational and financial data and
preparing periodic reports on Company operations; advising
the General Manager and other department managers on financial
matters;
keeping records of cash, property, supplies and finished
products;
receiving, safekeeping, and verifying disbursement requests
and disbursing cash for authorized Company expenditures;
ordering, receiving, storing and issuing materials,
supplies and equipment required by various departments;
obtaining and maintaining adequate insurance coverage for
Company properties and assets against loss or damage from
insurable risks;
negotiating Company credit lines with banking institutions,
subject to authority delegated by the General Manager and
the Board;
reviewing systems and procedures and initiating improvements
regarding computational accuracy and administrative efficiency;
maintaining corporate financial liquidity and solvency through
prudent control over Company cash flows.
These functions would be carried out by two staff officers (Assistant
Controller and Budget Officer, and Auditing Officer) and three Divisions,
namely, the Administration/Finance, General Accounting, and Cost Accounts
Divisions (Chart World Bank - 16466).
29.       The Assistant Controller's and Budget Office.  The Assistant Con-
troller and Budget Officer would, under the responsibility of the Controller,
coordinate the annual budgeting process with the cooperation of all other
Company Departments. This process would involve the preparation of crop
estimates for both the nucleus estate and outgrowers areas on the basis of
areas available for plant and ratoon cane, and estimated yields together
with the required inputs, materials, supplies, services, and manpower. On
hand of these estimates, all other Divisions in the Agricultural and Fac-
tory Departments would prepare their own operational plans and programs.
The physical estimates so established would be costed with the assistance
of the Cost Accounts and General Accounting Divisions, and the results con-
solidated and used to prepare projections of income, assets and liabilities,



ANNEX 18
Page 10
cash flows, operational expenditures, purchasing requirements, manpower re-
quirements, and such other supporting data and forecasts as would be needed.
Major capital expenditure proposals would have to be accompanied by an eco-
nomic returns justification where applicable. The timing of materials re-
quirements would be indicated for use in the scheduling of purchases. Be-
sides his involvement in the budgetary process, the Assistant Controller
and Budget Officer would be responsible for financial analysis of the opera-
tions and for systems reviews and for forumulating such changes in systems
and methods as would make data processing more efficient and the information
generated more useful for managerial purposes. He would also prepare the
systems and methods for the computation of charges for services rendered to
outgrowers, such as land preparation, extension, harvesting and transport.
The method would arrive at rates to be charged farmers allowing full re-
covery of costs by the Company.
30.       The Auditing Office.  The Auditing Officer reporting directly to
the Controller would be responsible for conducting auditing programs relating
to any number of subjects to be examined, such as account records, legal and
fiscal books, operating procedures, inventory valuations, purchases, cash and
bank transactions, fixed asset controls, payrolls, account receivables, cost
systems, and follow-up where corrective action is to be taken.
31.       The Administration/Finance Division.  To be headed by an Adminis-
trative Officer reporting to the Controller, and subdivided into three
functional areas dealing with Finance, Materials and General Services,
this Division would be responsible for receipt, custody or safekeeping,
issuance or disbursement of cash, materials and supplies, sugar and
molasses, and for purchases of materials and supplies, the ordering of
equipment, arranging insurance coverage, etc. The Division would operate
a central storeroom that would be responsible for all Company requirements
of materials and supplies including fertilizers, pesticides, herbicides,
spares, tires, fuel, lubricants, lime, sulphur, office supplies, etc.
It would also be responsible for sugar and molasses inventories transferred
from the Factory Department to storage.
32.       General Accounting Division.  To be headed by a Chief Accountant
reporting to the Controller, this Division would be responsible for all
general accounts and accounts payable entries necessary for recording and
reporting the financial transactions of the Company. It would also handle
review and verification of all vouchers for payment including invoices
and documents received from suppliers of equipment, materials and services,
or related to purchases of cane from outgrowers, sale of sugar and molasses
and other financial transactions. The review of vouchers would be made
to determine their accuracy, validity in terms of authority to incur such
expenses, prices agreed on, as to whether materials or services for which
payment is claimed have actually been received, and to assign the appropriate
account classifications for accounting entry. The Division would keep the
necessary subsidiary ledgers to provide ready determination of proper
breakdowns of receivables, payables, properties and equipment acquired,



ANNEX 18
Page 11
materials and supplies inventories and expense breakdowns for operations
other than for the Factory and Agricultural Departments which would be
kept by the Cost Accounts Division.
33.       The Cost Accounts Division.  To be headed by a Cost Accountant
reporting to the Controller, this Division would be responsible for keeping
subsidiary records of costs and expenses incurred by the Factory and Agri-
cultural Departments and for the preparation of prescribed operational
cost control reports for management use. These reports and the underlying
systems and procedures would provide relevant information on the cost of
cane production and sugar manufacturing by main responsibility centers.
Proper comparisons would be made of cost breakdowns with budgets and other
standards to enable management concerned to identify areas requiring their
attention. The Division would also handle the necessary labor distribution
and materials distribution analyses to facilitate accounting classification
for charging the responsibility centers concerned. Furthermore, it would
be responsible for the preparation of payrolls for the Factory and Agri-
cultural Departments on the basis of time and attendance records submitted
by these Departments. These payrolls would be checked by the General
Accounting Division, and actual payouts would be made by the Administration/
Finance Division.
The Personnel and Industrial Relations Department
34.       To be headed by a Personnel and Industrial Relations Manager
reporting to the General Manager, this Department would be responsible for:
recruiting personnel for the various Company departments;
keeping relevant records of personnel;
formulating, recommending and implementing Company personnel
policies including salary, wage and benefits administration;
complying with Government laws and regulations affecting
agricultural and industrial labor;
promoting programs designed to attain high levels of
employee morale, maintain industrial peace with Company
employees, and enhance harmonious relationships with out-
growers in coordination with other Departments;
administering Company employee welfare programs;
maintaining adequate security, health and industrial safety
standards in coordination with other Company Departments;
formulating, recommending and carrying out career development
programs for Company staff in coordination with the Kenya
Sugar Authority and other Company Departments.
These functions would be carried out by the Personnel Administration,
Industrial Relations and Training Offices (Chart World Bank - 16467).



ANNEX 18
Page 12
35.       The Personnel Administration Office.  To be headed by a Personnel
Officer reporting to the Personnel and Industrial Relations Manager, this
office would be responsible for the recruitment of personnel for the
various Departments through processing, testing and advance interviewing
of applicants before they are referred to the requisitioning Departments
for selection and approval. It would keep relevant personnel records and
be responsible for formulating and recommending policies on salary and wage
administration; preparing job descriptions for different positions; rating
positions based on such factors as experience, education, responsibilities
and working conditions; determining salary grades; and ranking of employees.
36.       The Industrial Relations Office.  To be headed by an Industrial
Relations Officer reporting to the Personnel and Industrial Relations
Manager, the Industrial Relations Office would be responsible for assuring
Company compliance with labor laws and regulations; attending to labor
union matters; and administering Company policies and programs on medical
security and safety, and employee welfare matters. Specifically, this
office would represent the Company in meetings with union representatives
on matters concerning labor union requests or demands, employee grievances
or programs for improving employee morale and productivity. It would also
be responsible for meeting the health and sanitation needs of employees
in the factory as well as the housing complex; implementing programs for
security and safety of Company premises, and the nucleus estate and out-
growers areas; and initiating recreational, educational, cultural and
sportive programs, and such other activities as would contribute to
the promotion of a high level of staff morale.
37.       The Training Office.  The staff of one training officer and a
general clerk, reporting to the Personnel and Industrial Relations
Manager, would be responsible for formulating and administering career
development programs for Company personnel in coordination with the
Kenya Sugar Authority's Training Officer and cther Company Departments.
The training programs would be based on an inventory of individual
staff in each Department and identification and quantification of
training needs of each. Training schedules would be prepared at
the beginning of each year specifying the objectives and nature of
each program, the participants, frequency and length of programs,
instructors and facilities required, and estimated costs of the
program for review by Company management. Approved programs would be
funded by sufficient budgetary appropriations which could be partly
recovered from the industrial training fund levy.
Management and Staffing
38.       The operation of the South Nyanza Stgar Company, with both
agricultural and factory activities combined in one entity, requires
management and technicians of high caliber and with considerable expe-
rience, which is presently not available in Kenya. An agreement has
been reached, therefore, with The Mehta Group International, Ltd., an



ANNEX 18
Page 13
experienced international firm, to manage the Company from its initial
years up to the point when Kenyans have attained sufficient experience to
assume managerial and technical positions initially filled by internationally
recruited staff (Annex 11). The management team would include the General
Manager and a complement of key second-level personnel such as the Agricul-
tural, Factory, and Finance and Administration Department Managers. The
Personnel and Industrial Relations Manager would be hired locally as this
position requires a person who is thoroughly familiar with the character of
the people, their personal attitudes and motivations, customs and traditions,
and with Kenyan laws and regulations affecting labor in agricultural and
industrial sectors. Selected management staff at the middle levels and
certain technical positions requiring highly specialized training and prac-
tical experience, estimated to number 32, would also have to be recruited
abroad. A list of these positions is provided in Annex 14. The Kenya
Sugar Authority and the Ministry of Labor would assist in recruiting local
staff. In order to avoid possible problems in the relationship with exist-
ing factories, whose cooperation would be required for apprenticeship train-
ing arrangements, the Company would entertain applications of candidates
from existing factories only with the appropriate prior clearance from
them. There is a likelihood that no more than 10% of staffing requirements
could be met from existing factories without unduly affecting their opera-
tions. An extensive program of training would be organized to supply the
balance of trained staff (Annex 14). Brief descriptions of the duties,
responsibilities and qualifications of key staff are presented in Appendix 1.
The Bank would be consulted in the appointment of the General Manager, the
Controller, and the managers of the Agricultural Department, Factory Depart-
ment and Personnel and Industrial Relations Department. It would be a con-
dition of loan effectiveness that the General Manager, Controller and Agri-
cultural Department Manager had been appointed.

DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR COMPANY STAFF
General Manager
1.        The General Manager would be the Chief Executive Officer of the
Company.
Report to:   Board of Directors.
Supervises: Agricultural, Factory, Finance and Administration and
Personnel and Industrial Relations Department.
Duties and Responsibilities:
Prepares Company plans and programs for Board approval;
Directs and controls operations of Departments and units
to assure that Company plans on production volume, sugar-
cane and sugar quality standards and profit plans are
achieved;
Maintains harmonious relations with KSA, Government auth-
orities, communities within the boundaries of the production
zone and with Company employees and outgrowers;
Represents the Company in dealings with Government, trade
associations, Unions and private parties;
Keeps the Board of Directors informed of Company operations.
Qualifications:
College degree in Business Administration, Industrial
Management, Sugar Technology or Agriculture with at least
10 to 15 years practical experience in managing a sugar
factory with a plantation.
Language Ability:
English.
Age:      40-50.

Agricultural Department Manager
2.        The Agricultural Department Manager would have direct responsibility

Reports to: General Manager.
Supervises: Nucleus Estates, Outgrowers, and Agricultural Services
Division Managers.
Duties and Responsibilities:
Produces cane in the nucleus estate and outgrowers areas in
the most economical fashion possible in accordance with Com-
pany-established plans and programs;
Maintains prescribed field management standards;
Determines ways for economically improving productivity of
cane-growing areas;
Provides appropriate materials and extension services to out-
growers in accordance with contract growing arrangements, and
maintains harmonious relationships with them;
Harvests sugarcane in the nucleus estate and outgrowers areas
and transports it economically to the factory in accordance
with predetermined schedules;
Safeguards and maintains farm machinery and equipment in
accordance with prescribed preventive maintenance and repair
standards;
Develops the capabilities of Department employees and promotes
good labor relations;
Coordinates functions with other Company Departments and peri-
odically advises the General Manager of significant developments
in the Department's operations.
Qualifications:
Graduate of Agriculture with at least 10 years experience in
the management of a large plantation; preferably in sugarcane.
Language Ability:
English.
Age:      45-50.

for the factory operations.

Supervises: Chief Engineer, Processing Superintendent, Chief Chemist,
and Administrative Assistant.
Duties and Responsibilities:
Operates the factory efficiently to process the cane received
from the nucleus estate and outgrowers area into sugar in
accordance with Company-prescribed standards and in compliance
with Government-prescribed regulations and standards for food-
processing industries;
Maintains the factory in excellent operating condition to
achieve maximum utilization of plant capacity, subject to the
availability of cane supply;
Carries out organized systems and procedures to maintaining
industrial safety;
Develops capabilities of Department employees through practi-
cal training programs in cooperation with the Training Officer
of the Personnel and Industrial Relations Department; maintains
high level of employee morale;
Coordinates functions with the other Company Departments;
Keeps the General Manager informed of significant developments
in factory operations;
Performs such other functions as may be delegated to him by
the General Manager.
Qualifications

of a sugar factory.

work involving small farmers.

KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
THE WORLD SUGAR MARKET
A. Market Structure
1.        Approximately three quarters of the world's sugar production is con-
sumed in the producing countries; the remainder is traded internationally.
Until 1974, when the U.S. Sugar Act and the Commonwealth Sugar Agreement
expired, 86% of the world sugar trade was concentrated in three separate and
protected markets: the United States, the United Kingdom and the Centrally
Planned Countries. The "free market" was a highly volatile residual market;
currently it includes about 75% of the world trade. Since the expiration
of the International Sugar Agreement, the world market has not been regulated,
although a number of significant bilateral agreements have been negotiated.
The expiration of the trade agreements, coupled with the high growth rate of
sugar consumption in developing oil producing countries, has changed the
structure of the international sugar market, and has improved the long-term
export prospects of the developing countries as they can compete for a larger
share of the world market. However, the risks associated with the production
of sugar for export have also increased. Past and future world sugar balances
and trade are depicted in Table 1.
B. Consumption
2.        World sugar consumption in 1975 was about 80 million tons.  The
growth rate of consumption from the mid-sixties to 1974 was greater than
the growth of production over the same period, primarily because of the pre-
vailing price structures in most of the consuming and producing countries.
The domestic price of sugar in most countries was kept insulated from world
market price increases during the period. Sugar consumption is responsive
to price changes as well as income changes, but the sharp rise in the free
market price of sugar in 1974 was not passed on to consumers and, therefore,
did not decrease consumption to a market balancing level.
3.        It is projected that 94-97 million tons of sugar will be con-
sumed in 1980 and 107-114 million tons in 1985 (Table 2). This projection
is based on current consumption levels and a price of US41O/lb in constant
1973 dollars, a price level which corresponds to the estimated long-term
marginal costs of the industry. The projected consumption levels represent
an average annual increase of 3.3-3.9% between 1975 and 1980 and of 3.0-3.7%
between 1975 and 1985. Due to higher per capita consumption and lower income
and price elasticities of sugar in developed countries, the growth rate of
consumption is expected to be lower in developed countries than in developing
countries. Accordingly, it is estimated that sugar consumption will increase
by about 8 million tons in developed countries between 1975 and 1985 and
by 15 million tons in developing countries.



ANNEX 19
Page 2
C. Production and Supply
4.        World production in 1975 was about 80 million tons.  Over the last
ten years world sugar production has lagged behind world consumption for a
number of reasons:                                        I
(a) Low prices throughout the 1950s and 1960s which provided
little incentive for export production;
(b) Lack of private capital from developed countries due to
fear of nationalization coupled with scarcity of public
capital in developing countries caused lags in new
investment and in maintenance of existing plants;
(c) The organizational restrictions set by the International
Sugar Organization (ISO) increased the rigidity of the
supply response to price increases. Members of the ISO
were unable to increase their export volume due to export
quotas;
(d) Production short-falls in Cuba, USSR and Europe during
this period caused by adverse weather conditions; and
(e) Other agricultural commodity prices, primarily of major
grains, rose faster than sugar prices and the negative
cross elasticities of supply between grains and sugar
caused the supply response to the increases in sugar
price to be slow.
Nevertheless, world sugar production increased from 39 million tons in 1955
to 78 million tons in 1973, or at an average rate of about 4% per annum.
The growth rate in the developing countries was about 4.6%.
5.        It is projected that world production will reach 92-99 million tons
in 1980 and 106 - 113 million tons in 1985 (Table 3). This represents annual
increases of 2.8 - 4.3% between 1975 and 1980 and 2.8 - 3.5% between 1975 and
1985. The major expansion in production is anticipated to take place in
developing countries where growth will be approximately 6.5 million tons
annually between 1975 and 1980 and 9.5 million tons annually between 1980 and
1985. At the same time it is anticipated that in the developed countries
production will grow annually by 4 million tons between 1975 and 1980 and by
2.5 million tons between 1980 and 1985. In the Centrally Planned countries,
sugar production is expected to increase annually by 5 million tons between
1975 and 1980 and by an additional 2 million tons between 1980 and 1985.
6.        Among the developing countries, the major expansion is expected
to take place in Brazil, which has recently become the world's largest cane
producing country. Other developing countries where production will be



ANNEX 19
Page 3
substantially increased are Mexico, Argentina, Peru, India, the Philippines,
Thailand, Sudan and Morocco. Among developed countries the greatest rise in
production will most likely take place in the EEC which aims to become self-
sufficient in sugar by 1980. The United States, on the other hand, may reduce
domestic production to some extent. Among Centrally Planned Economies,
Rumania and Hungary will account for the greatest part of increased produc-
tion, and it is expected that production in the USSR will be increased by
about one million tons to reduce that country's dependence on supplies from
the free market.
7.        During the last 10 years, production costs have risen from US$400-
500 per ton of annual capacity to US$1,000-1,200 per ton. It is estimated
that between 62% and 82% of the required growth in production capacity in
developing countries can be achieved through increases in the productivity
and expansion of existing facilities. The investment cost of US$120-400 per
ton of annual capacity of these rehabilitation and expansion projects are
relatively modest. Total capital investment needs for these types of
operation are estimated at US$1.3-2.6 billion for new capacity. The long-
term forecasts of demand and supply clearly point to viable opportunities
that are open to developing countries in sugar production and exports.
Nevertheless, the large capital investment needs in sugar and the rapid in-
crease in direct production costs could cause the world-wide expansion of
production to lag behind potential demand.
D.   Price
8.        The world market sugar price declined from about USJ38/lb in
January, 1975 to about USJ13/lb in December, 1975, at which level it stabi-
lized during the first half of 1976. This decline was attributed to increased
production in the EEC and the United States on the one hand, and to a decline
in consumption in some major developed and developing countries, on the other.
With the reduction of world prices to the present level and the economic re-
covery in the OECD countries, consumption is expected to increase back to
about 3-4% annually.
9.        The future price of sugar is projected to stabilize at USJ14.9/lb
in constant 1976 dollars; in current dollars the price is projected to be
about USJ20/lb in 1980 and USi28/lb in 1985 (Table 4). About 70% of the
factory gate price of sugar is factory cost. It is projected that the prices
of most factor inputs will increase at the same rate as general inflation;
and that in the long-run the price of sugar will approach long-run marginal
cost. The long-run price forecast is based on the following assumptions:
(a) Supply, demand and price mutually affect each other, and
supplies are usually more unstable due to their dependence
on weather conditions;
(b) World prices are not directly related to overall levels
of supply or demand, but rather to supply/demand imbalances,
i.e., the changes in sugar stocks;



ANNEX 19
Page 4
(c) Sugar competes with other food crops for land, and other
food prices, therefore, affect the supply and price of sugar;
(d) Certain special market arrangements still exist;
(e) Sugar demand is affected by the prices of other substitute
sweeteners, primarily for industrial usage; their share of the
world market is expected to expand, though modestly in markets
outside the U.S.
10.       The time path for developing countries to expand production capa-
city so that costs of production approach long-run marginal costs is highly
dependent on their ability to secure required capital. These countries
will have to invest quite heavily in the short-run in rehabilitation and
expansion projects to take advantage of the favorable market opportunities
and to reduce the risks of inducing a strong production response by other
producer groups. Given the relative cost advantage that developing countries
have in this type of capacity expansion, the realization of an adequate invest-
ment program in the sector should be given high priority. Furthermore, even
though the capital requirements of adding new capacity become progressively
larger, such expansion is justified as the possibilities of rehabilitating
and expanding existing facilities become exhausted.
December 30, 1976
