FARMER, COMPANY AND GOVERNMENT BUDGETS AND BENEFITS
A. Outgrowers' Sugarcane Budget
1.        Outgrowers would enter into a cane growing contract with the Company
and receive specified services and inputs on credit from the Company (Annex
7). It has been assumed that the growing cycle for sugarcane would consist of
a plant crop (22 months) and two ratoons (20 and 18 months, respectively),
yielding a total of 225 t of sugarcane. The harvesting schedule per hectare
would be: Year 2, 88 t; Years 3 and 4, 37.5 t each; Year 5, 62 t; to be
repeated in 5-year intervals.
2.        The Company would prepare the land which would include ripping,
ploughing, harrowing and furrowing and provide, delivered to the field,
chemically treated seed cane and fertilizers. Approximately 7 t/ha of seed
cane would be required. The fertilizer recommendations include application
of 300 kg/ha of single superphosphate each on the plant crop and the ratoon
crops, and 100 kg/ha, 200 kg/ha and 400 kg/ha of C.A.N. on the plant crop
and the two ratoon crops, respectively. The Company would also harvest
the cane and transport it to the factory. The farm family would only
supply labor, initially for land clearing, and subsequently for planting and
gapping, weeding, fertilizer application, and trash raking after harvest.
3.        The estimated direct cost per hectare is KShs. 1,271 for land
preparation, and KShs. 841 for seed cane. For a five-year crop cycle, the
cost of fertilizer would be KShs. 2,883/ha; harvesting, KShs. 1,926/ha; and
cane transport, KShs. 6,532/ha. In addition, farmers would be allocated
a proportionate share of Company overhead costs hlich total KShs. 11,666/ha
over a five-year period, of which KShs. 1,500/ha would be general overhead,
KSh. 2,066/ha would be related to land surveying and preparation, and
KSh. 8,100/ha would be for harvesting and transport-related operations,
mainly the workshop facilities. Interest charges would amount to KSh. 760/
ha. The total cost to the farmer of producing one hectare of sugarcane
would thus be KShs. 26,114, over a five-year period, with the highest annual
cost being KShs. 7,753 in Year 2, and the lowest KShs. 3,787 each in Years
3 and 4. A detailed sugar cane budget is presented in Table 1.
4.        These costs, expressed in constant 1977 prices, would not be
covered by the resulting revenues at the 1976 cane price of KShs. 105/t;
the estimated deficit wouid be KShs. 2,488/ha over a five-vear period.
To provide sufficient incentive to growers, cane prices would have to be
raised significantly, therefore. Various alternatives and comparisons
with maize, groundnuts and tobacco are shown in Table 2. It is important
to take into account the revenue flow over time since sugarcane yields
three separate returns in five years, while the other crops generate annual
returns. This is done by expressing the five-year revenue streams in terms
of their present value. On this basis, sugarcane does not appear to be



ANNEX 20
Page 2
competitive with maize and groundnuts below a price of KShs. 145/t of cane,
or with tobacco even at much higher cane prices. An increase in the price of
sugarcane is thus justified on grounds of comparative profitability, par-
ticularly as an incentive margin over maize is necessary since maize as an
established and familiar crop has an advantage over the much less familiar
sugarcane.
5.        To arrive at a viable incentive price for cane the break-even
analysis, summarized in Table 3, has been undertaken. For policy purposes,
it is essential to take into account, in addition to the factors of produc-
tion included in Table 1, farm family labor. Family labor has to be priced
at full market wage rates as some such labor presently employed in other
parts of the country would have to be attracted back to the Project area
to meet on-farm labor requirements as well as the requirements of the
Company. The results of the analysis show that with the projected cost
of production the break-even cane yield would be 83t/ha/year with a price
of KShs. 105/t, and range from 48-32t/ha/year with corresponding prices
ranging from KShs. 128-155/t. With the estimated outgrowers yield averaging
45t/ha/year under the Project, the price at which all cash costs are covered
and a fair return to family labor is achieved would be KShs. 132/t.  However,
this break-even price is not a sufficient incentive price as no compensation
would be provided for the inherent risk element as perceived by farmers, and
no returns would be generated to "management," or the farmer as a businessman,
and to land. Allowing a margin of 15-20% for these factors, an incentive
price of KShs. 152-158/t of cane is arrived at. At that price, farmers using
Group lb land for cane growing and getting yieils at 36t/ha/year would also
be able to cover their expenses, earn a market-equivalent labor return and
a premium of 4-8%. The evaluation of the Project is based on the cane price
of KShs. 155/t. Farmers' cash flow, depicted in Table 4, is discussed in
para. 19; an outgrowers farm budget is presented in Table 5.
B. The South Nyanza Sugar Company - Financial Results
With a Factory Capacity of 2,000 TCD
6.        With the initially installed capacity of the factory and no further
expansion, the Company's production of mill-white sugar would increase from
about 25,000 t in Year 4 to a full-production level of about 60,000 t in
Year 9. As the management gains operational experience under local condi-
tions, the extraction of sugar would improve from an estimated ratio of 10
tons of cane per ton of sugar during the first few years of operation to a
ratio of 9.5:1 eventually; the evaluation assumes the former ratio for the
first five operating years, and the latter ratio from the sixth operating
year (i.e. Project Year 9) onwards. With an assumed ex-mill price of sugar
of KShs. 3,050/t, and including molasses sales, the Company's gross revenue
in constant (March) 1977 prices would increase from KShs. 78 million in
Year 4 to KShs. 200 million in Year 9 and subsequent years.



ANNEX 20
Page 3
7.        Variable expenditures are estimated at KShs. 39 million in the
first operating year (i.e. Year 4) and at KShs. 99 million at full pro-
duction in Year 9. The Company's variable margin as a percentage of gross
revenue would fluctuate within the range of 49-51% at full development.
Non-variable expenditures would be KShs. 5 million in Year I and increase
gradually to a maximum of KShs. 43 million from Year 4 onwards. The
Company's operational margin would be negative for Years 1-4; it would
then increase from 12% of gross revenue in Year 5 to a range of 24-29%
in Year 9 and thereafter. Interest payments, net of interest income
from outgrowers, would increase from about KShs. 5 million in Year 1 to
KShs. 41 million in Year 4, and would subsequently decline gradually. Based
on these estimates of revenues and expenditures, the Company would have
annual operating losses up to Year 5 and generate profits thereafter aver-
aging KShs. 45 million per year before taxes from Year 9 onwards. After-
tax annual net income at full development would vary between 11% and 19%
of gross reveue. On a cumulative basis, the Company would become profitable
in Year 11 and have generated a surplus of KShs. 151 million by Year 16
(Table 6).
8.         Balance sheet projections are presented in Table 7.  By the
end of Year 5, the Company would have outstanding long-term debt of
IKShs. 352.2 million, and equity amounting to KShs. 196.5 million, repre-
senting a ratio of debt to equity of 64:36. From Year 6 onwards, the
share of equity capital would gradually increase.
9.        The Company's equity of KShs. 196.5 million would consist of
Government's contribution of KShs. 145 million, lMehta's contribution of
KShs. 10 million, and on-investment of World Bank loan funds of KShs. 41.5
million. In addition to equity, the Company during the initial five-
year period would receive KShs. 390 million in loans and KShs. 261 mil-
lion in revenues from operations. The overall cash inflow during the
first five years, expressed in current prices, would thus total KShs. 847
million. The Company's cash outflow over the same period of KShs. 814 mil-
lion, including KShs. 671 million for capital and operating expenditures
and KShs. 143 million for debt service, would result in a cash surplus of
KShs. 33 million.  There would be a small annual cash flow deficit
(KShs. 0.66 million) in Year 4 which would be financed out of the previously
accumulated cash balance.  The Company would continue the agricultural devel-
opnent program throughout Year 6 and complete it early in Year 7 with the
cash surplus carried over from Year 5 and with additional self-generated
funds, thus producing only relatively small cash surpluses in those years.
After Year 7, annual surpluses would average KShs. 44 million. The rate
of return on Company investments without factory expansion to 3,000 TCD
would be 11%. The Company's cash flow projections reflecting the develop-
ment with a 2,000 TCD factory capacity are presented in constant prices
in Table 8, and in current prices in Table 9; the rate-of-return calculations
are shown in Table 10.
With Factory Capacity Expanded to 3,000 TCD
10.       If agricultural development were continued at an even pace after
Year 5 (see Annex 7), the factory would have to be expanded from 2,000 TCD
to 3,000 TCD in Years 6-8, and total production of sugar would increase to



ANNEX 20
Page 4
about 90,000 t by Year 12. The Company's revenue would increase to
KShs. 283 million at full development. Costs would be identical to those
incurred without expansion during Years 1-5 (para. 7), but would rise to
KShs. 146 million in Year 12 and the following years for variable expenses,
and to KShs. 45 million from year 7 onwards for non-variable expenses. The
Company's variable margin would be around 47-49%, and the operational mar-
gin 29-31% of revenue at full development. The first annual profit would
be generated in year 6, and on a cumulative basis, the Company would become
profitable and pay dividends in Year 10. Annual net income after taxes at
full development would amount to 17% of revenue, and 24% of equity. A
break-even analysis is included in the projected income statements in
Table 11.
11.       The Company's projected balance sheet reflecting the expanded
factory capacity is shown in Table 12. With the funding proposed for the
Project (para. 9) and additional investment in Year 6 (para. 12), the ratio
of long-term debt to equity would change from 58:42 in Year 1 to 64:36 in
Year 5, then gradually move in the opposite direction and reach about 50:50
in Year 9, and 25:75 in Year 15.
12.       The expansion phase during Years 6-11, including factory expansion
and agricultural development, would cost an additional KShs. 93 million,
expressed in terms of Year 5 (1981) prices, of which KShs. 22 million would
be expected to be financed through a short-term loan in Year 6, and KShs. 71
million would be financed through self-generated funds. This arrangement
would result in a reduction in the Company's cash balance for three successive
years, i.e. Years 6-8. From Year 9 onwards, the projections show an average
annual cash flow surplus of KShs. 78 million. The Company's cash flow projec-
tions, reflecting the expansion of the factory to 3,000 TCD, are shown in
Table 13 in constant prices, and in Table 14 in current prices.
13.       With the estimated costs and prices, the Company's internal finan-
cial rate of return on investments over a 25-year period would be 13%. The
rate of return would be 10% and 8% in the event sugar prices declined by
10% and 15%, respectively; and 12% if capital cost increased 10% (Table 15).
C. The Kenya Sugar Authority (KSA)
14.       To assure that sufficient funds are available and the foreign
exchange requirements are met, the reorganized and expanded KSA would initially
continue to be funded through Government appropriations (Ministry of Agricul-
ture), supplemented by funds from the World Bank loan. KSA would study
alternative methods for the financing of its future operations (e.g. direct
appropriations from the national budget, channeling of funds through the
Ministry of Agriculture, etc.) and submit a financing plan to the Bank
by July 1, 1979. KSA's expenditures for the first five-year period (1977-81),
including the Project's overseas scholarship program and specific studies
(Annex 17, para. 26) but excluding any other projects or programs that KSA



ANNEX 20
Page 5
might undertake, are estimated at KSha. 25.3 million; with debt service,
expenditures would total KShs. 29.8 million, expressed in current prices. Of
this amount, KShs. 17.3 million would be financed from the World Bank loan and
KShs. 12.5 million would be Government's responsibility (Table 16).
D. The National Budget
15.       The loans from the World Bank and African Development Bank would
be channeled through Government, while all other external financing would
be directly to the Company. The former would amount to KShs. 253 million
(US$30.5 million) in current prices over the first five-year period of the
Project. In addition, Government would receive KShs. 165 million (US$19.9
million) in Project-related taxes and duties and KShs. 33 million (US$4.0
million) in payments of interest and fees from the Company. Total cash in-
flow during the five-year period would be KShs. 451 million (US$54.4 million).
16.       Against these cash inflows, Government would, during the initial
five-year period, provide to the Company equity in the amount of KShs. 187
million (US$22.5 million) and loans totalling KShs. 131 million (US$15.8
million), and finance directly various Project components costing KShs. 95
million (US$11.5 million); debt service on external loans would be KShs. 67
million (US$8.0 million). Total cash outflow over the five-year period would
thus be KShs. 480 million (US$57.8 million), producing a cumulative net burden
on the Government budget of KShs. 29 million (US$3.5 million) by the end of
Year 5.
17.       From Year 6 onwards and assuming expansion to 90,000 t annual
capacity, Government revenues from the sugar excise tax and, after Year 11,
from the Company income tax would grow steadily. Total cash inflows in con-
stant Year 5 (1981) prices would average KShs. 209 million (US$25 million)
annually during the first full-development crop cycle in Years 12-16; average
annual cash outflows would be KShs. 12 million (US$1 million) for continuing
Project-related expenditures and KShs. 57 million (US$7 million) for debt
service. At full development (i.e. average for Years 12-16 crop cycle),
Government would thus have an average annual direct budgetary surplus of KShs.
140 million (US$17 million), expressed in constant Year 5 (1981) prices, as a
result of the Project. In addition, the Company would generate average annual
profits of KShs. 64 million (US$8 million), most of which would be transferred
to the national budget as Company dividends (Table 17).
E. Foreign Exchange Implications
18.       Over the first five years of the Project, the inflow of foreign
exchange as external loans it is estimated would amount to KShs. 512 million
(US$61.6 million) in current prices; molasses exports would earn another
KShs. 11 million (US$1.3 million). The foreign exchange content of Project
investments and operations would be KShs. 448 million (US$54.2 million), and
debt service on external loans KShs. 177 million (US$21.3 million). Actual



ANNEX 20
Page 6
outflows of foreign exchange would thus exceed actual inflows by KShs. 103
million (US$12.4 million). However, the value of foreign exchange savings
through import substitution of sugar produced under the Project would be KShs.
320 million (US$38.6 million), resulting in a positive net effect on the
country's foreign exchange balance of KShs. 217 million (US$26.2 million) for
the five-year period as a whole. On an annual basis, there would be a net
outflow of foreign exchange only in Year 1 of Project implementation, and a
net inflow (or saving) thereafter.
19.       At full development to 90,000 t annual capacity from Year 12 onwards
(annual average of five-year crop cycle) the actual annual net outflow of
foreign exchange consisting of expenditures related to the replacement of
capital items and purchase of current inputs, debt service on external loans,
less earnings from molasses exports, would be about KShs. 140 million (US$16.9
million), expressed in constant Year 5 (1981) prices. Annual foreign exchange
savings resulting from import substitution of sugar are estimated at KShs. 487
million (US$58.7 million), leaving a net annual foreign exchange savings
effect of KShs. 347 million (US$41.8 million). A foreign exchange budget for
the Project is shown in Table 18; the lending terms of the external loans are
summarized in Table 19. Costs eligible for World Bank financing are detailed
in Tables 20 and 21.
F. Farmer Benefits
20.       At the proposed price of cane of KShs. 155/t, the total outgrowers
production of 690,000 t at full development would have a factory gate value of
KShs. 107.0 million; without expansion, farmers would produce 405,000 t of
cane, valued at KShs. 62.8 million. With a net cash cost of KShs. 116/t,
the average annual cost of production would be KShs. 80.0 million, and returns
to labor, capital and management would amount to KShs. 27.0 million, or KShs.
38.90/t of cane, or KShs. 1,752/ha per year on average; the cost of production
and net returns without expansion would be KShs. 47.0 million and KShs. 15.8
million respectively. The actual net cash flow would be zero in Year 1, and
KShs. 941/ha, KShs. 1,775/ha, KShs. 2,026/ha and KShs. 4,020/ha respectively
for Years 2-5 (Table 4). This cash flow would result from the Company provid-
ing all services and inputs on credit and recovering the loan plus interest in
full at the time of harvest of each crop, while again providing any services
and inputs towards the next crop on credit. With the prices and production
as proposed, farmers would receive no subsidies whatsoever.
21.       Assuming that an average farmer would grow two hectares of sugar-
cane and that the cane would displace existing production including crops and
livestock, of about KShs. 450, then the average nel annual incremental income
would be KShs. 3,055 per farm; it would be negative every fifth year and
average KShs. 3,931 per farm per year in the intervening years (Table 5). For
the average farm family of seven in the Project area, net incremental income
per capita would be KShs. 436 (US$53) per year. The total net value of farm
production would increase from KShs. 1,710 (US$206) to KShs. 4,765 (US$574)



ANNEX 20
Page 7
per farm per year, or by about 180%. By comparison, average annual GDP per
capita was KShs. 1,573 (US$210) in 1975. The Project's farm income effect
would be very satisfactory for farmers who could achieve the projected income
without some family members presently employed off the farm having to return
home; returning laborers, while being able to earn market wages at home for
the hours actually worked on the cane, would have to supplement their income
through work off the farm to reach equivalent earnings on an annual basis as
the total labor requirements would average only 126 mandays per year for a
farm with 2 ha under cane. Supplementary income could be earned through
casual employment with the Company which would require some 2,200 persons as
harvest labor.
February 20, 1977

ANNEX 21
Page 1
KENYA
SOUTH NYANZA SUGAR PROJECT
ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
1.        Project incremental costs and benefits are summarized in Table 1.
These are expressed in constant value terms (base = 1977 prices) and are
based on the cane production schedule as outlined in Annex 7 and the Project
cost estimates in Annex 15.
2.        In the calculation of economic Project costs and benefits foreign
exchange has been shadow-priced at a rate of US$1 = KShs. 11 to reflect
the economic cost of foreign exchange to Kenya. Project costs include the
full cost of agricultural development, the sugar factory, housing and social
amenities, and management and administration of the Company; 75% of the
cost of construction and maintenance of roads and tracks in the Project area;
and one-sixth of the cost of the National Sugar Research Institute, the Kenya
Sugar Authority, and staff training not directly related to the Company.
Twenty-five percent of the costs of roads and tracks are excluded because it
is anticipated that there would be offsetting increases in benefits other than
those related to sugarcane which are not measurable. The adjustment in the
cost of roads is to account for benefits other than the production of sugar
resulting from these investments which have not been quantified. The adjust-
ment in the cost of research, training and the Kenya Sugar Authority is in
recognition of the fact that the sugar industry as a whole, i.e. about five-
sixth outside the South Nyanza Sugar Company in terms of future tonnage of
sugar produced, would benefit from these investments and that such benefits
have not been explicitly included. All costs are net of taxes and duties,
which are estimated at KShs. 69.7 million (US$8.4 million) at constant prices.
The cost of land purchases for the nucleus estate also has been excluded.
3.        Tt has been assumed that Kenya would not achieve self-sufficiency
in sugar production on account of the Project, and sugar has been valued on
the basis of import parity. The import parity price, which is consistent
with the World Bank's commodity price forecast, adjusted for ocean freight
and port handling charges, is KShs. 5,113/t (KShs. 3,894/t with foreign
exchange not shadow-priced); details are shown in Annex 4, Table 9.
Molasses has been priced on the basis of export parity (KShs. 300/t) as
sufficient export demand would exist to absorb incremental production
resulting from the Project.
4.        The Project, when expanded to an annual production of 90,000 t
of sugar, would generate salaried employment for appxoimately 4,000 persons.
All salaries and wages have been costed at market wage rates to account for
the fact that, because of previous outmigration, the laborers remaining in
the area might not be sufficient to fully staff Project operations, and some
laborers would have to return from present employment in other parts of
Kenya. While the Company's employment conditions would be attractive enough
to enable it to recruit all staff required, labor in the cane fields is known
to be hard and not readily accepted by certain segments of the population.
For this reason, and because of other imperfections in the national labor
market such as restricted mobility into rural areas (e.g. South Nyanza) on



ANNEX 21
Page 2
tribal grounds, the pricing of labor employed by the Company at less than
market wage rates would not be appropriate even though unemployment would
continue to be a national problem in Kenya. The Project also would involve
about 7,900 outgrower farm families (4,500 without expansion) who would
spend nearly one million mandays per year in the production of sugarcane.
Farm family labor has been shadow-priced at 55% of the market wage rate as
a portion of such labor (i.e. women, adolescents) is presently underemployed
and relatively immobile.
5.        Since the planting of sugarcane would displace some crops pres-
ently grown in the area, the net value of these crops has been included
as an economic cost. Due to the relatively low land utilization in the
Project area, the actual productive area lost due to the Project is also
low; it has been assumed to be about 40% of the Project cane area. The main
crop being displaced would be maize, followed by millets, groundnuts, sugar-
cane for jaggery production, and a variety of subsistence crops of minor
importance. In the outgrowers area, sugarcane would generally occupy farmers'
most productive land and, upon establishment of the cane, farmers would be
expected to reduce the area presently under subsistence crops but to increase
the total area under cultivation on their farms. The net economic value of
production foregone, estimated at KShs. 6.5 million (US$590,000) at full
development, would thus be relatively insignificant.
6.        The data in Table 1 indicate that, over a 25-year period, the
Project would produce an internal economic rate of return of 25%. At the
official exchange rate, the rate of return would be 23%. Sensitivity analysis
was undertaken to examine the effect of changes in the output and cost assump-
tions. This indicated that if output were reduced by 10% and costs increased
by 10% the rate of return would be 20%. If costs remained unchanged but output
were reduced by 20% the rate of return would be 20%. If the factory capacity
were not expanded from 2,000 TCD to 3,000 TCD and annual production of sugar
amounted to 60,000 t at full development, the rate of return would be 23%
(Table 2).
7.        A major risk facing the Project would be the possibility of
greater frequency and severity of droughts in the Project area than has
been allowed for in the estimation of cane yields. Although the likelihood
of this occurring is remote, partial alleviation of the problem could in-
clude to restrict the Project scope to the initial factory capacity of
2,000 TCD to reduce the transport distance of cane and enable the Company
to pay farmers a risk premium for their cane; retain only the best soils
for the growing of sugarcane as such soils afford more drought resistance
and produce higher yields in normal rainfall years which would act as a
cushion against drought losses; and introduce irrigation in selected areas,
conditional upon the technical and economic feasibility of irrigation.
8.        There is also a risk associated with the Company's proposed
partial reliance on outgrowers' cane supply. This risk, which is a major
source of the problems experienced in the Sugar Belt, would be minimized
under the Project by the proposed organization of the South Nyanza Sugar
Company which would establish a strong linkage between the Company and



ANNEX 21
Page 3
outgrowers. This organization is designed to avoid pitfalls encountered
in the Sugar Belt and to incorporate favorable experience gained at Mumias
(Annex 18). In addition to the organizational measures, the development
of a nucleus estate also would represent an effort to counteract the out-
growers' risk. A further factor in alleviating potential problems with
outgrowers would be the real incentives farmers would be given under the
proposed price structure for the sugar industry; a major source of farmers'
discontent in the Sugar Belt is traceable to insufficient incomes generated
by the sugarcane enterprise.
9.        Another risk would be a possible oversupply in the World market and
a consequent long-term drop in the international price of sugar, coupled with
the ambitious expansion program of the Kenya sugar industry and, hence,
resulting surpluses facing uncertain export prospects. While it is unlikely
that the import parity price would fall below the domestic cost of production
for any significant length of time and the Project would remain economically
sound on import substitution grounds, there is only a very narrow margin
within which sugar produced in South Nyanza would be competitive for export
without subsidies; the comparative advantage of sugar produced at Ramisi,
Mumias or in the Sugar Belt also is not clearly established, although some of
these companies have a locational advantage and, it appears, lower production
costs than South Nyanza. However, Kenya's competiveness in the World market
is adversely affected by the country's overvalued currency. To make exports
profitable for the Company, Kenya would thus have to devalue its currency or
subsidize exports. Insofar as Government has a monopoly in the wholesale
distribution of sugar, operates a national sugar equalization fund, and
determines domestic sugar prices and currency exchange rates, Government would
have the means to alleviate the marketing risk associated with a potential
domestic oversupply. Consequently, this risk is basically financial rather
than economic in nature and affects primarily the sugar companies rather than
the economy as a whole, as its relative impact would depend on Government's
ability to appropriately apply the policy means at its disposal. The appro-
priate operation of the national sugar equalization fund would cushion the
impact on individual companies of exports priced below local levels by spread-
ing the financial burden over the whole industry and, if Government chose to
do so, the consuming public. Through consultation in pricing matters, the
Bank would be able to advise Government on, inter alia, appropriate pricing
policies and the operation of the equalization fund (Annex 4). In the very
unlikely event that the entire output of the Project would have to be exported,
the Project's economic rate of return would still be 19% (16% without expansion
to 90,000 t annual capacity).
January 20, 1977
